The Red Page
The New Theatre
Huntly Carter made a pilgrimage through
Europe, seeking The New Spirit in Drama
and Art (Palmer), and brought back im-
pressions of post-impressionism in art, much
information of new devices fo*r the improve-
ment of the theatre, and a sense of the
tremendous importance of rhythm in the
modern drama. Art and the drama in Europe,
we learn in our placid Australian biliabong,
is in a state of portentous ferment; especi-
ally do we note that in the simple matter of
the mechanical adjuncts of staging and light-
ing we and our theatrical managers in Aus-
tralia are in the theatrical Stone Age.
Recognising that the theatre and drama
of England are in a transition state, Huntly
Carter examined the ideas now being experi-
mented upon in the chief art centres of
Europe, especially the attempt to evolve a
new type of theatre, a new stage and a- new
setting. His own dramatic ideal, it is as
well to note, as it tinged all that he saw, is
a national drama comprising :
A rhythmic conception of play, player,
decoration and music. This drama will' be
represented in a rhythmic form of theatre.
Everything henceforth is to be orchestrated
to produce a single but infinitely varied
total effect. We need a stage which lends
itself to the simple and single vision, that
brings even the most unintelligent spectator
into the action of the drama and holds him
there, that promotes a direction of effort on
the part of all concerned which will unify
the results.
But he found that Europe was searching
for artistic rather than rhythmic results.
“The pioneers of the new movement are
chiefly concerned with attaining artistic sim-
plification, unity and suggestion.”
As for the modern realistic drama, Huntly
Carter decides that, biologically consid-
ered, it is a complete failure. He points out
that the disillusionists did not go far enough.
They attempted to represent degeneracy— :
marked deviation from an original type, as
seen in an unhealthy offspring—by actors
physically and beautifully normal. The
stigmata of degeneracy were missing, though
the mental imperfection was ably repre-
sented.
They wrote up the diseased fragments of
social iife, and they engaged normal types
to represent them, either because they had
not the biological knowledge requisite to
complete their undertaking, or because they
had not the courage to scour our infirmaries,
hospitals and lunatic asylums for the real
thing. .. .Their stage families have not even
a nose in common. . . .So to-day we are faced
with a disenfranchised drama confronting a
theatre whereon is written: “This house
is a house of art; but ye have it a den
of disease.” Ibsen, for example, has never
recovered from Mr. Bernard Shaw’s victim-
isation of him. Together with Shakespeare,
he was butchered to make a Fabian holiday.
The drama that the theatre needs, accord-
ing to Huntly Carter, is “a symbolic, social
and cosmic drama, not a drama of the debat-
ing-hall and the dissecting-room.” So off he
started to track it down iii Europe.
In Paris he found Bergson, classic revi-
vals and the artistic movement in the the-
atre. There he found The Blue Bird pro-
duced with infinitely more artistic effects
than it was possible to give in London —and
presumably in Australia. Owing to lack of
modern staging methods, the Parisian produc-
tion was inferior to its first production at
the Artistic Theatre in Moscow. As the
third version pleased Australian audiences,
what must the Moscow' presentation have
been like?
In Germany. Reinhardt, the producer in
London of ' “The Miracle,” rules. And
everywhere on the Continent the question
how the drama shall be housed and.staged in
the most efficient and artistic -manner is fore-
most. Reinhardt gets his effects by the em-
ployment of four modern innovations: the
revolving stage, the round or concave horizon
formed by a white concrete wall enveloping
the stage, the new system of horizon and
centre-stage lighting, and the use of a stage
entirely cleared of conventional canvas
scenery. The first offers a solution to the
problem of quick change's, expeditious hand-
ling of scenes, and act-division. The second
adds appropriate perspective. The third
brings the back of the stage into line with
the front, and reduces the old. harsh effects
of fqotliglits and sidelights. None of these
modern innovations are in use yet in Aus-
tralia. Instead we have what looks almost
like the proud boast of Oscar Asche—or
Oscar Asche’s press agent—that in his pro-
duction of A Midsummer Night's Dream in
Melbourne a stage staff of 70 men is re-
quired—7o men, when the work could be
done by a motor and a few switchmen.
Without elaborate waits, Reinhardt staged
the second part of Faust— and the produc-
tion of this extraordinary piece of work has
been the despair of German producers ever
since Goethe completed it. For, as Huntly
Carter nicely remarks, Goethe put every-
thing into Faust 11. except drama. Rein-
hardt possesses two theatres in Berlin, and
is aided in his efforts by leading poets,
musicians, authors and stage managers.
To-day we can represent actions which a
Shakespeare, or Sophocles, or Goethe could
never attempt to express. These are tre-
mendously important actions, carried beyond
spoken language by the language of move-
ment, and the visualised scene. .. .Thus,
through its co-operation with enlightening
music and eloquent decoration, dramatic
action must attain a height undreamt of by
the ancients, as well as by those moderns
who are engaged in the fruitless task of
adapting old forms of drama to the new
forms of representation.
The Ilebbel Theatre is mentioned as an
Edition de luxe —the last word, for the mom-
ent, in theatre building. And all through
Germany there are others. In several the
Fortuny system of lighting is employed
In consists, technically, of two parts—a
dome and a system of lighting, which can,
however, be used without the dome. The
object of the system is to obtain the effects
of natural sunlight either in direct rays or
diffused. These effects are obtained by the
use of an absolutely white light thrown on
to bands of colored _silk that act as reflectors,
and so diffuse the light over the whole scene.
It has many advantages over the present
system. It does aw'ay with the present un-
satisfactory method of obtaining a sky pers-
pective. by throwing colored limelight directly
on a white-washed wall or revolting back-
ground, and offers instead a method that
produces a wonderful illusion of a far-reach-
ing perspective. It eliminates the patchy
effects of the use of battens, floats and perch-
lights all over the top and sides of the stage,
and produces instead a uniform-tinted atmo-
sphere enveloping everything. Again, it dis-
penses with the use of a great deal of stage
lumber, wings, flies, etc.
The beautiful Kunstler Theatre in Munich
aims at artistic simplicity. There are no
wings, the spectators being so placed that
they cannot see behind the scenes. The
scenery is painted in monochrome and built
solid, so that the shadows from overhead
will be as natural as possible. But the
scenes, the investigator found, are too tightly
held by the decorative stationary frame.
The decorative movement in Vienna has
given birth to the Viennese Theatrical Cos-
tume and Scenic Studio. Its efforts to repro-
duce beauty on the stage are largely on the
lines of the Kunstler Theatre, Munich, wdiich
is actively engaged in putting frames on
stage pictures-
It is largely concerned with the attempt
to obtain the effects of direct light and cast
shadows, and is making interesting experi-
ments in painting and lighting scenery.
Jt
In Buda-Pesth another definite step has
beeh taken toward the artistic evolution of
the theatre. There an inventor discovered
that it is impossible to set a good landscape
on the existing stage, that stage pictures are
bound to be mannered artd unnatural, and
crowd effects inartistic, that the setting of
the scenes occupies much time, and that
owing to the narrowness of the exits and
entrances it is not possible to. bring on a
large crowd with any degree of naturalness.
So hh invented a new' stage. He got rid of
the primitive hanging backcloth by construct-
ing an immovable wall, sufficiently high and
wide to limit the sight-line of the spectator.
It could be prepared to receive any lighting
effect; he white-washed it and ingeniously
pierced it with invisible holes for star-
light effects. Then he divided his stage into
two parts, the back part close to his wall-
screen he used simply fok big panoramic
effects or for “crowds.” He constructed this
portion in three separate movable sections, by
means of which large things like ships could
approach or recede in a natural manner.
Next, in order to dispense with intervals
while scenery was changed, he constructed
his front stage in three parts, a centre and
tw'o side platforms moving on wheels. By
this means one scene can be struck and set
while another scene is being played. And
this innovation logically led to the dispensing
of side wings altogether. Then he was com-
pelled to get all his lighting from the top :
The mechanism for controlling and work-
ing this revolutionary stage is very simple
and economical, and the stage manager can
. obtain all the results lie desires by merely
moving a lever here and there. . . .It shortens
the waits. It dispenses with the use of the
conventional proscenium arch. It gives a
natural perspective to the scene. It allows
three scenes to be set at once. It promotes
economy in the use of scenery, and it gives
the scene-shifter a holiday. '
Another aspect of theatrical progress is
the remarkable growth of the open-air the-
atre in Europe. Each summer more new 7
open-air theatres are opened, and their popu-
larity is enormous. The classical, the reli-
gious, and even the contemporary drama is
played. But in a performance under these
conditions much has to be sacrificed. After
all, the conventions are necessary, and
Nature has no conventions. 1
J*
Moscow has the best organised and
equipped experimental theatre in the world ;
and, one wonders, why Moscow? The answer
is that the Moscow theatre is due to a band
of enthusiatic artistic Moscovians :
They resolved to get rid of trie ■ old-
fashioned theatre, its out-of-date plays, scen-
ery, actors and laws, and to set out in search of
new plays, new scenery, new acting and new
laws. They have found some of each.. . .Its
new naturalistic methods of acting came as
a revelation to a public long accustomed to
the old conventions of the declamatory
school. Indeed, the extraordinary attention
paid to the technique of acting, and the
over-emphasis on detail and effect, came to
most playgoers in the nature of a “new
thrill.”
It was at this dangerous stage that a new
aim came into the Moscow theatre. Its
name w r as “Stylization,” and its ideal, “To
give everything in the scene the harmonious
style of a conventional type and the immo-
bility of the ancient Greeks.” Words, ges-
tures, movements, decorations, costumes, all
were to be set vibrating in unison. But there
was one drawback to this vaguely expressed
ideal. There w'ere no plays that fully met
the demands of immobility or stylisation.
Enter Tchekoff, carrying the article in
demand: —
His peculiar genius for the stage and the
extraordinary technique of his plays, so ad-
mirably adapted to the natural methods of
the players, brought the first great success
to the Moscow theatre, upon which it has
never looked back. Tchekoff practically created
an immobile form of drama, and with his
arrival at the Moscow Theatre came a new
era of stage-production.
The theatre is admirably equipped. It
has a Very large stage provided with a revolv-
ing centre, the round or concave horizon, the
new horizon lighting. Perhaps here, says
Huntly Carter, the problems of stage-light-
ing, seen in the attempt to obtain the effect of
direct sunlight or diffused light, a truthful
distribution of light and shadow', are nearest
solution. And mentally it is still better
equipped. It took the theatre two years to
produce The Blue Bird, and, to produce
Hamlet, three years. The whole time was
occupied in experimentation. To the actors
the theatre is a university and a home. And
the theatre is now a paying concern. As
the management proposes to build a new
theatre costing £lOO,OOO, there is evidently
money in artistic ideals—in Moscow.
**
The New Spirit in Drama and Art is fully
illustrated wfith photographs and plans of
theatres, with daring dress designs, with
technical illustrations of staging and with a
gallery of Poet-Impressionist pictures, many
of them reproduced in color. For the reader
interested in art or the drama the book,
despite its rather rhapsodic style, will prove
of remarkable interest. Chiefly, however, is
it important in bringing home to us in w'hat
a stagnant biliabong Australia, in respect to
culture, remains. Many of these tendencies
rampant on the Continent of Europe will
doubtless tire themselves out; but the spirit
that is stirring the waters there has not yet
visited Australia. The book is especially com-
mended to theatrical architects and theatrical
managers.

An Empire prize novel competition is an-
nounced by Ilodder and Stoughton. A
sum of £lOOO will be divided into four prizes
of £250 each for the best novel submitted
from a resident or native of, respectively,
Canada, Australasia, India, and British
Africa, the latter title to include any colony
or dependency not included under the other
heads. Competitors for the £250 offered for
the best Australasian novel must be
Australasians by birth, or by . residence
of not less than one year, though not
necessarily resident now. MSS. must
be marked “Competition,” and be re-
ceived by Hodder and Stoughton. St.
Paul’s House, Warwick Square,,London, E. 0,.
England, by March 31, 1914, must not be
more than 150,000 words in length—SO,ooo-
- is recommended, and must be accom-
panied by a form to be obtained from Hodder
and Stoughton on request. The decision
will lie announced in TnE Bookman. The
final adjudicator for Australasia will be
Charles Garvice. One wonders why Gar-
vice? ITe is the author who has a “girl” or
“Cupid” or “love” in every title of his manv
novels, and turns out his two hookloads of
sentimental slush per annum with an ease
that does not inspire us with much respect,
for his critical or judicial abilities. Canada
has as her adjudicator Gilbert Parker ; India.
A. E. W. Mason; and Africa, etc.. Rider
Haggard—=all authors of repute, and all with
some connection with the countries assigned
to them. There is no evidence that Garvice
ever was in Australasia. In a recent
“colonial” special number of The Book-
man, his name is not mentioned. The selec-
tion does not inspire respect, and doubtless
will seriously limit the Australasian com-
petitors to the writers, if we unhappily
possess any, of Garvicean sentimentality.
A new series of Art Treasures of Great
Britain, in shilling parts, is being issued bv
Dent. The aim of the publishers is to make
familiar the art. including not only pictures,
but sculpture, drawing, pottery, metal work,
ivories, and Oriental Art, in the public
museums and private collections of Great
Britain. One feature that will be appreci-
ated is the inclusion of modern work. No
too-familiar masterpieces will be reproduced.
The first volume, with reproductions in
photogravure, color and half-tone, comprises
work of Jan de Mabuse, Rembrandt, Gains-
borough, Whistler —not in color—Nardon
Penieaud (an enamel triptych), Greek sculp-
ture and an early French ivory. The mere
Philistine wonders w T hy in the early repre-
sentations of the Madonna and Child the
mother is always fully clothed and the
baby naked. Even in an Australian summer
it is considered necessary for a newly born
child to be clothed. One wonders if the old
masters ever had any families.
“The Orange Tree Series of Children’s Books”
(Constable) includes two stories republished from
Nathaniel Hawthorne’s Wonder Book for Boys and
Girls (1851) and three children's stories by Dickens.
The latter are admirably and humorously illlustrated
in color and line.
The Commercial Artist
Spake W alter Jardine, aged 28, back f rnm
a bnet study in commercial art in the r, ; ,
States and London:— e Lmte «i
I was in America for three months and in
London tor two; but I had to cut mv f
snort owing to my inability to stand th
extremes ot climate in America The \
Students’ League of New York is the finest
school 1 know' of. There everything is tauetir
and practically everv well-known illustm„(
and commercial artist in America has nawH
through this well-equipped school. Person
ally 1 found invaluable the classes on lil us '
tration and Composition, Costume Sketching
and Anatomy. g
Lor the Australian art student who wants
to be an illustrator or commercial artiste-
poster and catalogue work —I should recom-
mend three years’ study in America For
illustration work the Art Students’ League
New York; for commercial art, Chicago’
This city specialises: there is, for instance’
a special firm for tailoring artists, liut a
good way would be for the Australian stu-
dent to start in San Francisco and work
east. He could begin by trying for a job in
an advertising firm or a firm of artists, start-
ing as an “improver” ; and with the experi-
ence he would get, it should be easy fdr him
to Work his way up. An improver is a copy-
ist of other artists’ wmrk for his firm.
As for his chances, a young commercial
artist in good repute in Australia would be
a second or thud-rater on Ins arrival in
America ; but after tw r o or three years’ work
he could easily hold his own. V was told
that in my line, as a suiting artist, I was the
equal to any of the Americans except Leyen-
deeker, who is the only top-notcher. 1 did
very well indeed at piece work, the pay of
which is three times as great as that in Aus-
tralia. But it is as well to admit that, just
as in England, in the U.S. it is necessary to
have some influence to get' in. I knew a
chap on the New York World, and through
him 1 soon got plenty of Work for advertising
tirms. But the climate !
The scope for advertising work is enor-
mous. What is regarded as a small order
for a catalogue is 500,000 copies; and the
house of Kuppenheimer orders millions of
catalogues at a time—and they are works of
art.
If an Australian commercial artist can
stand the climate he would be a fool, speak-
ing from a purely monetary view-point, to
come back to Australia. Unless, of course,
he wants the honor an<J glory of pioneering
commercial work here. 1 Why c-an’t the Aus-
tralian commercial artist make good here?
First, the etching is against the
machines, but the etcher. The Australian
etcher is usually a good workman, but the
American etcher is invariably an artist.
Next, the printing here is not up to the
standard of America. And, finally, the Aus-
tralian commercial artist has the Australian
business man to contend against. This in-
dividual has not been educated up to the
advertising value of good work, and simply
won’t pay * for it. There are, however, a
few firms who do know' that the extra £lOO
they pay is going to bring them in an extra
£lOOO. And that extra £lOO. distributed
between the artist, the etcher and the printer,
will give him a job that w T ill have almost
the appearance of the best and most fascin-
ating American wmrk.
That Australians can hold their own was
quite evident to me. I met several who were
"making good.” Busson, on the N. Y. Times,
draw's a salary of £l2 for four days’ work a
week, and is free to do any outside work he
likes. Alek Sass, on N. Y. World, is doing
very well; but, then, he possesses a distinc-
tive style, and anybody possessed of a dis-
tinctive style and good draughtsmanship can
do remarkably well in the U.S. In com-
mercial art, Harry Weston would swiftly
make his name famous, while for illustration
work Norman Lindsay has only to arrive to
be engaged at top prices. There are only
tw'o possible competitors to him in America
—Orson Lowell, of N. Y. Life, and the
climate.
l r es. it’s a great training ground for any
student: the rew’ards are sure, and our men
can easily hold their own; but. personally,
the climate over there is.. . .well, that’s just
w'liat it is.

VARIUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Youth’s Song.
Let’s live with our might
_ While ’tis day, for the night
is coming apace to make mock of our
pleasure!
Let’s dance, then, and sing,
. For each lad is a king,
And each lass is a queen while she joins in
the measure.
Let’s love while we may,
For a year or a day,
lilt the world and the roof of the world ring
with laughter!
For Death hath a sting,
And Time’s swift a-w’ing,
And there’s darkness and silence in plenty
hereafter!
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
Helene: A Lover’s Curse.
Helene hath murdered love, and therefore
she
Shall find on earth nor love nor charity ;
When love she craves, then fast and ever
faster
Upon her soul irreparable disaster
Shall fall, and sick with misery she shall
learn
How a heart breaks, how cruel and how
stern,
And oh, how lonelv is a world unfilled
By Love, now lying dead beyond return,
The love that she has killed.
Helene hath slain sweet Cupid, and for this
Shall Cupid’s mother cast her out from bliss
Into the darkness, full of vows unspoken,
Hopes unfulfilled, and all things bruised and
broken ;
The void wherein are death and mould and
rust,
The grave to which all sterile blooms are
thrust,
Where souls long damned lie laughing, ev'n
as I,
To see love trampled lifeless in the dust,
The love she made to die.
Helene kissed once, and would not kiss
anew,
So shall she go unkissed her whole life
through
By any true-love kiss, but she shall languish
With loveless kisses that are only anguish ;
In every lip that seeks her lip at night
Venom shall lurk, and little adders bite ;
But true love’s nectar shall be in them
never,
Because, although kissed once to mad de-
light,
She w r ould not kiss for ever.
Helene, your eyes are fountains whence
shall flow
The salty tears of wretchedness and woe ;
Dim they shall be with all the aching sorrow
Of sad to-day and dread of dark to-morrow.
When on the sunless path of years to come
You see no golden ray, and all is dumb
Save that owls hoot, ghosts shriek and pale
mouths whine;
Then, then you shall recall how cold and
lxumb
You left this love of mine.
Helene, I see a bruise on your white breast.
A broken heart that suffers without rest.
I see upon your longing and your hope
Fate pounce, a hangman with a knotted
rope;
Yea, things more horrible and grim and
hard
Wait on your footsteps, and you cannot
guard
Your soul from them by any feeble crying
Because so ruthlessly you struck and marred
Sweet Love when he came sighing.
Helene, the curse is on you even now.
Avenging Venus leans with heavy brow
Over your sun-bright head : therefore beware
The poisoned sting in every kiss you share :
Make ready for sharp sorrow: brace-your
heart
Against the pain of breaking: for the smart
Of burning tears prepare your frightened
eyes;
Since cruelly you left to die apart
The love that slaughtered lies.
A lover, who for one brief hour was given
A glimpse of Paradise, a hope of Heaven
By his dear mistress; but was soon dis-
dained
Since in her heart an icy prudence reigned—
Lo, where in cool, sweet loveliness you lie.
Mocking I say farewell: and laughter high,
And wrath, and scornful hate are in my
verse:
And though such laughter into tears be nigh.
Laughing I speak this curse.
N.S.W.
ADAM M‘CAY.
Clandestine Love.
I slink along the shaded lanes,
I shun the lighted street,
I go to most excessive pains,
To try and be discreet.
I do not walk with Alice fair
In open ways by day.
Because some people strolling there
Might have a lot to say.
I shirk the radiance of the sun,
The open pathways green.
In such bright spots by anyone
We never have been seen.
I cannot walk with my twin soul
As other lovers do;
Upon the beach we never stroll.
In daylight never woo.
I do not write to her address.
She does not write to mine !
Of correspondence I confess
No one has seen a sign.
We speak of Love in whispers low.
I’m married and discreet.
And She is married, you must know,
This lady that I meet.
vc tor 'A.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/' is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse. Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
Don. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn~will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are thosp
applying to MS.

THURSDAY. MARCH 27, 1913.
THE NORTHERN TERRITORY AND
THE CAMEL CORPS.
J. C. Watson, ex-Prime Minister, has
spoken a word which, though it may or
may not express the opinions of those in
the Federal Ministry, is full of danger.
According to the former leader and present
guide of the Labor party, there is no need
for a transcontinental north-and-south rail-
way for some time, the Territory being best
developed from its own ports. If there must
be railways, let them, he says, be constructed
within the Territory itself; but the expendi-
ture on a great railway from North to South
is something against which the late Labor
leader feels called to protest—and, signifi-
cantly, lie made his protest at a function
given by the Commonwealth Government,
whose representative present —the Minister
in charge of Territory affairs —had no subse-
quent word of reproach to offer. Moreover,
Watson was careful to preface his remarks
with a statement that men in active poli-
tics might not care to say such things on the
eve of a general election, thus leaving it to
be clearly inferred both that he knew his
opinions to be secretly shared by Federal
Ministers, and that such opinions were un-
popular among Australians to the extent of
alienating votes. It was a damning admis-
sion. and one which goes far to rob Watson
of whatever title he formerly possessed to
be classed among broad and clear-sighted
Australian statesmen.
* * » #
The development of the Northern Terri-
tory from its own ports has been going on
for half a century, the opportunities for
settlement have always been great, the ad-
ministration by South Australia did nothing
to retard, even if it did little to advance, the
progress of the country, and yet the white
population, exclusive of the large number
of officials lately dumped into Darwin, is
only about one to 400 square miles. Wat-
son admits that the chief port lies right in
the track of navigation, and it may fairly
be said that the Territory has always hail
as much in the way of shipping facilities as,
in its state of development, it could expect;
yet the plea is seriously put forward that the
means which have hitherto failed to settle
the country will now succeed. When an ex-
periment has had a trial of 50 years or
more, and does not make good, it would seem
reasonable to cast about for other methods;
and the strongly-expressed ooinion of every
recent visitor to the North, who has gone
there without prejudice, is that the trans-
continental railway is the method that
holds out the most promise. To say that
such a railway will carry little produce in
its earlier years is to say nothing. The rail-
way is required to develop the trade which
it will afterwards carry. By sea, Darwin
is at present 15 days distant from Mel-
bourne. The completion of the overland
railway from Oodnadatta to Pine Creek can
reduce this easily to 80 hours. Practically,
Darwin is now as remote from our centres
' of population as Ceylon or the islands of the
Central Pacific. Direct railway communi-
cation can bring the return journey within
the scope of a single week. In that differ-
ence lies the exact and specific reason why
the Territory is still without population.
Australian settlers have a perfectly reason-
able and natural objection to isolating them-
selves in a place that, through lack of rail-
way facilities, is so distant from centres of
Australian thought and feeling that it is, to
all intents and purposes, a foreign country.
Developed from its natural ports alone, the
Territory might, in two generations, have
the population of a Federal constituency;
linked by railway with the present settled
areas, it might, in a single generation, be
the richest and most prosperous seventh of
the Commonwealth. The transcontinental
railways of the American continent were
not required, in the first place, to carry the
goods and produce of the Pacific seaboard
to Boston and New York; but their con-
struction induced settlement, created trade
and gave a fillip to the western portion of
the country which could have come in no
other way. In the same manner, no one
contends that the transcontinental railway
is intended, primarily, to carry to Adelaide
the gold, pearlshell and bSche-de-mer obtained
in the North, or to bring cattle or wool to
a southern market; but it is contended, and
rightly, that the railway will cause settle-
ment, which will make trade, which will,
eventually, pay many times over the cost of
construction.
Were the object of the Commonwealth
solely and only the settlement of the North-
ern Territory, Watson’s remarks about de-
velopment from its own ports would show
an utter lack of statesmanlike nower to
grasp the situation ; but the Territory means
more to the Federation than that. Aus-
tralia’s tremendous danger lies in its empty
lands which face the great ports of Asia,
and are, in sailing distance, actually nearer
to them than to the cities of Australia. If
the black-and-tan races gain a foothold on
this continent, it will be through the front
garden of the North ; and once any consid-
erable number of Asiatics is lodged in any
part of the continent, our dream of a White
Australia is gone, .and the pollution, spread-
ing southward, will paint itself in tragic
human colors across every State. If our
lands remain unsettled, alien incursions
that are without any sort of international
significance may be as destructive as a regu-
lar invasion, and for either invasion or pri-
vate incursion, the coast of Northern Aus-
tralia must be watched and guarded. The
settlers of the Northern Territory must be
the frontier garrison of our race, and for
purposes of defence a transcontinental rail-
way must be constructed that will enable the
more populous States to support that garri-
son, and put up the largest possible sort of
scrap against any invader. The railway to
Darwin is thus, first of all, needed for de-
fence purposes, and so urgent is the matter
of its construction that even now it may be
too late. A distance of 3077 miles separ-
ates Oodnadatta and Pine Creek, and pushed
ahead at the rate of a mile per day it would
be three years before the rails were linked
up. Allowing for a widening of existing
gauges, and the delays which always have
to be taken account of, it would be much
more likely to be seven years before troops
could be conveyed in smartest time to the
point of danger. And seven years is a very
long time when you already hear the enemy
sharpening his gun and whetting his powder.
When the enemy batters at the Darwin
gate, and our citizen soldiers rush to shake
off his death-grip, there are two ways in
which they may cross the continent. One is
by railway, the other by camel. All Aus-
tralia is to-day divided into two camps on
the great matter of defence. There are the
railway men and the camel men—the men
who believe that preparation by modern
methods is the best safeguard of our shores,
and the men who are ready to trust to luck
and send a few small boys on camel-back to
look for the enemy when he has already made
good his landing. It is pitiful to see J. C.
Watson throw to the winds his larger Aus-
tralian views and join the camel corps. It
is quite true that it is of ships, and not
camels, that he talks, but it comes to just
the same thing; for if the Northern Terri-
tory is to depend on ships for its develop-
ment, Australia must depend upon the evil-
smelling beast for its defence. But worse
than Watson’s desertion of the cause of
Australian defence is the hint conveyed in
hi" words that he is the free spokesman of
the Federal Ministers whose tongues are
tied because an election is near. Is this to
be interpreted that the transcontinental rail-
way is no more than an election trick, and
that the men in power are prepared to play
fast and loose with a matter of such vital
national concern? It must be bitterly con-
fessed that every action of Fisher’s Minis-
try in its dealing with Northern Territory
matters gives form and color to the hint
conveyed in the ex-Prime Minister’s speech.
If it is really the case that the transconti-
nental railway is to be indefinitely hung up
at the bidding of alleged statesmen who are
too blind to see its urgency and importance,
it F time that the people whose, votes rule
the coming election said something on the
matter. What The Bulletin wants, what
every thoughtful citizen of the Commonwealth
wants, is a direct statement from Fisher,
given without hedging or shuffle, as to
whether he also has thrown over the cause
of Australia and joined the camel corps.
And lest his answer, if satisfactory, should
be regarded as merely having relation to the
elections, he might also mention the exact
date at which he will have a thousand men
working on the line.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Sydney Ferry Hold-up.
Whatever else comes out of the strike of
Sydney ferry-hands which cut the city in
halves last week, this much is certain —the
traffic to Milson’s Point must be taken out
of the hands of the company that now con-
trols it. It is quite possible, no doubt, that
if the State ran the business a strike might
still happen ; but if a strike did happen the
public would at least be able to deal with
the responsible parties. If the Government
was to blame, the public could punish that
Government and eject it. In any case, it
would have a voice in the selection of the
responsible Government. That Government
would represent the people, so far as our
system allows, and that fact alone would be
calculated to prevent any such extreme
action as the ferry hands took last week.
Though N. S. Wales certainly has had one
short-lived tramway strike, the State rail-
ways have never been hung up in all their
long history. The public has, on the other
hand, no say whatever in the choice of the
directors of the Ferry companies, and no
control whatever over them. These men
and their managers v be the most grasp-
ing and unreasonable and irritating men to
be found in the country. The shareholders
who choose them look to them to make divi-
dends ; that is their principal job. The
chances are, therefore, that they wilt be
money-grubbing, endangering the public
convenience and safety for the sake of sav-
ing sixDence here and threepence there. And
if convenience and safety are sacrificed, the
public has no redress against them. It
cannot eject them from their seats on the
Board; on the contrary, since the biggest
parcels of shares are most likely held by the
Fattest men in the community, it is highly
probable that the more the public cries out
against these directors the better the Fattest
shareholders will think of them. This is all
utterly intolerable. The State has built
lines of railway and tramway converging at
Circular Quay on the one side of the Har-
bor and at Milson’s Point on the other, and
it has left the connection in the hands of
men who may actually get kudos for har-
assing the public. The ends of the chain
are designed to serve the public; the central
link is designed, not to serve the public, but
to make dividends for a few private people
who may have the most profound contempt
for the opinion of the public. Whatever
else may be done, the connecting link must
be promptly taken out of such hands. It is
not the time to discuss whether the general
ferry service of the Harbor should be in pri-
vate or public control. That is a larger
and more debatable question. There is one
thing obvious—so obvious that it is really
beyond debate—and that thing should be
done on the instant. A less futile man than
J. McGoweN would have done it within 24
hours of the commencement of the trouble.
The Day of the Little Nations.
Through the uprising of the Bulgarian
peasants and the Servian pig-feeders, a new
and difficult element has come into inter-
national politics. The big nations have,
through their enlightenment and civilisa-
tion. which means their love of ease and
distaste for special exertion, lost a lot of
their lighting force. The small senn-bar-
baric nations, on the other hand, appear to
have a tremendous lot of “go” in them. The
fact that these little wasps can sting, and
don’t in the least mind being stung in re-
turn, has made all Europe put a bewildered
hand to its forehead. Had England suffered
losses as great in proportion as those which
have fallen to the share of Bulgaria in the
present war, the whole country would long
ago have been mad with' hysteria. Had
Germany been killed and wounded to the
same extent, the domination of Prussia
would have been in danger, and the Imperial
throne would be shaking like a skyscraper in
an earthquake. Yet the Bulgar person goes
on with the bleeding, and will evidently see
it to the bitter end. It is the' lesson half-
learned in the Boer war. and now driven
forcibly home. Every half-civilised State is
loaded and liable to go off in both barrels, to
the great inconvenience of the diplomatic
person who thought that because it was
small it could be safely handled. From the
Australian point of view, the new develop-
ment is full of hope and full of danger. It
means that, few as we are, if we are in dead
earnest, and get all our shooting machinery
and transit and other arrangements in pro-
per order, most people will be afraid to speak
unpleasantly to us; but it also means that
we have not merely to fear a great Jap in-
vasion, or the mighty hosts of a China
awakened to life and power, but that any
one of the little barbaric nations of Asia
may rise to sudden strength and challenge
our right to hold the part of this continent
that we have not yet learned to use. Big
nations might count the cost; smaller
nations —and they are mighty big nations
when their numerical strength is compared
with our own —might regard success as
cheap at the expense of three-quarters of
their blood and all their treasure.
The AVfrite Man in the Pacific.
Tuus Australia’s Minister for Defence r
F. Pearce, in the Australasian £ ’ G-
and Military Annual (just issued
The Australian ideal is that there shnnla i
the defence of British interests a strong ?> e for
fleet, jointly contributed to by each n 1 acitic
having interests in the Pacific providing 9 r? mini °n
taming a fleet unit which, together with main '
force which the Government of the United 8 ?/ naval
may see fit to maintain there, would form lng ? om
for the defence of British interests in th? 135
in time of danger. rac 'nc
With a bit of luck, this ideal is in n f •
way to realisation. As a matter of fact>
might have been well over the horizon Sow
if Maonland had thought a few years mr
as it is beginning to think now, and if
Canada had decided about the same time t
take its coat off like a man to defend its lif
and honor and prospects, instead of buying
weapons for old father Bull to do the scran
ping for the whole family. Thus, 2?
now. there would have been at least tw
Dreadnoughts in the Pacific for Australian
defence —the Australia and the Maorilandb
for the “gift” warship which lousepii Ward
stampeded his country into persuading 1
Bull to build for himself in his own vard
at his (Bull’s) personal expense could—
and. no doubt, would —have been the first
outcome of a local navy policy: a Dread
nought owned and controlled in Maoriland
and stationed thereabouts, instead of in the
North Sea or some other unhandy place to
bring a warship from in a hurry. " Likewise
Canada, instead of pursuing Bull with a
flash-in-the-pan contribution of three Dread
noughts' (which Bull can have on some-
thing like the same terms as he received
the Maoriland), might have had them con-
structed for itself and planked into the
Pacific with a splash that would have re-
sounded through a century of the Younger
Nations’ history. Had such an unfortunate
difference of opinion not existed amongst
statesmen, tuft-hunters and plain fools, the
White Man's fear of the swarming of Asia
would rest easier on his chest o’ nights.
Japan has, at present, four Dreadnoughts
in Pacific waters, and another building or
about to be built. Hard financial times
will keep Nippon at that battleship strength
until 11)16, wdien four more will be added
to fleet. Experts reckon that in that
year Nippon will be able to land an army in
Australia, if it has a hankering that way;
but a lot may happen in three years. One
thing practically certain is that “the number
of Dreadnoughts will play a most important
part in the argument when it starts, and it
is something to know that it would not be
impossible for the White Man to throw
seven of the ironclad devils into the scales
—three from Canada (provided the proposed
scheme stands), one from Australia, one
from Maoriland (provided it is on the China
Station by 11)16), another “gift” vessel from
the. Federated Malay States, and (if the
decision of the 1909 Imperial Conference at
London holds good), one from the East
Indies squadron. All this is very comfort-
ing, even if the realisation of the project
appears at present vague and dim. But dim
as the signs are, they point to an awaken-
ing amongst the peoples of the Dominions
from the mental sloth into which they have
hitherto wallowed as far as their defence is
concerned. The old idea of paying subsidies
to the Imperial navy, and having all the
eggs in one basket, is being rapidly rooted
out. J. Bull has made it clear that he can
no longer pretend to be a reliable protector
of his unwieldy empire. The job is too
massive for him, and the time is ripe for
some stalwart assistance. The form that
assistance should take was mapped out for
Australia by Bull himself, so that no mis-
take should he made about it, and. Dr.
Arthur notwithstanding, all other schemes
are merely an embarrassment to the old
nian. They are worse ; they are an impudent
intimation that he is an incompetent old
driveller. With the turning of Maoriland
to a policy of some self-reliance, an enor-
mous stride will have been made towards
the ideal. James Allen, M.L.’s Minister
for Defence, w r as enthusiastically sent off a
little while ago to talk over this very ques-
tion with the British authorities, and at the
farewelling proceedings he made very plain
what the basis of that talk would be. This
is what he said :
It seems to me an absolute necessity that if we
in these Southern Seas desire to keep open our
trade routes we should establish a fleet in the
Pacific to supplement the one in the Atlantic. I
think the burden of such a fleet should be borne by
those who live in the Pacific. There is no reason
why Australia, Maoriland, Canada, South Africa
and even India should not take part in the defence
of the Pacific.
As for Canada, the signs there need not be
disheartening, even though they be exasper-
ating. With the Laurier Government in
power, the country was pledged through its
Parliament to a local unit of sea defence;
but Laurier’s defeat brought to the Trea-
sury benches a party that, temporarily, any-
how, favored a cash subsidy and “gift'
Dreadnoughts. Three, Dreadnoughts were
offered, but there was a healthy condition
attached to the offer that if it was desired
by Canada at a future date to take over
control of the ships, no obstacle should be
put in the Dominion’s way. The not re-
mote chances are that the disturbance now
going on between the two parties in Canada s
House of Commons on the subject will be
sooner or later decided by the country, when
the naval policy will be definitely fixed.
And if a spirited, nation-like decision is for
a local fleet, the White Man in the Pacific
will begin to loom across the shadowy
future of things with much more definiteness.
The Sydney Government House Muddle.
The centre of dispute in connection with
Sydney Government House is changed, but
the decision of the Full Court does not seem
to have closed the argument. The State
Governor is equally with the Governor-Gen-
eral the King’s representative. The party
in power, though beaten on the first point
of law submitted, may, therefore, possibly
decide to recall the State Governor from
the slopes above Rose Bay and •e-establish
his throne in Macquarie-street, and then the
row will start afresh ; for the ultra-loyalists,
fierce State spiters as they are in all other
matters, are determined that the boss vice-
regal person of Australia, and nobody else,

Black and White Pictures.
The Government of India, through the Brit-
ish Government has desired the Common-
wealth to warn white widows and spinsters
hereabouts against the foolishness of marrying
adventurous Afghans and Pathans who lure
them with “lying stories of the magnificence
and comforts of their homes, of their status
in their own country, and of the future life
of the wives” they are going to adore. Ihe
Indian Govt, declares that, having captured
a gullible white woman, the black humbug
takes her away across the North-Western
frontier of India and sells her at auction :
The miserable woman is (loomed to a life of close
imprisonment under barbarous conditions, and when
her attractions wane she becomes simply a household
slave. She lives and dies within the walls ot a

whilst the. men smack their thick lips in
contemplation of her, the ignorant women
will hate her for her intrusion. White soci-
ety shuns her ; black society boycotts her.
Her position as a married woman is not
worth a string of beads. The deception
practised on the white woman in such cases
ia ajffiost, if not quite, as cruel as where the
Afghan is concerned; but it is neither In-
dian nor British Government policy to make
the truth public.
Group Settlement in the Northern
Territory.
The descendants of the most ancient vari-
ety of Briton appear likely to make a
descent on the Northern Territory and estab-
lish themselves there in force. The Welsh
colony in Patagonia has been looking for a
change, and Robert Williams, late Cus-
toms expert at Wellington (M.L.), has re-
ported in thrilling terms on the advantages
of settlement in the Territory. The conclu-
sion of his Report is as follows :
I feel that I would be wanting in duty and
patriotism if I did not urge you with all my might
to abandon your inhospitable home under an alien
flag and lose no time in availing yourself of the won-
derful offer now before you. Never in the whole
history of British colonisation have such inducements
been held out to the white settler. Australia needs
to people her vast spaces of the North. Her need is
your opportunity !
Williams has announced his intention of
leading the way by taking up land in Aus-
tralia’s-' front’ garden, and has thrown up
his : Government job to do so, and there is a
very big chance of his countrymen shaking
the dust of S, America from their whiskers
and joining him. This looks like very good
business. In an absolutely new country,
group-settlement has very much to recom-
mend it. A bunch of people bound together
by some ties of friendship or kindred aim
will feel its isolation less keenly, and will
warm up to greater enthusiasm, than those
who go single-handed into the wilderness,
leaving all their friends and associates in
Gippsland, County Clare or the Black
Forest. The most successful colonisation
done in Maoriland was along these “group”
lines. Otago was given to a Presbyterian
cmwd; Canterbury to a bunch of Church
of England enthusiasts ; Taranaki was first
exploited by men of Devon; Moutere, near
Nelson, is occupied by the descendants of
a single German village; the Forty-Mile
Bush and other parts of the Wellington
province are held by the sons and daughters
of hardy Norsemen; in the Far North, in-
dustrious Austrians are covering the barren
hillsides with vines • Akaroa is a French
settlement; and up and down the Shaky
Islands there are the descendants of Shet-
landers, Scottish Highlanders of various
clans, and Irish exiles who fled from the exac-
tions of the rent-lord. All of these came to
the country in friendly moos, whose mem-
bers helped one another through the trial
years of their initiation into a new life, and
all, with the exception of some weird and
detached Highlanders, have in the end iden-
tified themselves completely with the coun-
try of their choice, and become Maoriland-
ers. The Territorv, with its immense un-
peopled areas, is a splendid field for group
settlement, and the Welsh invasion may be
the beginning of an important development.
Meanwhile, there is a word in the report
made by Williams to his Welsh com-
patriots which Federal Ministers might
paste in their hats:
It is to be hoped that the agreement (with regard
to the railway) already entered into with South
Australia will be adhered to, and that no considera-
tion, political or otherwise, will be allowed to de-
flect the direct bisecting line of the continent from
Adelaide to Darwin.
Everything turns upon that. The country
is all that it should be ; it is the statesman’s
faith that is in doubt.

Jimmy McGowen Throws a Boomerang.
McGowen’s refutation of Wade’s state-
ment that house rents had risen because of
the scarcity of labor to build dwellings,
proved • most unpleasantly convincing. As
usual Wade fell over his boots through his
fatal habit of making statements before in-
quiring into facts. McGowen produces the
facts, but in doing so furnishes a damning
indictment of his own administration. On
March 6 Sydney, he says, had 2000 men out
of work, including 100 ironworkers. 150
bricklayers, 50 carpenters and a large but
unspecified Dumber of painters. Already
processions of unemployed have been seen
in the streets, and there is a general feeling
that things are going to be worse before they
are better. The real reason that rents are
high is because owners of houses realise that
as much cash must.be raked in as possible
before the slump arrives. McGowen’s deal-
ings with Cohen have created only a ficti-
tious prosperity, and the person interested
in collecting rents recognises it as such. He
has seen things like it before. Also, the
capitalist who builds, jerries or otherwise
produces terraces and things, is filled with
very grave doubts. If the present boom
could continue, he would have houses and
cottages up even if he had to lay the bricks
himself an'i hold them in position with stick-
ing-plaster until he had them leased ; but he
also sees a winter of discontent following
last upon a summer of borrow and squan-
der, and he holds off to watch events. So,
had McGowen kept his non-borrowing
pledge, there would have been more houses
aV ij » e * n a t lower rents. There
wouldn t have been much unemployment either,
tor had the fiscal-sinking crowd in Federal
politics not been provided with cash
through the fictitious prosperity caused by
the States’ borrowing, they would long since
have been compelled to tackle the work of
raising the tariff walls to a decent height.
MCDowen s explanation is thus a boome-
rang which, after knocking out Wade, has
rebounded and hit James himself very hard
in the stomach, or thereabouts.

“Quiz” tells of another happy arrange-
ment: —
The Master Printers’ Combine of Melbourne
euphemistically styled Typothetae, is going the pace
with a vengeance. When, on the genesis of the
movement, Ihe Bulletin placed it under the
microscope and probed its motives, no little sore-
ness was caused. Indeed, its official organ, The
Pmnteu, was furiously indignant. But somehow
the thing grew, and it has enough vigor now to
cease worrying about a possible post-mortem. It
boasts of embracing to-day a high percentage of
city and suburban houses. Its secretariat in Col-
lins-street is a sumptuously equipped institution
controlled by a staff with gilt-edged salaries. It is
here all the big “nobs” foregather to stack the cards
and give instructions to the hirelings as to how to
play them. The “costing” list prepared by the
combine is the last possibility in the way of ex-
ploitation. There was a growl of dissent from the
smaller craftsmen when the list was issued, but the
big fellows had their way. Next, when the fiat
went forth that there was to be no more competi-
tion, that prices were to be absolutely uniform, and
that when a printer had a customer on his books he
or she was to remain his property for ever and
anon, matters wore a somewhat angry appearance.
But again the big man won. All manner of threats
were made in order to bring in recalcitrant prin-
ters. These included such convincing suggestions
as the financial thumbscrew and svstematic boycot-
ting. The timid ones went in. Their small stock
of courage oozed out like ink from a leaky barrel.
There are still, it is true, a number of self-respect-
ing printers holding out —but at what a cost?
Tyranny has materialised in a hideous form. Paper
warehousemen in Melbourne, as well as indent agents,
have point-blank refused to supply material to these
non-combine printers. And, to make matters more
fiendish, Adelaide and Sydney houses are in the
swim. Surely this is the quintessence of the bar-
baric boycott! What a stunning demonstration it
is of the cruelty that is possible under a Constitu-
tion that is allegedly a warranty of freedom !
»
The Laborite who thinks that the tariff
doesn’t matter, and the other one who fancies
that Customs duties are an invention of the
Devil to fatten the capitalist, ought to have
been at a meeting the other day when the
manager of one of Melbourne’s largest boot-
manufacturing concerns told his remaining
etnployees a few solemn truths about their
business. He reminded them that they were
steadily getting fewer in number, and even
those that remained were mostly on half-
time ; he told them that if they acquainted
themselves with the existing Customs tariff
on boots and shoes, and considered the pub-
lished figures of importations, they would
perceive the reason of this lack of employ-
ment ; and he coldly assured them that,
while it mattered a great deal to them, it
mattered little to him. He merely had to
earn dividends for his shareholders, and he
could, under the present tariff, just as easily
do that by importing as by manufacturing.
He had already, in fact, imported 40.000
pairs of boots this year, and it was likely
that he would import 10 times as many
before the year was out. But if the tariff
were made Protective he could not import at
a profit, and his shareholders would sneedily
tell him to set his machines going again. So
the shareholders are not desperately inter-
ested in making the tariff what it ought to be
—looking at the matter from a selfish point
of view. The people who are interested are
the boot-operatives and their wives and fam-
ilies, since importing brings them no profit.
All they get out of it is the sack.
Sydney very nearly got some fried hospital
patient the other night. The Coast Hospi-
tal, a group of wood and iron buildings, faces
the Pacific on a breezy cliff where the fresh
sea winds can blow the germs skyward. All
around is dense scrub containing plenty of
turpentine, and ready to blaze furiously at
short notice. The nearest fire station is
miles away. During burning-off operations
the other night the flames got beyond con-
trol, and began to roar towards the sick
people’s inflammable shelter. The brisk sea-
wind seconded the motion, and helped it
along so well that the hospital morgue and
the body it contained were surrounded and
singed, and one of the wards missed destruc-
tion by a few yards only. While the staff
was beating the fire out, the patients, lying
helpless inside, became hysterical, and the
nurses had to stand by in case it became
necessary to rush them into the open. Just
when the situation seemed hopeless, the
wind veered from south to west, and started
to drive the attacking flames towards the
sea. The first section of the distant brigade
hadn’t yet arrived, so it was mere chance
that averted the tragedy. If hospitals must
be made of wood and iron, it seems a fair
thmg to clear the surrounding land so that
the risk of a wholesale grill by bush fire will
be eliminated. But, on the face of things,
there doesn’t seem to he any reason why im-
portant and permanent institutions should
be flimsy woodsheds.
“Six-and-Eight’s” little bomb:—
„ A . point ca P«ble of causing; consternation, mental
contusion and kindred emotions, has been raised in
connection with the Sydney Government House case.
~,7®. Co " st ' t !; t , I , on . Act of 1855 vested only the waste
lands of N.S.W. in the Legislature, and as the Gov-
ernment House grounds weren’t waste in any except
an economic sense, the contention is upheld that they
are still in the direct charge of the Imperial Gov-
ernment, and quite beyond the control of local
authority. But a lot of land in the State was far
from being waste in 1855. Already, the bvst por-
tions ot it had been alienated, and the settlement
had spread far out into the wilderness and stuck
the survey pegs of Private Enterprise all over the
landscape from Watson’s Bay to the sunset. A lot of the
argument which applies to the land on the shores of
harm Cove must therefore have a wider application,
tor it the Imperial authorities handed over onlv
waste lands everything that was occupied or pur-
chased by Private Enterprise before 1855 must be
under Imperial control also. Which means or seems
t ( > mean that the State has no right to interfere
with a single clod of N.S.W. earth which had become
private property or had been set apart for any pub-
lic purpose before 1855. It is a cheerful ideas and
one with at least enough sporting chance of being
good law to make certain fat persons leap high in
the air, wave their abdominal extensions round
their heads and sing “Rule Britannia” with a loyal
vehemence that may disturb the chained kangaroo
m front of the G.P.O. If Private Enterprise could
only shake itself free from State law in its applica-
tion to all lands alienated before 1855, and every-
thing done on those lands, what a time Svdney would
have! It is a great idea, and if taken up with
solid enthusiasm the cry of Imperial Rights might
drown even the slogan of State Frights in its volume
and intensity.
One Mr. Kwei Chih, of the Maoriland
Chinese Consulate, lately spoke reprovingly
about the White Australia policy:—
Yours is a big, fertile country, and so sparsely
populated. How are you going to get it populated
if you insist upon these restrictions? Consider the
state of the Australian ‘aborigine. He has been ren-
dered almost extinct by a philosophical idea of iso-
lation. There has been no intercourse with other
nations, and he is dying for want of it. Australia
must not restrict itself if it wants to become a great
nation....My own country was degenerating until
we opened the doors about 50 years ago. Now we are
glad to get ideas of Western civilisation.
As requested, The Bulletin has consid-
ered the case of Binghi, and finds that while
Bmghi was permitted to carry out a “philo-
sophical idea of isolation,” he was alive; he
only began to die when he began to have
intercourse with a nation of a different
color. In fact, it was the very intercourse
which Kwei Chih reckons Binghi needed
for his perpetuation that has caused his vir-
tual extinction. On the other hand, The
Bulletin agrees with Kwei Chiii that
Western ideas are valuable goods to have
about the premises, and that Chinese ideas
are inferior. And because Australia wants
the best goods discoverable, it has decided to
keep , out the inferior article; and Mr.
Chih’s implied confession merely supports
it in that attitude.
“Milmeridien,” from the Front Gate : —-
Was it Prof. Spencer who instigated the ab-
surdity of appointing as chief protector of Northern
Territory aborigines an otherwise worthy but super-
annuated gentleman of 66? What blazing folly it
is! Any man who understands the Northern nig.
must have a big affection for our dirty, sore-eyed
and ill-treated countrymen. They lie like gas-
meters and smell like dead sharks, but they’re the
kindliest things on earth. Always they are show-
ing us in actuality the things our Wowser brethren
enjoin on us, but which we never do ourselves. I
have never known any of these Northern men
to bear malice for personal injury. They may
kill for jealousy, for superstition, and in spiteful
cruelty; but there’s a lot of generosity and chari-
tableness in them. And the local Administration’s
policy is all in the interests of the white man, as it
was under the old regime to which the new-old protec-
tor belongs, and not for the blacks the Australian
people desire to protect. The Administration is
bad through and through. You can’t get any land
in all these millions of acres unless it’s a fresh-
water selection on the Daly—up to 1000 acres,
e\ery farm teeming with fish, crocodiles (porosus),
ducks and geese, and scarcely enough dry land at
present to grow a cabbage. The Daly River fraud
is going to make a row loud enough to be heard in
China when the “farmers” arrive to claim their
sections. You certainly can get a grazing ar<‘a
(with permissive occupancy for a year) up to 250
square miles, at a shilling per head per annum for
big stock and 6d. for small; but you’re liable to be
thrown out on three months’ notice. The Common-
wealth Government invites people here (and in the
case of Americans pays the exes.), and then tells
them they can’t get land except on these very
foolish terms. It’s all a farce, but there’ll be
tragedy behind it all. A few weeks back a concrete
and iron bridge over the railroad was completed.
It cost about £2OOO. The Administration now
finds it was stupidly planned and constructed, is
incapable of carrying the traffic, and that (as the
crown of the joke) it was built on private land
without the owner’s consent. So —we have de-
molished it with dynamite! Grotesque; but, after
all, only part of the piece.

“Vagabond” tells of Egyptian light and
Australian darkness: —
In Egypt I found several points of similarity
between that country and Australia, and I also had
to regretfully take off my hat to the superior way
in which Egypt has grabbed Opportunity by the
forelock, or, if Opportunity hadn’t a forelock, the
way Egypt got to work with the hair restorer and
raised a sufficient crop to get a grip of. Port
Sudan is an excellent instance of a long-distance
look into the future. Only 15 years ago the Sudan
was the happy hunting-ground of the Mahdi and
the dervish tribes. The Omdurman performance
burst that, and then Egypt began to .figure out the
Sudan’s prospects of trade. There was already a
railway to Alexandria and Port Said, both flourish-
ing seaports with good accommodation and shipping
facilities and connection such as Sydney and Mel-
bourne possess. Egypt did some tape-measuring,
and concluded that cheaper and quicker transport
would boost the backblocks of the Sudan and the
country generally. Suakim was selected as an out-
let, and a railway rapidly strung along to it. That
was in 1904. Suakim was good enough in its way
(as Port Jackson and Port Phillip are good enough
in their ! ways) ; but it was evident that another
and better port would help trade and the country,
even as other and better ports would do ditto for
Australia. A survey showed that there was a natur-
ally good position for another port at Port Sudan,
just as it "is admitted that Twofold, Jervis, Port
Stephens, etc., are good natural ports. Egypt
took off. its coat or its jibbah or whatever it
wears, and got such a hustle on that Port Sudan
was formally opened as a harbor in 1909. (Here the
Australian "narallel falls dead.) The new outlet
was on the beach of a sandy desert without even
an occasional camel on the horizon ; now there is
an up-to-date port, sizzling with electric arc-
lamps and purring with electric capstans and elec-
tric cranes. Of course, it couldn’t have come into
existence without the backing up of the railways,
any more than our deep-water ports can. But the
Egyptian and Sudan Government railways are
there for the good- of the country, not of the fat
cities. The Railway Department- reckoned that
trade would come from Kordofan arid. Obeid if the
railways went to them. The Tails hurriedly

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cathedral Church of St. Andrew’s, Easter Day
10.30, Anthem, “Oh, Give Thanks.”
—Sydney advt.
Now the Easter bells, are pealing, and I
draw me to the fold,
Where in gratitude I raise my voice on high
To a kind and loving Providence for bless-
ings manifold,
For the miracles that awe and edify.
Teh* the Miracles are with us, we can see
them day by day—
Think of ’Enley when he prayed for Den-
man’s home;
Prayed confusion on disloyalists who’d drive
“the Flag” away—
Now is judgment ’gainst them entered on
the Tome.
Oh, give thanks, ye meek and lowly, while the
ferry strike is on,
Fbi> the Daily Telegraph is by your side.
It has risen from its thraldom as the Rich
Man’s myrmidon ;
It is with the stricken host this Eastertide.
This wealthy eo., it thunders, has had pay-
ment in advance,
Y« “ignored an undertaking” to the crowd.
In effect, it’s joined the strikers in their
brutal arrogance—
Which is more than even Wealth should be
allowed !
Oh, give thanks, my fainting brothers ; comes
of penitents one more,
With a pious look upon his eager face.
This be Joseph Cook, ex-Tory, yea, but
, _ Tory now foreswore;
His sins are purged, no more a hardened
case;
He will fight the goodly battle for the
wearied under-dog;
From the people’s cause he ne’er again will
rat.
Shout a fervid hallelujah, write his name
in Heaven’s log—
Joseph Cook, Esquire, the Labor-Democrat!
Oh, give thanks and burn your incense for
the law of this our land
That is sweeping all the evil from our track :
For the “cat” that swings and whistles so
that all may understand,
No matter what in intellect they lack.
Sing hosanna for the gallows (this is Cruci-
fixion time,
And ’tis not long since the Cross to gallows
changed)—
What sentimental fool was it who said the
law’s a crime
That sentences to death a child deranged?*
Let us close, beloved brethren. What a heri-
tage is ours !
Yea, and glorious the Nation we shall be !
Oh, sing a psalm of ten to von, nor heed the
cloud that lowers
Where Asia turns and hovers hungrily.
For our little brother Billy he is learning
how to drill,
And, by and bye, in twenty years or so,
We shall have a grown-up army that for
readiness and skill
Will give ’em all a fair and willin’ go.
We shall then chastise the heathen, and the
wicked shall be broke ;
Our star-spread banners flaunting on their
walls.
And, now, the Lord be with you all —go
out and have a smoke,
And watch how Thumper wallops googly
balls.

had incautiously asked him if the shilling was the
fh’ C tw]p setur?5 etur ? tr , lp ’ ,“ Yer ca m’t expec’ us ter do
th single trip fer less ’an a shillin’.”
The launch rolled deeply in the green swells, and
the silent passengers, cramped underneath the roof,
searched the cabin for life-saving gear, and, observ-
ing none, wondered if any was carried overhead
they went on wondering. The familiar harbor, so
exceedingly narrow and shallow when mail steamers
are clumsily getting in the way and disturbing the
bottom with their .twin screws, seemed unusuallv
deep and empty. It had the bleak, clean-swept look
ot a busy street early on Sunday morning. Circu-
lar Quay corner, where the trim white-and-yellow
terries usually give a sidekick' to their rippling
green-and-white skirts, looked like the entrance to a
church on a week-day. The ferries themselves,
clustered thicklv at their wharves, were uninhabited
f., fnbkckss. A few wobbling launches crawled
like.futile beetles about the Milson’s Point pontoon
where, at ordinary times, the big boats of the ser-
vice glide like trays of undrowned flies, and pretend
they are just as useful as, a bridge.
„ Tl ' e shilling launch did not impudently wobble to
the Sydney Ferries’ leased jetties in Circular Quav •
it just tottered gently to one of the Fort Macquarie
jetties and allowed its passengers to jump ashore to
the penny tram. During the shoreward scramble,
some of them noticed that the roof was unencum-
bered with lifebelts. The life-saving gear of the
launch, if it existed at all. was concealed as well
as if it had been opium. Some of the landed people
took the penny tram round to the city; others
started on the long, hot tramp uptown. As I went
I figured my water-travelling exes, for the day thus :
Self, two trips to and from office, 2s. Wife and self,
double trip to and from theatre job at night, 4s. •
total, 6s. Well, if it breaks me, I will have the
consolation of knowing that Private Enterprise got
it all. Blessed be Private Enterprise! Its meat is
also its poison, and its poison is its meat; conse-
quently, dear brethren, it is in no danger of dying
out altogether. Let ps prey.
That signpost of Tory incompetence,
Charles Gregory Wade, leader of the Oppo-
sition in N. S. Wales Assembly, has returned
trorn China and Japan with his capacity for
putting his foot in it thoroughly recuper-
ated. The other day. he fell clumsily upon
a couple of “Liberals” who are not satisfied
with party arrangements, and was promptly
and painstakingly cuffed by one of them
(H. D. Morton, M.L.A.). One biff:—
, I* J s just as well for' someone to stand up and
tell Mr. Wade openly and candidly that all through
the country, amongst erstwhile Liberal supporters,
there is a total lack of sympathy with his party and
its tactics under his leadership.
Morton says that. his own contemptuous
opinion of Wade is clearly shared by the
country, and quotes as proof that when Car-
handed over the Premiership to him
(Made), the party’s majority in the Assem-
bl/ was 18. Three years later, at the poll
of 1910, the majority dwindled to a mino-
rity, while the chance of making up lost
ground at the elections this year is almost
as small as Wade’s national spirit.

“Why Women Love Wowsers,” with
Get-Rich-Quick Niblo as the first Wowser
stage-hero in history, was explained at
Sydney . Criterion on Saturday. They love
him chiefly for his beautiful black clothes,
his neat fiat flat, his fondness for church,
his holy face, his humility, and the
bright hope that he is a fraud.
And Nathaniel Duncan, “The Fortune
Hunter,” is a fraud —the useless son
Of an indulgent father, who brought
him up expensively and then died broke.
Nathaniel discovers not only that he dis-
likes work, but he can’t get it. His
rich friend, Harry Kellogg, suggests a way
to become a millionaire in one year. One
way, as Mr. Niblo has shown us, is to in-
vent a covered carpet tack ; the other is to
marry a female rube. Apparently, there
are several million female rubes in the U.S.,
all inhabiting small country towns. The
bright young men of these backblock vil-
lages slip away to N’York to sweat blouse-
making factory girls or to corner sawdust
sausages in Wall-street. The bright young
girls are left. Kellogg fits out his hard-up
friend with the latest striped socks, and
sends him down to corner the marriage
market in the backblocks. He follows
instructions and bashfully accepts the
hand of the selected heiress.' Unfortunately
he has fallen in love with the daughter of
the drug-store where he got employment.
But he has made that derelict chemist’s
shop pay, and the derelict chemist has in-
vented a method of making gas—which
should come in useful in Sydney when the
next quarterly gas strike is due—the
“pattent” of which is worth £lO,OOO. He
has, therefore, no hesitation in allowing
the. heiress to break the engagement by re-
fusing to clear himself from the accusation
that he is an absconding bank cashier.
Such is the slight . plot, but the comedy
“goes” with the cleverness of the incidental
business and the rattle of the dialogue inher-
ent in this sort of American play. A useful
touch of pathos is introduced in two delight-
ful characters, that of the derelict inventor
and his drudge of a daughter. And the
final curtain, though obviously manufac-
tured, finishes the. comedy with American
slickness.
“THE MAN ON THE BOX,” AT THE
LITTLE THEATRE.
W. R. Hunter
as Pierre, the
Colonel's chef.
Fred Niblo, as the Wowser, is afforded
more scope for comedy than in “G. R. Q
Wallingford,” to which comedy “The For-
tune Hunter” is a first cousin. There are
the same rubes in each, and the plot and
setting are strikingly similar. Mr. Niblo
shows himself an expert and pleasing com-
edian. His antics behind the sodawater bar,
and his assumption of the Wowser and the
shop-walker manner, were unctuous, though
tinged with a tendency, easily forgiven, to
clown. In the first act, as the useless
young man, down and out, he proved him-
self a convincing actor. Miss Josephine
Cohan, who appears as a slatternly girl in
the_ petered-out drug-store, had the oppor-
tunity denied her in her previous appear-
ance of proving herself an accomplished
comedienne. She never stresses her
emotions; the character is real. Ray L.
Royce, as the genial dreamer-inventor with
the sweet smile, was also absolutely in
character. But both he and Miss Cohan
were exhorted by the gallery to speak up.
Robert Greig, as the business-man who
backed his friend to marry a million dol-
lars, had the task of talking hard all through
the first (and womanless) act, and man-
aged it. Edwin Lester, the richest rube,
gave a consistent rendering of the part, and,
as a “Wallingford” youth, E. Pirie Bush
confirmed the good impression he had
already made. The other backblocks
heroine, Miss Marion Dunn, did her best,
and Miss Beatrice Holloway’s infantile
charm was well placed in an ingenue part.
The other characters, all of whom shouted
most of the time, except ope who was
pleasantly drunk, gave the plav the 1000
horse-power necessary to carry it over the
patches of badly metalled roadway of the
plot.

“We hope to see you here again. Remem-
ber, we are here every night. Hugh Buck-
ler, managing-director of the Little Theatre,
Sydney, shot a look of humorous appeal at
the crowded first-night audience, and seemed
refreshed by the answering roar. Leading
lady Violet Paget, in response to a special
cheer, smiled and faltered her thanks over
the usual rampart of flowers, and the new
curtain made a final rustling descent. The
Little Theatre (late Standard) waves its
golden sign-light in Castlereagh-street, just
alongside the Central Fire Station, and the
wandering playgoer can always . find it by
breaking a street fire-alarm and going back
in state on an engine.
“The Man on the Box,” with which the
season opened on Saturday, is a three-act
dramatisation of Harold Macgrath’s novel
of American society life. It is a bustling
comedy which starts with a misfire practical
joke. Lieutenant Robert Worburton dis-
guises himself as a coachman with the idea
of driving his sister’s carriage home after a
dance, and kissing her as she alights. He
manages to get on the wrong carriage, and
drives off instead with a couple of strange
ladies. Captured by police, he is locked up
for the night, and next morning the kissed
lady (a friend of his sister’s, with whom he
has fallen in love, but to whom he has not
yet been introduced) calls and pays the fine
of the handsome young stranger. He accepts
her challenge to take service with her family
as coachman, and endures various mild humil-
iations while she is falling in love with
him. The reckless but gentlemanly pa of
the bride-elect sells a Russian diplomatist-
spy the plans of some fortifications. Being
a hero of boundless wealth, 11., Buckler
light-heartedly snatches the plans from the
Russian, and arranges to redeem pa’s note
of hand next morning. The play is. sugary,,
and the central idea highly improbable; but
it contains some good comedv scenes, is
brightly worked out, and the Little Theatre
company—an agreeably even array of talent
-—gets every ounce out of it.
* * * *
Hugh Buckler is almost an ideal Worbur-
ton—the resistless army youth of romantic
fiction. Violet Paget makes a suitably be-
witching Betty Annesley—the rash young
lady who engages the kissing coachman. She
handles her comedy scenes with vim, and
wears Some exquisite gowns. Reynolds Den-
niston grapples skilfullv in a minor key with
the unheroic part of Betty’s busted pa; G.
R. Souper deals lightly with the role of
v\ orburton s reporter friend, Henderson;
and Arthur Cornell makes Judge Watts
the fussy old man without whom no
light comedy can get along. Others play
with a thoroughness that give the production
a polish; the mounting is adequate; and,
generally, the playseeker is confidently ad-
vised to go, along and inspect foy himself.
