Business has been brisk at the Pitt-street
(Sydney) stand during the past week, and
the fine party of mummers playing “Dr
Wake’s Patient” for the Ilamilton-Plimmer
firm has been receiving a sustaining help-
ing of applause, which, it may be recalled
in this season of Christian humility and
horseracing, the dour Carlyle warned re-
cipients was not fame.
Wirths’ circus can’t accommodate the
crowds that want to feel the momentary
but delicious thrill of wondering whether
Mrs. Garcia’s somersaulting car will
land right side up. La Belle Victoria
still swings gaily as a bird on her long
'slack wire, the Codonas whirl gladly
through the middle air. the elephants bow
with their usual grace, “Lizzie” is as cheer-
fill as ever, and the clowns sprinkle the
performance with their wonted sorrow.
Proof that Ireland is a most distress-
ful country was found by hundreds
of confirmed Australians who visited the pic-
ture show put on for the past three nights
at the Town Hall (Sydney). “Ireland To-
day” the show was nominated, and the title
conveyed a fair view of the position. The films,
to a considerable extent, were what is known
in the trade as “rainy”—that is, flickered
and split—due, possibly, to over-work; but
they illustrated to painfulness the gulf in
suffering Ireland between ignorance and pov-
erty, on the one side, and arrogance and
wealth on the other. So much for the in-
habitants as the photographer screened them ;
and, presumably, he did his best, since his
main object was to secure the appreciation
of their kith abroad. For so much of the
scenery as was shown, this scribe has little
to say, except that the fellow who wrote that
Heaven reflected in Ivillarney was a
poor traveller, or else the original press
agent of the Golden Shore overdid the thing.
It was somewhat discouraging, too, to con-
template. the exhibited samples of Irish
beauty-girls and reflect upon the raptures of
some of the Celtic poets and other peddlers in
imagination. Still, it wasn’t the fault of the
girls ; and if the camera-man had known his
job he would have surreptitiously run into
his collection a few Manly surf maidens, and
saved the reputation of a wickedly-depleted
nation.
If the first appearance of “Milestones,” at
Melbourne Royal, fell a trifle short of ex-
pectation, it was not that rumor had rated
the comedy too highly, but that the theatre
seemed somewhat large for a play of very
limited action. Nothing to speak of happens
m “Milestones” save the breaking off of com-
monplace marriage engagements, through
the interference of relatives of the contracted
parties, or the withdrawal of objections to
an engagement after it has been advanced,
or the free and independent betrothal in the
third act of a middle-aged couple whose mar :
riage was prohibited in the second act, wheii
they were young. The little incident that
closes the domestic story is the prettiest,
perhaps. Old John Rhead, having remarked
that the rebellious young women of the new
generation are heartless and “unsexed,” is
surprised by his up-to-date granddaughter’s
Graceful display of sentimentality. Then he
snakes his head over the rose she has brought
him as a peace-offering, and reflects that
We live and learn.” For ancient people in
the audience, “Milestones” is a sermon with
the old, old text uttered in its final words.
For the young, it is full of sweet flattery,

“The Chocolate Soldier,” originally adver-

not be disimproved if Wynne continues to
lepiace Roberts in the Alexius part, as he

the opera, go louder than of yore—for that

S 1 be . au thor, Theodore Kremer, has

The 1 atal \\ edding, and copied the com-

dous shindy a fond mamma was separated
from her doleful husband to be fed by her

goes forth in his ickle bare feet into fashion-

of the trouble is a stylish miscreant. Harold
Crane, the twin brother of a semi-mystic,
the Rev. George, who differs only from
Harold inasmuch as he has a kind of gap
in Ins walk, while Harold has holes in his
reputation. Harold, of course, is aided in
his devilry by the customary lady who plavs
a big hand in the divorce proceedings, and
is, later, a witness to his assassination of
brother George—a crime indicated by a
gurgle from the O.P. wings. The pair even-
tually tall out, and they carry their crazv
argument into _a cathedral interior, where
Harold, preaching a bogus sermon in the
vestments of his relative, is attacked by his
passionate female accomplice, p,nd the nair
stab one another to a gory conclusion.

• At , Wi Hhs’ Hi PP o drome, inside the build-
ing, the VV onder Show is open twice a day
to all-comers. Seekers after the unusual will
surely find it. Some circus and vaudeville
items are foreign to the beaten tracks; the

painful significance attaches to the Shooting
Monkey; and the Fat Man of the establish-
ment is alleged to turn the scale at 55 stone.
The strong suit at Melbourne Majestic
last week was “The Trail of Cards,” a
narrative dealing with the hot, misguided
passion of' a greaser called Pedro for a
prairie flower named Bess. The lady be-
stows her shy affections on a young ranch
foreman, whereon Pedro becomes unpleasant
in his rude Spanish way, and soon has the
film flicking off anger and commotion.
“Face to Face with a Leopard” gave a
wild-animal sensation, and “John Arthur’s
Trust” decorated the screen with the atti-
tudes of John protecting his ward’s large
fortune from the fingers of an adventurer.
** * *
Of Spencer’s effusions at Melbourne
Olympia The Bulletin selects “When
Thieves Fall Out” as the best offering dur-
ing the last few days. It was a narrative
of buried treasure and wickedness generally,
with a ’tec uncovering the nefarious con-
duct of a banker, a lawyer and a handsome
lady, what time a lovely lieutenant plays
the devil with a bashful maiden’s heart. A
quaint contribution was “The Virtue of
Rags,” the idea of which strolled or strayed
into the plot from that fruitful source of
suggestion, “A Message from Mars.” “Six'
O'Clock” was one of those tense, dramatic
trifles that pack a life’s excitement into two
or three minutes; while all that can be said
for “Willie Watches Over the House” is
that the programme ought to be fined a
large sum for misdescribing it as a “comic.”

Consider the nerve and pluck of Herbert
Bradford, of Brunswick (Vic.), livery-
stable keeper. On the evening of March 15
he was driving the foremost of a string of
14 four-horse drags along the Beach-road
between Sandringham and Brighton. The
night was black and wet, he was alone
on the box-seat; inside were nearly a score
of passengers. Coming to a level crossing
he noticed the gates ooen for him, and the
gatekeeper and a friend standing beside
them, talking. Just as he got on the rails
the further gate, on the “down” side, was
either shut against him or blown so by the
wind, and he stopped dead. At the same
instant he saw the lights of an on-coming
train from Sandringham, on the “up” line.
The gatekeeper shouted to him to go back—
with a four-horse drag and about three
seconds to do it! He could jump to safety
and leave the picnickers to their fate; but
he wasn’t that sort of man. He could sit
there and meet it bravely with the rest; but
he wasn’t that sort of man, either. Swifter
than thought he pulled the horses round and
drove them, plunging and rearing, over
rails and sleepers along the “down” line.
The back of the drag was caught by the
engine and ripped away; one passenger had
some broken ribs and several suffered shocks
and minor casualties. Only one man, mis-
trustful of the driver, jumped—and jumped
straight to his death, merciful in its swift-
ness. Every other life was saved.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
, , i f a " y Prominent actors on the stage in London
take a pride in being first with a new stvle of trmi
sers or a coat of a novel cut.—tL Ige tFOU '
’Tis well to blather in the light,
And tear a passion all to bits,
To soar with Hamlet’s highest flight
Or score with Falstaff’s nimble wits ;
Hut while the modern actor rants
His hopes are centred in his pants.
be in favor nowadays
’Tis not enough to act a bit,
You have to brace your ribs in stays
To make your special waistcoat fit
Success depends, e’en with the star,
On what you wear, not what you are.
Observe the thoughtful actor stand,
His forehead seamed with many a line:
the hot flatiron in his hand
Turns creases in his trousers fine.
Respect the sanctuary of art -
The pro. is studying his part!
Shakesbacon ruin spells because
His Romeo wears nought of note
In spotted vests, and scorns the laws
That rule the cutting of a coat,
tame waits the actor who’ll translate
And tailor Hamlet up to date.

SOCIETY
“Kola”: The Federal Parliament has its
hands full of work, certainly, but surely it
might find time to pass a short Act forbid-
ding marriage between whites and colored
people. One case has been prominently in
the public eye; there are scores of other
cases that, being merely sordid, never get
there. Why not follow in the lead of Cali-
fornia? There such marriages are illegal,
and the local infatuated and ill-balanced
Miss who wants to marry an Asiatic has to
go north to British Columbia to perpetrate
her foolishness. Public sentiment would be
found almost wholly behind such a measure ;
the only opposition would come from the
small knot of brethren who continue to
preach the futile old gag that “all men are
brothers,” but would mostly faint if they
were told that their daughter was “walking
out” with a Chow vegetable John. Fur-
ther, cohabitation between the two races
should be made illegal, with a penalty on
both the male and female. What is the use
of orating about a White Australia when
Chows and Hindus are allowed to marry
our white women and breed piebald Aus-
tralians?
“Ru” : A newsboy was punished the other
day in Melbourne for yelling his wares too
loudly, and a lot of people were filled with
holy glee. I want something done to the
German band. Why an able-bodied mob
should be allowed to block the streets in
business hours, making noises that are
merely distressing to those who are doing
real work, has long been a great mystery.
Also, why are they allowed to publicly beg
for alms? Is a man supposed to have a
special privilege like the blind and lame
because he is too lazy to do honest work,
and chooses to blow wind into an idiotic,
tube of brass for several hours a day? Tom
Carlyle defined dirt as “matter in the wrong
place.” The German band is in the wrong
place; therefore, the German band is dirt.
If the Gaud Mayor and City Council' of
Sydney want clean streets, let them give the
tram tickets a rest and remove the big dirt
first. Failing that, somebody will rise one
of these days and kill the German band with
an axe, and when the case is put fairly to
the jury, it will bring in a verdict of “Highly
Commended,” or words to that effect.
“N or M”: Hobart is seeing beautiful
visions. Its big pier stretches to comple-
tion, and will soon be prepared to accom-
modate anything afloat. Its length will be
1210; the depth of water is 60-odd ft., and
the approach is broad and deep and without
rocky vertebrae. Each year sees the build-
ing of larger and yet larger vessels. The
White Star line’s latest addition runs to
18,000 tons —too ponderous a monster to
play tricks with the Melbourne rip, which,
it will be remembered, showed claws to a
far smaller vessel a short time ago. The
depth of water there is all right in calm
weather, but a heavy sea alters the case,
and a big liner, wallowing in a trough, finds
the lid of Davey Jones’s locker dangerously
close. And to hang about waiting for the
weather to moderate means an expense of
at least £5OO a day. So Hobart sees itself
becoming a depbt for oversea trade and a
great distributing centre, and it already
wonders where it should put the long-talked-
of docks

A favorite caffi is endeavoring to infuse a
purple note into the oppressive grey calm
that sits on Melbourne in the early Sabbath
hours. Some of the Most Expensive, who
love to play at being wildly Continental, are
supporting the idea, which consists of get-
ting fizzy refreshments between midnight
and cockcrow from the hands of a masked
female, who also does a mild turn that fails
to bring a blush to the cheek of the most
hopeful. No outsider can secure a seat,
much less a table, for this late revelry. Seri-
ously considered, the mask is a pretentious
fraud. A Collins-street milliner with vaude-
ville yearnings alternates the star role of
mystery with a little soubrette who isn’t
continuously behind the footlights. It is all
very painstaking, and the simplest sinner
wili find it not worth the sacrifice of a
Good Night’s Rest.
TACTICS.
“Hullo, pore Bill’s got his gruel!"
“Oh, pore Bill’s awl right — ’e’s just lyin’ low."
“Montalex”: “0.1 v.” (B. 20/3/T3) gets
right on to the nail. I wear no creedal
label myself, but, as an Irishman, I have
always strongly objected to the R.C. at-
tempt to make a “corner” in Nationalism.
To say nothing more of Parnell —an Angli-
can of English descent —has “the Church”
so soon forgotten Robert Emmett, Wolfe
Tone, Smith O’Brien, Lord Edward Fitz-
gerald, and all the rest of the non-Catholics
who fought in the front rank of National-
ism? The present writer’s grandfather was
a member of the last Irish Parliament; and
I know that he was offered a heavy bribe
(not in money) to vote in favor of the
Union, and —Anglican, Tory and military
man though he was —promptly and scorn-
fully refused it.
“Pontius Potter”: Hobart has an untidy
way of burying its dead. Years ago, in the
old insanitary days, the good English cus-
tom of planting corpses around a church
was adopted. It may have been conducive
to the odor of sanctity, but it left the city
with a sprinkling of weed-grown and aban-
doned graveyards that would shed discredit
on an Asiatic community. Within gunshot
of the G.P.O. half a dozen crumbling and
decrepit cemeteries are failing desperately
to hide the bones of their occupants. Vaults
have burst open, and disclose the rotting re-
mains of cheap coffins mingled with gaping
skulls. Sportive dogs leap down and rum-
mage among the bones of the dear departed,
and small boys try their marksmanship with
stones on the plaster angels that cant drun-
ltenly on the verge of yawning sepulchres.
In the summer the rank weeds catch fire,
and the woodwork railings get charred a
funereal black, and occasionally the stone
boundary walls collapse in places and spread
over the adjacent tombs. Citizens whose
incomes will not allow them to pay the
necessary sum that entitles them to the ser-
vices of the municipal dustman creep out by
night and deposit their refuse in the handi-
est subsided grave. The trouble is that the
churches derive no more "revenue from these
boneyards, and when God’s acre ceases to
pay a dividend it goes the way of other
non-paying business propositions. The more-
recently deceased are getting no better at-
tention, and Private Enterprise is stowing
them away on the same old principle in the
midst of residential suburbs. The fool-law
that demands a grave to be at least 6ft.
deep neglects to remark how many bodies
may be buried therein. The result is that
to say that certain departed are entombed
is to exaggerate the case. Altogether, Hobart
is not the place that a tidy man would
choose to die in, and the neglect of the
authorities is making it a bad place for the
casual resident who has a desire to live.
The other day, J. Bull handed out 18
months’ gaol to the aristocratic Suffragette
who fired the Ivew Tea Gardens. On the
same day, a mere laboring bloke was
awarded seven years for making a bonfire of
some cocky’s hay stacks in Kent. The labor-
ing person may think he got a compara-
tively unfair deal, particularly as the Anarch-
istic female will, in all probability, starve
her way out within 18 days; and he will
know better for the future. When next he
has an edge on a man. he will get the missus
or the best girl to join the nearest Suffra-
gette league. She can then dynamite his
enemy’s residence, poison his whisky, or tie
kerosene-tins to the tail of his house-dog
with impunity—or next door to it.

THE HOLY BLOUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The open-work blouse will live in fashion ; and
skirts, though they may change a little, will pro-
bably keep the curves of the figure now so much
admired. — Fashion item.
Who makes a poem to his mistress’ brow
(A faultless crescent over starry eyes)
Is too ideal for our ladies now,
Who care not inspiration for, nor sighs ;
And so my ardent glance makes gay carouse
Around the yoke of Muriel Edith’s blouse.
Man he makes garments for his nakedness;
Women use clothes their charms to advertise.
Once crinolines and all their swollen dress
Hinted at beauty, which was mostly lies;
And Princess robes that only youth could bear
Were fatal to the lady fat and fair.
And then the bustle, to exaggerate
The part that schoolboys used once to be taught
on ;
Man’s coats, superfluously to state
That woman likes as manly to be thought on ;
And then she threw away these modes of apes,
And was converted to her primal shapes.
Tight bodice, tight short skirt and curven stays
To make her figure an imperfect S
(For which her trepidatious tamer pays)—
The front and southern bourgeons of her dress
At last, returning to her ancient shape,
That snake who Eden shared with the First Ape.
Now, by the straight front and the swelling bust,
By grace of webbing and suspenders she
Raises the reptile’s shape from ancient dust
(Ere Adam lost his job in Arcady),
And comes to ask us with her ancient lies
To start an orchard in New Paradise.
I’ll make no moan unto a brow of light;
I’ll sing no verses praying “Death come soon”;
I’ll not grow hoarse by crying to the night;
I’ll raise no howling rhymes unto the moon;
A cage of lace my waking thoughts shall house -
The teasing perforations of the blouse.
Flesh-pink beneath the ventilated white,
Flesh-pink the breeze knows, though I may not
see —
Oh, ivory column gleaming in the night,
The neck that grows out of sweet mystery !
Oh, Sol., thy life has chosen well, well done !
Wise Singer of the Song! Oh, Solomon!
Lips of geranium, skin of cream and rose;
The eyes like pools; the brow as white as milk ;
The dainty head and its mock-haughty pose;
The neck of ivory that shines as silk;
The glance that’s daring and yet seems afraid —
The terror of becoming an old maid.
And the arcana, underneath the laces,
Hinting near facts and mystery far away,
The unknown beauties of the fragrant places,
The ears pink as the shell new-flushed with day;
A breath like scent of hay and breath of cows,
Above the meshes of that meat-safe blouse.
I care not how all other ladies dress,
Nor when, if she change not, all fashions cease !
Oh, blouse, the haven of white silences,
Hiding from men the snowy hills of peace;
Let fashion go in hoops; or, nude, carouse,
So long as Muriel wears a Holy Blouse!
N.S.W.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

sorts of medicines that proved useless. Being almost
afraid I could never be cured, I tried Chamberlain’s
Tablets, and can assure you that they completely
cured me, and I feel like a new man. *

One of the most humorous results of par-
son Albert Knight’s bolt with his best girl
was the Bishop of Ripon’s personal address
to the flock of the wandering shepherd. The
Bishop was introducing a temporary shep-
herd to the forsaken ones, and he spake as
follows:—■
Y ? U L h m\ gon fu throug i 1 a fier y trial of faith. You
must hold together and stand firm and steady for
your faith which has been so sorely tried. Mav
the winter of this discouragement be turned into a
glorious spring-time!
Now, the simple truth is that the brethren
had been enjoying the time of their lives.
Their cup of scandal had been filled to over-
flowing and sugared with three lumps. They
had derived the greatest possible spiritual
satisfaction from assuring one another that
the shepherd’s little lapse was just what
they had feared would happen, inasmuch as
the young woman was no better than thev
had expected. What the deali Bishop called
°f faith " was, therefore, a
delightfully warm realisation of their most
cherished suspicions.

“Paroa” : Maoriland has
been under a cloud of
smoke for the last month
or two, in addition to its
usual pall of mortgages.
Amongst other things con-
sumed is “Hone Heke’s
flagstaff,” at Russell—
about the most historic
relic in the whole country.
1 A scrub fire did for it
what Hone Heke and an
axe did for its predecessors
no less than three times
nearly 70 years ago. Many
reasons have beeft given
for this action of his, such
as that he regarded the
flag as the emblem of the
enslavement of the Maori;
but the Maori version was
that it was an emblem of
tapu to keep trading ships
away. So Hone Heke,
being covetous of rifles,
powder, etc., made his first
chop at the flagstaff. It
happened that just as he
did so Customs duties were
repealed, and ships came
trading to Russell again.
Naturally he thought he
had hold of the right end
of the stick, so he chopped
down the thing as soon as
it was put up again. The
third time he went a bit
further, and, on March 11,
1845, not only chopped
down the flagstaff, but
sacked and burned Russell,
or Kororareka, as it was
called then. And so began
the first Maori War.
According to a breathless cable message,
Ohio (U.S.A.) is threatened with something
extra-special in the way of Wowser legisla-
tion. A political person called Capple is
feeling dreadfully shocked at the immodest
dress now worn by women, and is introduc-
ing a Bill to keep the fashions in the way
they should go. Women’s dresses must be
solid affairs, with no holes in the lace or em-
broidery, and not one particle of skin must
be allowed to show more than 2in. below the
neck. But, really, if the people of Ohio are
ail such prurient-minded decadents as the
proposed measure suggests, wouldn’t it be
better to shut them all up in an asylum?
“Warrumbucca’s” kick:—
Someone in authority should be led out in the
cold grey dawn and shot or otherwise suitably
dealt with. The N.S.W. troop trains provided for
transporting the Light Horse units from Ouean-
beyan to their various detraining stations after the
recent camp were a disgrace to a civilised com-
munity. I saw nearly all the trains leave Quean-
beyan when the camp was struck, and had the plea-
sure of a long trip in one myself. Not one lava-
WHEN MAN RULES THE
UNIVERSE.-SHIRTING CAMP.
tory carriage Was provided, and, in some cases,
troopers were fired into ordinary suburban car-
riages for a long distance journey. I do not know
whether the Molonglo River water was responsible,
but diarrhoea was epidemic in camp, and the ab-
sence of lavatory accommodation was severely felt.
As fully 80 per cent, of the Mounted troops are
serving voluntarily in order to gain practical know-
ledge of defence, it seems to me that the best car
riages available should have been placed at their
disposal, while the derelicts might have been passed
on to the bagmen, politicians, etc., who, as a rule,
do not care a darn about National Defence. When
the time comes the average Australian Light Horse-
man will be prepared to travel by cattle truck, if
necessary, but in piping times of peace he should
be provided with, at least, decent accommodation.
England’s White Slave Traffic, bad as it
is, is small potatoes alongside some of the
awful results of Freetrade and Low Wages.
At the Pioneer Club at Wellington (M.L.)
the other day an English visitor named Miss
Hodge put it as delicately as she could when
giving one reason for the Suffragettes’
uproar:—■
They were fighting to gain a living wage for the
sweated women of Britain, upon whose labor so
much of the revenue was built—labor that was so
badly paid that many of them found it impossible
to live, and so, unless they preferred death, were
forced to, live in the worst possible of all ways,
there were big firms in London, whose employees
were so badly paid that they were expected, to sup-
plement their wagfs in this way, and who were given
latchkeys as a hint that no questions would be asked
as to how they spent their off hours.
The Latchkeys of the Bottomless Pit digged
by Fat!
N.S.W. Full Court has brought all its
big guns to bear on one small bush pub. It
seems that in 1907 the Burrangong electo-
rate was graced with 40 puhs; one for
each day in Lent. In that year the electors
at the local-option poll voted Continuance.
In 1908 a publican in the electorate was
convicted of disrespect towards the law and
his license was cancelled, thus reducing the
Pubs to 39, one for each Article of the
Church. In 1910 the local-option poll again
happened along, and again the faithful voted
Continuance. In 1912 a prospective Bung
asked for a license to be granted. Local
Wowserdom hotly opposed the application
on the ground that the electors had voted
Continuance the second time on 39 pubs,
while if the license were given the number
would .be 40; and it was argued that
Wade’s Liquor Act doesn’t allow opinions
to be expressed on the ballot paper in favor
of more pubs. The Licensing Bench held
that 40 was the original number of licenses
allowable, and to the joy of the thirsty the
Full Court upheld this sensible view.
* * * *
“Briner”: Studying the log of our cruiser
the Melbourne, I thought of Graham Berry
as, in a manner, the virtual author of the
present strength of the English Navy. Eng-
land was quite satisfied witih its fleet when
Berry, as Victorian Agent-General, flung a
bombshell at the Colonial Institute. He
declared that the English navy must be
enormously strengthened, and claimed a
fleet equal to that of all the rest of the
world! Of course Germany was small
potatoes then; France was much ahead.
Berry particularly declared that the coaling
stations were unprotected. This fired Tenny-
son into writing his celebrated poem on
Naval Weakness. By his express direction
an extract from Graham Berry’s speech is
prefixed in every edition of that poem. A
national clamor arose. The Admiralty pro-
posed building 70 vessels. Randolph
Churchill, then Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer, opposed this programme, and his
fall resulted. By a coincidence, his son
Winston also opposed recent naval exten-
sions ; but he changed his tune when, ap-
pointed to the Admiralty, he could get at
the back of things. The 70 vessels author-
ised in 1889 were followed by another large
order (the Spencer) in 1894; and soon
Germany came in to flog England up some
more.
The illustrious Justice Ed-
wards, during the recent crim-
inal sessions at Auckland
(M.L.), handed down the cat
for the flogging of four sexual
lunatics (one an old man).
Owing to a flaw in the Act a
fifth escaped, much to the ex-
pressed regret of Edwards,
who promised to draw the at-
tention of the Minister of
Justice to the gap in the law,
so that the triangle might be
erected there also. If Ed-
wards’s remedy for the dis-
ease is not successful, The
Bulletin desires to draw the
attention of the Minister of
Justice to the fact that there
is still the rack, the boot, the
thumbscrew, the searing iron,
and, if the worst comes to the
worst, the faggot and the
torch.
The scriptural dictum that “unto him that
hath shall be given,” seems to have been laid
to heart by the Federal Government, which
recently announced its contribution of
£2OOO to the Scott fund. Yet the splendid
heroism of the miners at Mount Lyell, which
was displayed in the hour of real need, and
not to win fame or titles, obtained only—
let’s see? What was the princely sum which
ex-miner Fisher’s Ministry voted to the
fund for the relief of the Lyell widows and
orphans?
DUE OSTENTATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lady Scott boarded the vessel about half an hour
before sailing time, and was soon mingling unos-
tentatiously with the immense crowd of other pas-
sengers.—The Argus.
When Lady Smith is leaving us
It should be as becomes her station —-
With suitable melodic fuss,
And gratifying ostentation.
The common Mrs. Smith can flit
Without a welkin-waking racket;
But when the better classes flit
They’re called upon to make things hum.
At least they each should have a drum,
And en-er-get-i-cal-ly smack it.
Without its complement of noise,
This world would drift to stupid courses.
The Duke who knows his worth employs
A bunch of guns and maddened horses,
And with a roar and rush will come,
Just as the picture cowboy push’ll
Invade a township after rum.
No Duchess who respects our views
Will visit us on rubber shoes,
Or hide her flight beneath a bushel.
All gents, who dearly love a Lord
Abominate equivocation,
And hold the nation should afford
Each Earl his proper ostentation.
Mistaken for a feeble clerk,
The Viscount drifts across our borders.
All men whose titles shine in Burke
Should have a crimson uniform,
And like a tropic thunderstorm
Descend upon the lower orders !
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Sundown Jack”: Bill had been skiting
about his rough riding experiences when the publi-
can mentioned that there was an “outlaw” in the
yard. Did Bill reckon he could ride it? Bill reck-
oned he could ride it round the spouting of the
pub roof. So they poured an enthusiastic drink (or,
maybe, two) into him. Then Bill viewed the horse,
and they had another drink. He chose a saddle
and then there was a bob in (somebody put in Bill’s
bob). Then Bill remarked that it was a long time
since he had tamed an outlaw, and the crowd sus-
pected that Wm. was getting cold feet. So they
started to put a pint (or, mayhap, a quart) of Dutch
courage into him. Each stage in the process of
catching and saddling required another nerve
soother. He had a final one before he gathered his
reins and grasped the stirrup. All stood dumb-
founded. Perhaps they had judged him unjustly.
Then Bill's mate stepped from the spectators, and,
tapping him on the shoulder, said: “Bill, remember
the promise you made your dying mother.” With a
bowed head Bill stepped back from the tame moke.
“Boys,” he said; “it’s a fact. I promised mother
I’d never ride another outlaw. I can’t do it.” With
that he staggered from the yard, but far be it from
me to suggest that Billiam staggered through drink.
It was emotion at the thought of his dying mother
that got him down and sat on him.
“Karee”: Can “yabber plenty” many an
abo. dialect, but, have never struck “Myola.” The
nearest to it I know of is “Myalla”—bark coffin or
burying ground, and “Mawoollah”- —corroboree or
initiation place. A euphonious and appropriate ap-
pelation for Kink’s capital would have been “Mool-
looyan”—plenty dust. Or Baltabbilla —no-plurry-
water.
CONSTERNATION IN TOPHET.
The arrival of some of the Sydney milk supply.
“Pick” : Talking of “runaway holes.” Near
the Bungonia Look Down(N.S.W.), at the lower end of
a deep gully, a considerable stream pours firstly into
a roomy cave, and thence drops down an enormous
perpendicular shaft. Without again seeing the light,
the waters reach the Shoalhaven River at a point
over a thousand feet below the entrance to the shaft.
The underground passages are about five miles long,
The shaft itself has been explored during dry times.
At the bottom there is a series of limestone caves,
with a marvellous splendor of stalactites, stalag-
mites and petrified reeds. Ingress to some of these
wonders has of late years been made easier by
means of a few ladder ways, and the place has be-
come a popular local ‘resort, though it is still without
the conveniences that its interest entitles it to.
‘Silas Snell” : Donn started it. He went
over the Hump to visit the Colletts one Sunday after-
noon, and planted expectations in the heart of
Ann. He had met Ann at Scower’s hop the night
before, and the Sunday afternoon visit was a curious
coincidence, to say the least of it. The Hump was
not a considerable elevation, but a jaunt over it
to John Collett's house on the Springs side was try-
ing on a hot Sunday. Tommy’s second visit took him
to Collett’s fence at the foot of the Hump, a
quarter of a mile this side of the Collett demesne.
Ihe couple met there three times. Then the meeting
place shifted a quarter of a mile up the east side
of the rise, still further from the home of Ann. It
lingered there a week or two, when the rendezvous
jumped another couple of hundred yards up hill. In
three months it arrived at the top of the Hump, and
oscillated round the apex for some time. In seven
weeks the tryst started down the west side. It
reached Hanging Rock in February. By March 2
it had arrived at Bill’s Leap, and on the third
day of April Tommy and Ann met at Tommy's log
fence. On the Sunday following, Tommy Donn found
Ann sitting on the red rock near his hut, when he
awoke from a prolonged after-dinner nap. At the red
rock Tommy made a brave stand for a matter of 80
days; then one afternoon he gathered his belongings
into a blue blanket, and started off down the iron-
stone road. Ann intercepted him at Wonnan’s corner,
and Tommy explained that he was just taking swag
exercise to keep his back in, and allowed Miss Col-
lett to lead him home again. Two weeks after this,
Tommy, with the same weight up, made a dash for
the station at Honeysuckle. Ann encountered him
on the platform, and Tommy explained that he was
expecting an uncle of his from London. Mr. Donn
was rather moody throughout the walk home. At
the red rock he faced Ann. “Look ’ere,” he said sul-
lenly. “The fact iv it is, yo’re wantin’ t’ marry me,
’n’ it’s no use yer tryin’ t’ hide it!” After that,
subterfuge was useless. Ann married him.
“D.M.” : Is the Australian the biggest leg-
puller on earth, or what happens to the strangers
from oversea that, after talking to a few of us, they
write such gorgeous information about this country?
The other day “The Empire Annual for Australian
Girls,” edited by A. R. Buckland, M.A., wandered
into my house. One section is devoted to “Some
Australian Snake Stories,” by W. T. Greene, M.D.,
M.A., F.Z.S., and this is how he begins:—
There are many kinds of snakes in Australia, many
of which are extremely venomous—the diamond
snake, for instance, the carpet snake, . the whip-
snake and others.
He goes on to relate that travelling in the Plenty
district (near Melbourne), his companion picked up
a piece of curled wattle-bark, from the end of which
glided a diamond snake of the largest dimensions —
“that is to say, nearly three fest long! ”
Had my companion... .been bitten by the reptile
his tenure of existence would have been summarily
terminated in from 20 to 30 minutes.... There are
harmless snakes in the Australian bush as well as
venomous ones, and among the former is the black
snake, or southern python.
And not a word of warning is given against the
deadly jim-jam, which teaches its young to pursue
remittance men. Too bad !
“Mallarapoonie”: In case the Methodist
Conference or some other body of sports is short of
a thrill, let me recommend alligator trapping. The
best style of ’gator trap is a narrow stub yard,
shaped like a cattle race, and built on the tide-wash
with wings opening to the
water’s edge; while the most
efficient bait is an aged he-
goat. William, very much alive,
is placed in a small enclosure at
the landward end of the ’gator
pen, and when the saurian saun-
ters up to investigate, he inad-
vertently engages the mechanism
of a trapdoor, which falls unex-
pectedly behind him. An ad-
junct hereabouts is a rough
gallows, erected over the ’gator
pen, so that the beast may be
handled with facility. Only
scientists or late arrivals in the
country ever shoot or kill a ’gator
in his trap. A corpse of that
kind on the premises is too
dreadful. The old hand of North
Australia nooses his capture by
the neck, hauls him up to the
temporary gallows, and, while his
forepaws claw the south-west
breeze, methodically disembowels
him. The beast is then freed,
and waddles to the beach and heads for the open sea,
showing amazing vitality until the sharks, attracted
by the scent of blood, rush him, or, failing sharks,
his own kind set upon him like wolves.
“Grallina”: I can tell “Mallarapoonie”
(B. 20/2/’l3) of another dumb animal that can
count fairly well, and that is the nag that earns his
oats in any coal mine in the Newcastle or Maitland
district. I’ve seen him bringing out heavy loads
from the bords, day after day, to the accompani-
ment of the yells and screeches of the wheelers. But
sometimes a driver who is on a “you’ll do” (i.e.. a
bribe from the miner to give him more than his
ordinary share of skips) will try to put an extra
tub on to Pilot. That is where his arithmetic comes
in. He counts the jerks as the couplings draw tight,
and if there is an extra bump he stops, and neither
sleepers nor iron sprags can persuade him to budge
an inch.
“Yam”: The assertion by “Fax” (B.
27/2/’l3) that “the tuberculin test for cattle is of
no value,” is opposed to the dictum of the highest
scientific authorities. The final report of the English
Royal Commission (which sat from 1901-1911) states
that the test is absolutely reliable. Not until the
tissue of the udder is breaking down with tubercular
disease, or the cow is becoming emaciated, is the
secretion of milk visibly affected; so, before any-
thing can be detected by sight, the cow may for
months be giving poisonous milk. It is horrible that
Fax and others should be content to wait until the
EVASIVE.
SERGEANT BRANNIGAN: “ Whin Oi sid to the accused, ‘Did ye stale Ring’s alliged
horrses last wake? the mahn gave me an [?] ansther."
MAGISTRATE: “ Well, what did he say?"
B RANIGAN: "He sid, 'Ye ould divvil, ax the pigs!’"
disease is in the advanced stages before culling the
cow. Re dirty milkers as described by “Jersey” (B.
6/2/’l3). There is no doubt at all about his re-
marks applying to a great many Victorian dairymen.
I know Gippsland well, and though a steady improve-
ment is going on in the districts supervised by the
Government, even they have still a long way to go.
But all of Gippsland is not under the Government
control, and not 50 p.c. of the entire State. The
balance is in the hands of the shire councils, and
their administration of sanitary laws in this regard
is either nominal or altogether farcical. Here in the
north-east of Victoria the conditions are deplorable.
It would be absurd to say that the dairying is done
on business lines, though there are a large number
of cows. Along the Murray the dairies in N.S.W.
are very much superior to the ones in Victoria. On the
Vic. side, as well as in Gippsland, the competition for
cream supply among the factories leads to faulty
grading of the cow-fat, and the pooling of the cream
in the churns tends to discourage the scrupulous care
necessary to produce a high-class butter, as the pro-
fits are also bulked.

“ J.C.A.”: We’ve got one or two coots at
home without waiting for the festive back bushman
from Manchester. We had a Queensland-born youth
up as surveyor’s apprentice, and he filled the comedy
part easy. The second day of his appearance he was
left to boil the billy while the party did a half-hour
job at the head of a line. On returning we found
him sitting on a log, looking at a billy full of water
and a heap of sticks. “Hullo,” says the boss, “have
you no matches?” “Yes, I have plenty matches,”
says his Nibs, “but I haven’t got a bit of news-
paper to start the fire with.”
“Southern Cross”: Anybody know why
the eaglehawk does not seem to increase and multi-
ply and wax prolific in Australia? He is a long
liver (has been credited with scores of years), and,
as far as I know, has no enemies ; and, as the old
people put in a claim for one to two maternity
bonuses every year, he should by this time be a
notable feature of the birdscape. Yet he is so rare
that, in a three months' trip on the South Coast
(N.S.W.), I saw only two or three of him, though
no poison is laid around those parts, and the wild
ranges afford an ideal home.
“Inehiquin”: “Yannatherra” is quite right
about the dog being the farmer’s best friend. Blake
was going to sell 300 sheep at the Millicent (S.A.)
market, and arranged (as he was 16 miles out)
to paddock them the night before the sale
at Brown’s. When he arrived there it was
getting dark, so he opened the gate, drove
the sheep into the paddock, and cantered on
to Brown's house. There he learnt that Brown had
forgotten the arrangement, and had left some 200
of his own sheep in the paddock. It was too late
and too dark then to do anything, so, resigning all
hope of drafting the sheep in time for the market,
Blake went to bed. Next morning his dog had
gone. Blake cursed himself for a fool for not
chaining him up, and rode off to the paddock.
Arrived there he found the gale open. He rode
all over the paddock. Brown's sheep were all
there, but not one of his own. Cursing, he shut
the gate and rode off to the market. There he found
his dog and his sheep. He kicked the dog for its
carelessness in leaving the gate open.
* *■ * *
“A Perry-Winker’: Can anyone supply
'me, gratis, with a reliable remedy in the case of
a horse who has swallowed an overdose of paddy-
melon? Just at present my stable is in deep
mourning over the death of one of the horses; he
was A 1 in the morning, but a corpse in the
afternoon. And the only way I know to remove
the curse when it gets into the animal’s innards
is by a post-mortem, and a horse is never the same
when a coroner has finished with him. Just now
when the farm horses are out in the stubble —where
the melon seems to thrive—the time is ripe to hand
out any information on the subject.
* * * *
“Frae”: I’ve read all these endurance
stunts of mokes and blokes and other animals in
Australia with much patience, and now I propose to
quote a case which will out them all. At the
North-eastern Saw Mills, at Cheviot (Vic.), there
resided a cat with six kittens on her hands (father’s
address unknown). When they were a few weeks
old she used to bring home a small bird oc-
casionally, but one day she faded to return to the
mill from a hunting expedition. Nine days later,
one of the bushmen, returning from work, heard a
faint mewing from some bushes. On investigation,
he found the cat transfixed by a sharp stake, upon
which she had evidently landed when springing for
the earth from a tree trunk. She was in such a
position that it was only with her fore paws that
she could reach the ground. There she hung, and
endured untold agonies from the wound, exposure,
hunger and milk pressure. Nevertheless, the vitality
of the beast was such that when released and cared
for she rapidly recovered and took over the family,
which in the meantime had been boarding with a
frail young aunty.
DEAD EASY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Bill shakes hands with "Jh the mob
At Chance-it, on the Yarrangob,
You’ve got to understand
That Bill is bound for Sydney-side
To cut his cheque out good and wide
In style to beat the band.
For Bill has not been there before ;
In fact, I must say, furthermore,
He has not further sped
Than once or twice to Down-and-Out
To slake a predatory drought
And paint that small place red.
“Well, so long, Bill,” the boys once more
Shout after him in hearty roar,
And Bill he waved his dook.
When Ginger King ran up the hill,
And yelled, “I say, there, hold on, Bill;
I wanter ask yer—look,
When you get down to Sydney-side
Where do you reckon to reside?
Where will you bunk and grub?”
“Gorstruth,” says Bill, you oughter know!
When I get inter town—What-o 1
I’m stopping at the pub!

“WORTH POUNDS,” SAYS WANGANUI WOMAN.
“Nearly every summer I have an attack of dysen-
tery,” says Mrs. E. J. Vinall, Dublin Street, Wan-
ganui (N.Z.). “Last summer I was so bad with it
that I had to consult a doctor, and not getting any
relief from his prescription, decided to give Cham-
berlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy a trial. The re-
sult was amazing; I felt my usual self after three
doses. Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy is
a wonderful medicine, and I would not be without a
bottle in the house now. It is worth pounds.”*
Disciples of I. Walton (deceased) will
find plenty of handy information in Mick
Simmons’s 1913 catalogue. Besides price
lists, there is a tide-table, a directory of fish-
ing resorts, and an illustrated description
of principal Australian fish. It’s worth hav-
ing, even if you do catch your fish at Deme-
trius Spagoni’s.*
“Wilson’s Street Directory of Sydney and Sub-
urbs,” which includes a Mountains tourist guide, a
heap of general information about theatres, consuls,
wharves, Government Departments, etc., and 70
maps of the various suburbs, etc. —celebrated its
eleventh birthday this year. It is a valuable infant,
worth a lot more than Is. to anybody who ever has
to go hunting for anybody else.

“Gouger”: About the most cussed of all
the world’s beasts of burden is the donkey. I had
experience with a team of 17 over heavy sand coun-
try in AV.A., and that’s how I know. Also, I had
one blackboy with me, and that's how he knows.
Remember one morning when we had done six miles,
the sun poured down like liquid Sheol. . “Donkey
fellar travel all right come along night?” I asked
the blackboy. “Him fellar all right. Him walk
along quick night time,” the native replied. It was
16 miles to the next waterhole. AVe turned the team
out for a few hours, and started again in the cool of
the afternoon. All went well, until the dark set in.
That was the donkeys’ knock-off time, and they
stuck to the rules. I tried moral persuasion ; I tried
a little intellectual argument; I knocked the whip to
ribbons, smashed the handle, and lost my beautiful
contralto voice for ever. Meanwhile, the stars were
shining beautifully from the far-off heavens. I sat
down exhausted, while the boy unyoked the team and
drove them five miles back to the waterhole. Never-
theless, it is marvellous the weights the little brutes
will carry. One team of 24 used to pull a load of
seven tons over 200 miles to a way-out station in
N.AV. Australia. The trouble is to get them loaded
and started. I’ve seen them pull the packs off with
their teeth and kick the stuffing out of them. I’d
as soon try to manage 17 wives as 17 donkeys, and
that’s putting the case as fairly as I can for all
parties.

“Kukipi” : Re “Yarrum’s” pet shark (B.
31/10/’l2). In the AVilliams River (Papua) there
is a huge alligator known amongst us white men as
“John William.” He is the chief marine scavenger
of the native village of Motu-motu, and sometimes
disputes with the village pigs for pride of place as
principal land-garbage remover, also. In these
encounters the hogs generally get the worst of the
argument. Should a nigger be in the way when
John AVilliam is carrying out his land duties, the
nigger is removed, too. It is quite a daily event to
see John AVm. swimming near the shore where the
village is built, and in view of any natives who may
be on the beach. Should they jeer at him or throw
stones. Jno. AV. treats the matter with contempt.
a vessel anchors off Motu-motu, he noses
around for anything he may pick up. On one occa-
sion an oil launch, which is Often in the river, and
whose captain knows John’s ways, happened to be
anchored near the shore opposite the village. The
saurian came along as usual, and endeavored to get
aboard. A passenger, becoming alarmed, beat him
off, whereupon the captain interjected, “AVhatcher
hittin’ John AVilliam for ? He’s hungry, and only
came around for a feed.” And with that the
skipper passed a junk of salt beef overboard. John
AVilliam collared it, and swam off.

“Windy Wellington”: Let the immi-
grant score one. He was engaged on the wharf here
unloading Oregon pine. The other day all hands
were carrying 4x2 scantlings from the ship and
dumping them on the wharf. The Yank crew every time
carried two pieces and hustled like bees. Not so
new-chum Pat. He carried one every time, and did
it with a sedate stride. AVhen the mate observed
it he went for Pat: “You lazy hound, why in
thunder don’t you take two, the same load as the
others? I don’t want to be here till Christmas.”
Pat looked very serious. “Ut’s this way,” he said,
“the other divils are too lazy to walk back for
the second piece.”

“Fax” : “N. or M.” and “Leo Lear” (B.
9/1/’ 13 and 6/2/’l3) are very emphatic in calling
the starling an imported pest, and have hardly a
good word to say for him. I would advise them to
visit this district (Yarram, South Gippsland). Down
here we mention the starling in our prayers. The
bird made its appearance here five seasons ago, and
in that time it has wiped the grasshopper curse
right out, and is now busy doing ditto to the cater-
pillars. And when it has finished the caterpillar it
will probably tackle the fruit, or the cows, or our-
selves. There is no more striking spectacle than
that of a Starling .eating a cow.

“Poonie”: I don’t want to appear argu-
mentative, but must dispute “Te Babarantiko’s”
statcfflfent (B. 13/2/’l3) that T. Tanna, Esq., of the
South Pacific Islands, is not a decadent institution.
I can’t supply figures, for the reason that, in many
instances, figures relating to the ravages of disease
among South Sea Islanders simply don’t exist; but
my own personal knowledge convinces me that “Te
Babarantiko” is wrong in his generalities however
correct he may be in his specific instances. AVhere-
ever the march of white “civilisation” has pro-
gressed in Oceania the brown races have invariably
gone down. Not only that, but “civilisation” (of an
unspeakably loathsome order, in many instances)
visited atolls and islands where it didn’t take up its
permanent abode, but merely remained long enough
to leave a dreadful reminder of its stay. And where
the Asiatic either followed the white in the Pacific,
or was introduced by him, leprosy was heaped on
syphilis, as shame might follow shame, or as one
injustice might be piled upon another. In the Fiji
group, in Tonga and the Ellice Islands, and in most
of the older-settled quarters of the Pacific, the brpwn
race has suffered all the time. Only lately, right
under our noses in Australian-administered and sup-
posedly-clean Papua, and within hail of the big
white settlement of Port Moresby, where a Govern-
ment medical officer and supervisor of native troubles
has resided for the last 20 years, cases of both loath-
some diseases have been dragged to light. And if
the medical officer lately appointed to inquire into
disease in Papua can dig out leprosy and things in

“Eddyson”: Crater was held to be blame-
worthy. It was he who incited the Rev. Walter
AVinterfleld to visit Cup-Cup. Meeting the R ev
Winterfield at Cross-roads, sitting dubiously in his
buggy, Crater had paused to give advice and invite
confidence. Cup-Cup, he said, was a place offering
a spacious opening for evangelical enterprise. Cure
Cup was without priest or parson, and five weddings
and three christenings had accumulated, pending the
arrival of a certified clergyman. There had not been
a service at C'up-Cup in eight months. In fact, Cup-
Cup was a splendid stand for a parson seeking to
spread Christian knowledge and make an attractive
collection of threepenny-bits. The Rev, Walter
AVinterfleld needed no further persuasion; he whipped
up, and took the road to Cup-Cup. He had re-
ceived his call, he said. Cup-Cup welcomed the Rev.
Walter. He put up at Crater’s, and was attended
with infinite care and given the best of everything.
He performed four marriage ceremonies and three
christenings at fancy prices, and his service in Cole’s
Hall was so great a success that he followed it up
with two lectures, one for men only, the other for
women only, one shilling admission. Cup-Cup is
bitter about those lectures to this day. They were
a grievous disappointment. Then good Parson AVin-
terfield disappeared in a most mysterious manner.
His clerical suit was found on the banks of Crab
Creek, and Cup-Cup was thrown into a state of pro-
found emotion. For two days the adult male popu-
lation devoted itself to fishing for the missing shep-
herd in the waterholes along Crab Creek. Several
tradespeople who held the Rev. AValter’s cheques
betrayed a touching uneasiness. AA’alter had not
paid Crater at all. Neither had he paid for the
stabling of his horse. In fact, Mr. AVinterfleld had
been exceedingly remiss in making payments, but
had collected with commendable promptitude. On
the fourth day the cheques came back, each endorsed
very legibly in a firm, round hand, “No account.”
The third day brought the Rev. Ephraim Chill, a
small, timid, wrinkled clergyman of 30, who said
he had been recommended to the place by a tall
stranger with a blue wart on his chin. AA’interfielrl
was tall, and had a blue wart on his chin. “The
good fellow told me I should presently find a cry-
ing need for a minister of the Gospel at C'up-Cup,”
said little Mr. Chill. “He gave me this letter of
introduction to you as a prominent personage.” Chill
presented the letter to Crater. It ran: “Give North,
and Ambury, and Kettlewell, and Smith my compli-
ments, and urge them to get married again imme-
diately. I forward herewith a guaranteed parson.
—Yours sine., AV. AATnterfield.” So AValter AA’in-
terfield was no clergyman. Once more the four
couples made haste to the wedding. Cup-Cup was
out about £BO, and had bnly the horse and buggy
to recompense it. Subsequent disclosures enlight-
ened it on that point. The horse and trap, with a
bag of parsonical garments, had been stolen from the
front gate of an Anglican clergyman at Ballarat.
Furthermore, AValter AVinterfleld had sold the turn-
out to Hodgkin, of Tip-Top.
“Mowyle”: The star act of the great
comic opera, “Re<T Tape Tangles,” has just been sup-
plied to a South Australian town by the colored
comedian. For months past manless and bulldogless
households have spent the broiling nights in armored
garrisons, with the keyhole barricaded and the poker
under the pillow. The cause was “Peeping Tom,”
an elusive nocturnal marauder with a taste for bed-
room windows as a favorite means of entrance and
exit. Through a piece of woefully inartistic care-
lessness, which can only be ascribed to too much
civilisation, the peeper has at length been identified
as none other than the official black tracker of the
local police force. After conscientiously tracking
himself to some non-committal dead end, his sense
of security as a specialist proved his undoing. The
carefully lost trail was picked up later by the police
and followed back to departmental headquarters,
while a hat dropped at one of the elusive one’s ports
of call and boots retaining some of the country
traversed did the rest. The giddy jester has fled at
full speed on account of ill health, and will
reside in future at one of the native reserves where
windows are few and nobody seriously minds what
anybody else sees by night or by day.

at Mailu was almost as heavy again.
“Barnder”: “Pick” wants reliable infor-
mation regarding any latter-day performance of
black-trackers. Here is one experience. About three
years ago a blackboy, by name Jimmy Keating, was
murdered with an axe on Lily Creek, Cairns (N.Q.),
Suspicion fell on another nigger named Charlie Gor-
don, who had last been seen in his company, and was
missing the next day. The police called in the ser
vices of tracker Jacky Sullivan, a nigger who ha
been brought up amongst whites. J-cky tracked
Gordon across country for 47 miles to Mareeba, where
he was located hiding in a yard. When thq. police
rushed in one side he rushed out the other, an
again made for the bush. After another 20 miles o
tracking, they ran him to earth in a timber-hauler s
hut, where he had gone for some tea. I know other
instances; but that’s my contribution.
THE DINGOES DOWN THE CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It isn’t what you drank before you banged into your
bunk,
Though it may be that your midnight griefs in
shilling tea are sunk;
But when you wake and shudder with strange geese
upon your grave,
AVhen you hear the yelping chorus and the howling
starts to rave,
When within the black bush shadows all the tongues
of sorrow speak,
You will learn the hidden anguish of the dingoes
down the creek.
Did you leave your love in Melbourne with her sleep-
less pillow wet?
(By the look she wore at parting she’ll be grieving
for you yet),
Now you’ve blewed your blessed wages on the blisses
of Outback,
And you’ve got to write and tell her that vou'H have
to keep the track —
Oh, you'll think of her at midnight when you start
at every shriek,
For the dingoes prod like conscience when they’re
howling down the creek.
You may be as hard as anvils, with a tough and
calloused soul,
Blanketed and fully nourished, and prepared to
nicely roll
Down the quiet seas of slumber, broad and billowy
and deep,
Or your mellow pipe’s narcotic may have lulled your
crimes to sleep;
But your own familiar devil will display the yellow
streak
AA’hen the dingoes howl together like the lost along
the creek.

This is not the first time the Bar of N. S.
Wales has withheld its congratulations from
a new Judge. When the learned John Flet-
cher Hargrave was sworn in 1865, the Bar
as one man absented itself. Cheeke was
sworn on the same day, but the boycott was
against Hargrave only.
At the luncheon which followed the chris-
tening of the City of Drought, G.-G. Den-
man expressed regret that men who had fos-
tered Federal sentiment, like S’renry Parkes,

Died in Adelaide, suddenly, Alexander
Clark, who had taught nearly half of young
S. Aus. how to sing, more or less. He had
been training the young idea for 55 years—
first in a Great Western Railway school in
England, then at St. Andrew’s Cathedral
School in Sydney, and, finally, starting
nearly 40 years ago, under the S. Aus. De-
partment. He head-mastered some of the
biggest schools in the State; but his heart
was always with his singing and his soldier-
ing, and he trained tens of thousands of
youngsters for the public school indoor
‘displays” that are a feature of S. Aus-
tralian life.
The Oldest Inhabitant has his doubts:—
Mr. George Hamilton, who died at Ben Bullen
near Oapertee (N.S.W.), a few days ago, at the re-
puted age of 110 years and seven months, claimed
to be the son of a Colonel Hamilton, and to have
been born on board the Silver Eagle in Port Jack-
son, in 1802. The old man, who said he remem-
bered William Cox going to make the first road over
the Blue Mountains, asserted that his father was
Acting-Governor of N. S. Wales for six days. Un-
fortunately for Mr. Hamilton’s memory, the only
military doing duty in N. S. Wales from its founda-
tion till 1810 was the Rum Corps, and there was no
Colonel Hamilton or any officer of that name in the
Corps. Also, the records from the foundation of the
colony up to 1810 don’t mention any such ship as
the Silver Eagle having arrived in Port Jackson.
THE LOVERS.
Few men know more about commerce
statistics than Sir Alfred Bateman, a mem-
ber of the Imperial Royal Commission
lately gazing with astonishment at Maori-
land’s mountain of debt. For 50 years he
has been engaged in making them up as
ammunition for the big political guns of
England, and has done his best, from behind
the scenes, to make the reputation of 20
Presidents of the Board of Trade, including
Lloyd George. He tells a good story about
John Bright, a former President of the
Board. Bateman’s predecessor, as head of
the commercial dept., was requested to pre-
pare a report on a commercial treaty about
which there .was considerable difference of
opinion. Bright, who was not one of the
most studious occupants of the president’s
chair, read the report and attached this
memo.: “After reading Mr. ’s most ex-
cellent and well-reasoned minute twice, I
cannot make out whether he is in favor of
or against the proposed treaty; but his
arguments on both sides are so cogent that
in either case I concur.”
Harry Hawker (son of squatter George
Hawker, Speaker of Adelaide Assembly
from 1860 to 1565) who was killed while
‘hunting” in Hampshire (Eng.) about
Christmas time, left a tidy little £120,000
behind him in S. Aus. His father had a big
area of pastoral land, which was equally
divided among a large family. Old George
Hawker has the distinction of being about
the only legislator in S.A. whose vote was
ever influenced by a Parliamentary speech.
The late Tom Price, afterwards Premier,
pleaded fervently for the first Factories Bill,
and, to the surprise of everyone, George
Hawker, supposed to be a Conservative,
crossed the floor and voted for it. He said
afterwards that he could not have slept
peacefully that night had he opposed it after
hearing Price’s eloquent appeal. Rich
squatter as he was, Hawker also gave the
vote which enabled Kingston to carry his
progressive land-tax resolutions through the
Assembly, and he repeated the vote subse-
quently on the Bill, despite the frantic ex-
postulations of his brother squatters.

James Holland, M.A., for
many years senior inspector
of schools in Victoria, has
reached his 65th milepost,
and has consequently re-
tired. Joining the service
40 years ago, he knew all
about the hovels in which
learning was instilled into
the young Australian of the
’so’s and ’6o’s. His influ-
ence had some effect iu in-
augurating the re-building
of schools policy, which was
really begun in 1878. N<>w
he sees where mistakes were
made. “Most of the archi-
tects,” he said the other dav.
“were trained in Great
Britain. They were used
to the gloom of the English
climate. When they came
to Australia the first thing
that struck them was the glare. so they built
the new schools to keep out the light.” And
they succeeded, with dreadful results to the
ventilation also.

£lOOO a year, and the new one is worth only

F. M. B. Fisher, Maoriland’s Minister of
Customs, is due at Melbourne shortly to talk
reciprocity at the conference between repre-
sentatives of Australia, Canada and, of
course, M.L. He is the young man of the
Ministry, and has found little difficulty in
capturing the limelight from a dull platform
team. And he can stand it, even if it sets
fire to the premises. He is a strong Cold
lea-party man, as was his mentor, the late
tommy Taylor; also, he is a front-rank
tennis player. Sydney Daily Telegraph,
referring to him the other morning, said he
was “one of the handsomest men in M.L.’s
House of Representatives.” That may be
so too; but it was hardly worth mentioning.

of Pmton Aged 89 years. This Antill’s
father, who was in the 73rd Regiment, came

ong rmgn in the colony. When Macquarie

Henry Carey Dangar, M.L.C. and father of
the N. S. Wales Bar, now in his 83rd year
This Dangar is a native of Port Stephens
and belongs to one of the very few old
families who have retained their wealth The
original Dangar came out in 1822, and w a
an assistant-surveyor under Government Ho
settled at Neotsfield in 1832, and owned
also, the Myall Creek station, where the
dreadful massacre of black brethren hap-
pened. 1
The latest N.S.W. Law List shows that
there are 17 K.C.’s in practice in the Ma
State, and 137 counsel who don’t wear silk
The most ancient members of the Bar are
not working—Henry Carey Dangar ad-
mitted in 1854, and William Alexander
Long, admitted in 1862. The father of the
Bar, leaving out Messrs. Dangar and Long
is C. E. Pilcher, called in 1867. He does
not look for briefs now, either. The oldest
solicitor is A. C. Quaife, of Stanraore, who
was admitted in -April, 1862. His runner-
up is A. W. Castle, of Taree, admitted in
October, ’62. Then follow Henry Deane
(1863) and J. F. Fitzhardinge and Fred
Gannon (1864).
Making his first speech in N. S. Wales,
new Excellency Strickland recalled the
sending of the Soudan war-contingent as
the first visible out-pouring of the “Im-
perial” spirit in these parts. But while
Gov. Strickland knows that much, he prob-
ably doesn’t know that a namesake of his,
Sir Edward Strickland, a retired Army
officer, was behind W. B. Dailey in the
matter of sending that contingent. Strick-
land and Dailey were neighbors at Manly,
and this old soldier it was who grasped
that opportunity to light and feed the
Australian statesman’s fires of oratory over
this business.

“Senior Stripe” identifies the accused:—
Tommy O’Callaghan makes room for A. G. Sains-
bury as Victorian Commissioner of Police on April
1. The two most curious things about O’Callaghan
are his State uniform, which he designed himself,
and his close and serviceable friendship with Jack
Murray, the Chief Sec. O Callaghan recently con-
fided to a Melbourne paper that he was of German
extraction. Presumably he adopts Blucher as h:s
ideal. Blucher seems to suggest boots, and bools
suggest police. On the other hand, John Milne, the
inspecting superintendent, who steps into Sams
bury’s shoes, models himself, confessedly, on Napol-
eon, although facially he more closely resembles
Lord Charles Beresford. To call him “Nap” is the
sincerest form of flattery. It is only a fiction that
policemen are mainly irishmen. O’Callaghan is a
German whose name is probably a low-Dutch cor-
ruption of Ilohenzollern; Milne is an Englishman;
at least two of the Victorian force are Jews; am
Fitzroy rejoices in a constable with the fine old
Irish name of Mafferzoni. If his Christian prefix
were Spiridio, he could open da oyst without ex-
citing suspicion.
’ENLEY AND HEMPIRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Henley, M.L.A., one of the relators in the
action successfully brought against the N.S.W. State
Government to restrain it from using Government
House and grounds for anv other purpose than the
accommodation of the King’s representative, re-
marked that the unanimous decision would be very
popular throughout the British dominions. The pub-
lic, he said, must not misunderstand.... It does not
mean that the State Governor can oblige the Gov-
ernment by going into residence, etc. —Daily paver.
Be’old the ’ouse! ’Ere ’Enley’s glory stands,
Written in solid and hendurin’ stone;
A loyal hempire lifts applaudin’ ’ands
To tell ’ow Burwood buttressed Britain’s throne.
’Olman, who done the hunimperial deed,
Is shamed before the majesty of law,
And ’igh himportin’ people pause to ’eed
The loyal flush on ’Enley’s ’appy jaw.
The Gov’nor comes again to claim ’is right—
The Gov’nor-General, not the other kind —
’E knows ’ow stubborn ’ot we made the fight,
And 'Enley is a ’ero to ’is mind.
The people, as is right, will foot the bill.
Gor’save ’is Majesty! Our hanthems ring.
The flag of hempire floats above us still,
AVhile ’Enley keeps ’is eye upon the King.

Six months ago the directors announced that they
thought they were “justified in complying with the
repeated requests to increase the dividend to the rate
7 p.c. p.a.” Seeing that the visible reserves had
actually shrunk in the preceding" five years, although
a dividend of only 6 p.c. had been paid, there was
no apparent justification for the increase; and
already the reserves have to be bitten into to pay
the 7 p.c. Still, it is so easy for a shipping co. to
stuff away undisclosed profits that one is never safe
in declaring that profits were less than the dividend.
Here, for example, are some tell-tale figures re-
lating to this Newcastle Co.:—
June, Dec., . June, Dec.,
1909. 1911,. 1912. 1912.
Ship property .. £85,350 £62,401 £58,875 £56,551
Freeholds, etc. .. 3,036 1;684 1,484 1,700
Plant, etc. .. 5,446 4,407 3,462 3,414
Liquid assets .. 24,624 56,688 61,260 69,491
Note how the book-value of the ship-property has
shrivelled, while the liquid assets (bank deposits
mostly) have grown like Jonah’s pumpkin. Cer-
tainly the co. may have sold ships for cash, or col-
lected the insurance money on vessels that have been
lost, but the chances are that the bulk of the re-
duction in the ship-property item is due to writing-
down—in other words, profit (or, more strictly,
revenue) has been put aside ostensibly to provide for
renewals. Obviously, the visible profits can be made
to look great or small as the unspecified amount
dropped into that renewal fund is little or big.
On the record of the co. this paper is inclined to
think that the provision for < repairs and renewals
and replacements is very ample, and that, probably
for prudential reasons, the management under- rather
than over-states the true profit. Latterly the co. has
been having more wharf-side troubles, and its ex-
penses have gone up, while apparently trade hasn’t
increased. And as those expenses are likely to keep
up, even when trade slackens, the wisdom of the in-
creased dividend still looks doubtful, even allowing
that the co.’s position is better than it is made to
look on the official paper.

Considering that “tin.? season has been the worst
experienced for many years” (vide directors’ re-
port), the profit shown is extraordinarily good. But
in a, business of this sort you can’t go much on one
year s figures. The usual practice is to take in
stock at a very low valuation, and, when beasts are
sold, to show the net surplus realised above that
valuation as profit. Also, of course, the net revenue
!T om , the , wool-clip is profit. Last year, though
7 !!,, p were shorn » the lambs marked totalled
only 4835. On the other hand, in view of the bad
season and the high prices ruling most of the time
for stock, sales were fairly large. Further, the
wool market has been very good. These things ac-
count for the satisfactory profit shown. Future
figures will, however, be affected by the poor lamb-
ing—you can t shear a sheep that has declined to
be born or to live after it has been born. And even
for the past year the profits wouldn’t have shown
up anything like so well if an average amount had
been written off improvements. This is the record
in that matter for some years past:—

The co. was formed to buy Bundaleer and Thurul-
goona, over the Queensland border, in the Far West.
It paid far too much for them, and it had many
dull years in which to whip the cat; but it has
succeeded in getting its once-big debt down to prac-
tically nothing. In 1899, after a few good years, it
was struck by a bad drought, and showed a net
loss of £36,092. At that time it had a mortgage
of £60,000 and other liabilities amounting to
£3902. Altogether it owed on December 31 last
only £lOO7, and it had about £25,000’0f liquid
assets, in addition to stock (£55,582). The change
in the position is excellent; but it has only been
brought about by skimping dividends. Moreover,
there is liable to be an end to the earning capacity
of some of the capital. “No provision has been
made for the wastage of the leases, which now have
22 years to run for an area of about 1050 square
miles. Station and improvements (not counting in
stock) represent £216,812 of the total capital of
£292,005. Probably at the end of the 22 years
the co. will still find most of the area available;
but there’s a risk about it. Meantime the selector
is liable to drop in, and though he pays for improve-
ments he doesn’t usually pay anything like the cost
price. However, much profit can be made at th’s
business in 22 years, given favorable conditions. The
trouble in this case is the excessive capitalisation,
the result, of course, of the over-valuation of the
back countrv when the co. was formed (1882). A
similar holding now would be considered dear at half
or even one-third the money. The co. is in 97,335
shares of £3 10s. paid to £3, and valued in the
market at about 265. There are nearly enough liquid
assets and stock (at valuation) to represent that
amount.

This bank has kept on a fairly even keel so far as
profits are concerned since its last issue of shares in
1907. The average well-conducted bank has a strong
objection to showing erratic profits. Such results
suggest that the concern is carrying on a more or
less speculative business; and the public naturally
doesn’t feel happy in leaving its deposits with a bank
that speculates. The basis of the modern banking
business is credit. It wants to be trusted. It, there-
fore, never wants to even hint that it has made a
heavy loss—a thing which would, of course, suggest
the possibility of further heavy losses. Hence the
practice of accumulating what are called internal
reserves. A bank can have those reserves in all sorts
of ways. For instance, supposing it made £lO,OOO
more than it had been showing recently, and sup-
posing it had £lOO,OOO worth of Government stock,
it could regard that £lOO,OOO as worth only
£90,000. By doing that it would wipe out that
extra £lO,OOO of profit. But the juggle wouldn’t
affect the market value of the Government stock a
cent. Presently the bank might have to meet some
heavy losses. If it chose it could then sell the
Government stock and get the full £lOO,OOO for it;
in other words, it would make a profit of £lO,OOO,
which profit could be set against the losses; and so
the losses would never be disclosed any more than
the abnormal profit was. It is a system of equalis-
ing disclosed profits—a system practically forced on
banks because people are inclined to take alarm if
a bank which showed a profit of £50,000 this year
showed one of only £30,000 next year, as it might
very easily do without having sustained any really
serious trouble.
In theory a bank should never make losses. It
is supposed to always have securities to cover its
advances, etc. But in practice things work out
differently, and one never knows when its contin-
gency fund may be called for. Some years ago this
Commercial Bank of Tasmania, wanting to make
its reserve fund extra safe, decided to go into
what was regarded in Right Thinking circles as
absolutely the most gilt-edged security in the Em-
P ire -British Consols. For various reasons those
Consols have, however, shrunk in value, and paper
worth £lOO face value is now saleable at only £73
7s. 6d. Now, if a Government undertook to pay par
for its paper at some time or other, a co. might
possibly be justified in keeping the stock in its
balance-sheet at par. But Consols are perpetual:
that is, the Com. of Tasmania is not entitled to at
“yf l ™ 5? *0 the British Treasury and demand
£lOO for the £lOO bond. All it can do is to go
into the market and sell. For that reason the
market price is the only figure at which a bank is
entitled to keep such securities in its balance-sheet.
I his has meant that, as Consols have shrunk, the
value has had to be written down out of profits.
Jjie following shows what has happened of late
(the face value of this bank’s holding is £160,000):
February, February, August, February
1911. 1912. 1912 1913
Gross profit .. £37,480 £32,361 £37,335 £4O “>6S
Less: ’

Ihe £BOOO struck off profits this half-year brings
the stock down to £72 p.c. of its face value; and
as the present price is £73 7s. 6d., provision has
been made for some possible future shrinkage.
Meantime, though the stock is worth less in the
market than it used to be, it produces as much in-
terest as ever it did ; so that, so long as the bank
doesn t want to sell the stock, it is just as well off
as ever it was; and the profit which has been used
to write down the book-value of the stock is also
earning money in some other direction. In this
way the bank has been strengthening its position
and its dividend prospects.

A pretty rapid growth of advances is disclosed
there —and the chances are that it is not very cheap
money that is going out now. When the principal
State of the Commonwealth is desperately paying
the equivalent of about £4 7s. p.c. p.a. on a 10-year
loan, the public can’t expect cheap money. Booms
are fine things while we’re drinking the champagne-
but there is more than one sting in their tail; and
when the bank s customer has to pay an extra 1 or
2 p.c. for accommodation, he will know who to
thank for it. More than all others, James McGowen
anc * jfl 18 .,, crow d of pseudo-Labor boomsters and
spendthrifts in N. S. Wales. And there is a limit
rm-. h® amount a bank can let out at any price
This bank now holds only 32 p.c. of the public’s
money (deposits, etc.) in liquid form; and it won’t
be safe to make advances and thus reduce it much
below that point. In fact, even 32 p.c. is generally
reckoned a bit over the safety line.

The co. has had a record half-year, so the directors
invite shareholders to come along and have a “jubi-
lee bonus, this being the tenth vear of the co’s
operations.” The Bulletin hasn’t heard that the
shareholders scorned the invitation. There is this
to be said for the directors: they are under no de-
lusions with regard to the maintenance of these
boom profits. They know that the State’s wild bor-
rowing must, and will, end presently; very likely
they know that if it doesn’t end before it will end
simultaneously with a bad season and a slump in
production. When that transpires, things threaten
to be extremely bad, because of the excessive in-
terest which the people will have to pay on the
recently-borrowed money. There is thus every
likelihood of a very bad time ahead in the construc-
tion trades; and this co. is going to suffer worse
than most from that trouble when it arrives. There
will be a gradual revival afterwards, of course ; but
normal conditions, when they return, won’t, so far
as this co. is concerned, be anything like the boom
conditions that have prevailed lately. The man
who thinks of buying these shares on the strength
of the £7OBB profit made in the past six months
had better, therefore, cool his head with the icy fact
that in the corresponding half of 1905, after the
bad times of 1903-4, profits sank to £612 and the
dividend to nil. Admittedly the financial position
has been very much improved since then. Here are
some figures:—
Dec., 1905. Dec., 1912.
Liquid assets .. ..£2986 £17,221
Liabilities 7814 1,710
Deficit .. .. £4828
Surplus .. .. £15,511
That means that the co. is anything from £BOO to
£lOOO a year better off now than it was then—
provided that the £11,004 now represented by book
debts, and included with liquid assets, is good
money. On the other hand, over £lO,OOO of the ac-
cumulated profit has been transferred to capital, and
some new shares have been issued, so that it takes
a trifle more to pay a 5 or 10 p.c. div. than it used to.
The transfers of reserves really amount to the restora-
tion of capital, because previously when the State
got into the backwash of one of its periodic booms,
ss. in the £ of the capital had to be written off
as having dropped through a hole somewhere and dis-
appeared. This alone gives a hint as to the char-
acter of the co.’s business.

Bananaland is taking a long while to find out the
benefits of co-operation. This is the co.’s 34th
birthday, and yet it isn’t the flourishing concern
it should be —not by miles. The capital is very
small —only £5OlB. Possibly if the co. got in a
little additional money it could open out more
stock and make a more attractive showing. Also,
it might be able to escape from its overdraft —not
a large one (£1257 net), but still too much for a
concern which ought to be doing nothing but cash
trading. Unfortunately, the co.’s customers haven’t
learned the benefits of cash trading yet. Of
£22,942 of sales last half-year, £17,539 were made
on credit. That, of course, means that the co. can’t
buy for cash either, and thus the customers and
shareholders rob themselves of the benefit of trade
discounts.
