The copper boom is mainly responsible for the long
jump in profits and dividends. Standard metal
averaged £73 Is. 3d. last year, compared with
£56 Is. 9d. in 1911. That is to say, the improve-
ment was £l7 per ton—or so near to £l7 that
the difference doesn’t matter. The fine copper in
ore and tailings was 6251 tons, and £l7 per ton on
6251 tons amounts to £106,267. If, therefore, the
co. had merely made the profit resulting from the
advance in the metal, there should have been
£106,267 to divide. And as it didn’t'* make
£106,267, it follows that the cost of production
last year was greater than the average market price
was in 1911. On the co.’s own showing this is how
costs have worked out: —
1907 .. £73 17 9 I 1910 .. £54 11 2
1908 .. 54 16 8 1911 .. 51 12 5
1909 . . 53 3' 11 I 1912 . . 56 18 9
That, of course, is per ton of copper.
Two points of importance have, therefore, to be
considered: (1) What is the price of copper likely
to be in the future? (2) What are the prospects
for a reduction in the cost rate? As to the price,
copper is a most erratic commodity; but in the
past 10 years it has never been down below
£52 15s. The average of the lowest points touched
each year for the past 10 years is £5B 11s. It
seems to be a very safe thing to assume that the
average price for the next 10 years won’t be lower
than the average of the lowest points touched in the
past 10 years, the tendency of prices in general
being upward. To figure on £6O seems safe.
To figure on £65 seems not very risky
Nothing even so definite as that can be
said on the subject of costs. In metallurgy the
greatest advances are being made in the utilisation
of what were formerly waste products. For a
good many years past this co. has been reaping a
crop of copper from the old tailings dumps by the.
cementation process. The co. doesn’t now mention
the cost of recovering metal by this very cheap
method; but in 1909 the costs, including smelting
charges and heavy depreciation on plant, amounted
to a few pence over £45 per ton. And it had
been lower than . that. The slimes are now being
attacked. But suitable stored by-pToducts, the accu-
mulation of 52 years or so, will not last much
longer, for the Wallaroo residues are not adapted to
the cementation process. Excellent results are how-
ever, said to have been got of late from the flotation
(Minerals Separation) process. The directors don’t
whoop about the matter, but general-manager Han-
cock says: “During the latter half of the year a
little over 200 tons of copper was obtained from
the mundic and tailings dumps, and the residues
from the plant represented about 0.3 p.c. copper ”
The plant is being extended, and there is very
little doubt that a lot of reliance is to be put on
this process in the future. This doesn’t apply to
tailings only, but to slimes and crude ore as well
An entire re-organisation of processes seems indeed
likely in the future. And there is room for it
Apart from the very heavy loss of metal by the old
concentrating process (at Moonta almost exactly
25 p.c. of the metal was non-recovered last year),
the costs are very severe, especially i
remembered that the average valie nf A® il i»
very nearly 4 p.c. Of course thin*. 0 *- the ore is
are getting no better. How neat the ™ the wi «e

* * *
The Melbourne-owned Bourke’s Hill, Em-
maville (N.S.W.), has paid another 6d div
a further tribute to the policy of reorganisation
entered upon some months ago. Prior to that the
show was a very lame duck, partly owing to in-
sufficient water. A more certain supply has been
got from the Severn River. The plant also had to
be thoroughly overhauled and removed. Moreover
some ground which the co. was sluicing on a
royalty basis was advantageously' bought outright
These alterations necessitated a couple of calls, but
the_ shareholders should now have ahead of them a
period of fair profits.
B. H. Junction North is the fantod*of the
sharemarket. Two years ago (April, 1911) lead was
£l3 and shares 16s. In December, 1911, lead had
risen to £l5 11s. 3d. and shares to 255. This
spurt was helped by the improper ls. dividend (De-
cember 21, 1911). For 1912 lead averaged £l7
15s. 104 d., and yet the Junction North had to re-
construct and make calls. Shares next took a neck-
brealong drop below Bs. ; but lead boomed to £23
and they came up again to 255., with no very defi-
nite prospect of a dividend. Since then there ha«
been a slump to 10s. The mill is asserted to have
achieved a metallurgical success in the treatment of
slimes, but the extraction from all products milled
is under 8 p.c. lead and soz. silver, and costs are
heavy. The return for February (four weeks) was
2279 tons of concentrates (340z.' silver, 56 p.c. lead,
14 p.c. zinc), so gross metal contents figure 1276
tons lead and 77,4800 z. silver. Two grades of con-
centrates are, however, made, and it is the price that
can be got for the goods that counts. The latest
hint is that the fine slimy concentrates are finding a
market; and if that is so, and lead keeps up, the
market may recover a bit. For a nerson who wants
to enjoy peace of mind, Junction North scrip is, in
spite of that prospect, a poor counter to gamble
with.

P. G. Morgan, Government Geologist, has
driven several spikes of hard, cold fact into the
comfortable belief held by Maorilanders that the
shaky Isles contain such unlimited supplies of coal,
iron and oil that they arc destined to become the
great manufacturing centre of the Pacific. This has
been one of the baits held out to Cohen when fresh
loans were required. It has been harped on per-
sistently by the press and politicians of the type of
the one who, when giving evidence before the Em-
-111 . Trade Commission, referred to the countless
millions of tons of coal yet to be discovered. He
was asked, “Are you a geologist?” “No,” was the
reply; ‘but lam a man of common-sense and have
been about a bit.” According to Morgan, however,
after allowing for waste, etc., there is only sufficient
coal for 100 years, even at the present rate of con-
sumption and increase of population. As to iron,
there are 22 million tons of ore. Taranaki iron-sand
contains titanium, and is difficult to smelt; besides,
] t is scattered along miles of coast-line. Regarding
oil, it is surprising to learn that a refinery, with a
1 y ca P acl ty. of 10,000 gallons, is being erected,
though the daily production of the wells is so far
only 689 gallons. A Job’s comforter is Morgan in
these straitened times.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. 10d.- — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2

* * * *
Last year Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) treated
155,603 tons for £248,083. Local expenditure
amounted to £185,000 or thereabouts, and on top of
this has to be reckoned depreciation, London office
exes., and other charges at present unknown. The
dividend distributions for the year aggregated
£69,062 or a few thousands more than the gross
working profit, and a good many thousands more than
the actual net surplus is likely to total. The main
shaft has been deepened to 2652 ft. on the underlay,
and the big winding engine referred to in back num-
bers of this paper is nearing completion.
* * * *
The cheeky Gwalia Central (W.A.) has
lately been puffing its prospects, and inferentiallv
leading London to believe that it had something
in common with the Sons of Gwalia and most of the
remaining riches on the Leonora field. Inquiries
since made by the Agent-General and others from
the Mines Department have revealed (1) that the
co.’s belongings comprise two more or less worn-out
leases known as the Trump and Trump Extended,
situated on the other side of Leonora township, and
about two miles away; and (2) that nothing is
known locally of the rich finds that are being
placed to the property’s credit in London. About
18,000 oz. have been taken out of the Trumps during
the last 12 or 14 years, but only 2090 z. represents
last year’s output. They are not, in their present
state of decrepitude, the class of venture that a
sensible co. would care to bother about.
* * * *
“Bar None” : Have seen Bullfinch’s official
plans. They show a fair-sized patch of sweetening
ore in the old boom workings ; also a larger tonnage
of milling-grade reserves. Values are, however, mis
erably poor at No. 3 level in and around the locality
where a continuation of the rich stuff was looked
for; but, of course, the unexpected may happen and
another enrichment be met with in deeper ground.
The Northern series is a mine in itself. At the No.
1 level official plans and assays show the shoot to
have a length of 450 ft. and an average for 21ft. of
about 13dwt. stuff for 400 ft., and a look underground
at that point proves beyond doubt that the ore is
there. Judging by the development already done,
length, width and values will continue to 200 ft. At
300 ft. the first 60-odd feet of driving has averaged
655. 6d., but it is a moot point whether the lode
• that is being opened up at this depth is the main
make or a branch. Mill supplies are at present being
drawn from five different sources, and unless the co.
has made a bloomer in its assay values a return of
£3 per ton on present reserves should easily be
maintained for two years or more. The plant is an
eve-opener as regards efficiency and economy, and re-
flects credit on its designers and builders.
* * * *
Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) is more rick-
eftv than ever. Profits are becoming harder and
iiar.ler to get, and, judging by underground appear-
ances, a resumption of dividends is very unsure.
What seems to puzzle supporters of the stock most
of all i< that, although work was to have been com-
menced two months ago on the rich make cut just
inside the boundary at 2480 ft. by a crosscut from the
Boulder workings, nothing has been heard about it
since, and the market has weakened to 355. The
Bulletin said at the time that this enrichment was
part of one first met with in the Boulder at 1900 ft.,
and followed down in that property almost to the
point where it passed into the Horseshoe. It is rich
and wide in the Boulder, but has a length of only
from 30ft. to 40ft. Then values dwindle consider-
ably. No doubt they have done so also in the Horse-
shoe. Hence the official silence.

The following is believed to be a true
and faithful statement of Edna May’s position. Cr.:
20,000 tons of actual and probable ore in sight at
(say) £4 per ton = £BO,OOO. Dr.: Mining, milling
and extraction. £20,000; plant equipment and sun-
dries, £12,500 ; total, £82,500. Estimated sur-
plus, £47,500; or, approximately, £1 per share.
Then there is speculative value to be taken into ac-
count ; but the sick condition of the neighboring
Greenfinch is considered to discount this considerably.
The co. is,, however, putting down a new shaft to
prove the reef at 150 ft., and this work should throw
a good deal of light on the matter.
Meekatharra (W.A.) produced a 14dwt.
average (44,5040 z.) in 1912, and, bar accidents,
it will do better fit ill this year. As in previous
vears its chief means of support in 1912 were
Fenian, Ingliston Consols, and Ingliston Extended.
The first-named has now yielded between 80,000 oz.
and 90,0000 z. and paid >161.165 in divs. The
other two have also turned out a lot of gold, but
only the Ingliston Consols has given shareholders
anything, all the profits earned by the Extended
being put back into the mine or laid out in equip-
ment. The adjacent Queen of the Hills, with its
estimated £lOO,OOO profit in sight, has its first
clean-up this month. The co. is very sanguine,
but there are unkind persons who say that the gold
in the mine is less than the coin expended in
making preparations to take it out.
Greenfinch, at Westons (W.A.), is having
a drowning-man sort of struggle. Prospects have
been anything but rosy for some time, and, judging
by the bottom, the end isn’t far off. As a last
resource some boring may be done in the eastern
end to prosuect the ground in the direction of
Duff’s and the Edna May leases.
Ida H. (W.A.) asserts that the rich shoot
is continuing at 1070 ft. and trending towards the pro-
posed extension of the incline shaft. This is de-
clared to be a most encouraging feature. The shoot
has, it adds, now been proved to go 101 ft. and to
average 3oz. Bdwt. for a width of 19 inches. The in-
formation is also imparted that as this is the longest
shoot of this grade known in t’’o history of the mine
the Board has decided to sink the incline shaft a
further 100 ft. in order to “assist rapid develop-
ment, ensure good ventilation, and enable the ore to
he more economically extracted.” Good ; but past
experience hasn’t had a habit of confirming esti-
mates made about this show; so caution is advis-
able.
Lionel Robinson.

Shaft Sinkers, Ltd., of Victoria, is out
tor more cash. This co. started out with £5OOO
to demonstrate at the Great Poseidon lease, Tarna-
gulla, that it could successfully use the freezing
method for drifty ground on deep leads. So far
trouble has hung to the Shaft Sinkers, Ltd., which
couldn’t pick up the stuff in- Australia it wanted
quickly to freeze the ground. Time hasn’t, how-
ever, been altogether wasted. The co. has hitched
on to its venture seven miles of the Madame Hop- ,
kins lead, three miles of the Working Miners-
lead, and eight miles of the Ascot lead,
thus freezing up a vast block of mining
land. Shaft Sinkers not only does this, but produces
facts about the Working Miners and other leads
which invite one Alfred A. Billson, Minister of
Mines, to inquire why such areas shouldn’t be
manned. The reply may be that the Shaft Sinkers
proposes to pop its little lot of assets in London
for £lOO,OOO in cash and £lOO,OOO in shares.
But whether Bull will come up to the scratch de-
pends on whether he has forgotten the money he
dropped under Bewick, Moreing and Co. at Moo-
lort, Rutherglen and Ararat. Meanwhile, the tale
is told that J. Bull has suggested to Shaft Sinkers
to form a co. of a million, with a fifth interest to
Shaft Sinkers. To evolve £200,000 out of £20,000
in this way would be a snap that would appeal to
Jack Baillieu, now that he has screwed up the Cocks
Pioneer coffin.
“Jimmy” Mackay bad better keep a close
eye on Poseidon Alluvial. It isn’t in accordance
with sober Scotch ways for a stock to be up at
18s. and down 25 p.c. two days later. There seems
to be the suspicion that a leakage exists some-
where. If that is not so, then Poseidon Alluvial is
a stock to beware of. The novice in deep-lead
work doesn’t know that the clean-up in the sluice
boxes is made daily, but it is a fact that ought never
to be lost sight of when a man is trying to measure
up the strength of the market.
* * * *
Woah Hawp Canton, Ballarat, is moving
more spryly at 1200 ft. than of old. There driving
is being hustled up in the black slates to the south,
as with the Prince Regent and Dalzell leases to
draw upon the co. has plenty of scope for pro-
gressive work in that direction. West, beyond the
big slide, there is also the chance that payable
quartz may be got; while at 850 ft. the western
indicator is to be picked up with the object of
finding Tinworth’s shoot of gold to the south. A
hit here would galvanise a section of Ballarat that
is as inert as the Labor party over Protection.
* * * *
Those who gave up Confidence Extended,
Bendigo, as dead, rubbed their glasses when they
saw a block of shares swallowed by a cute Beehive
broker, who is credited with a liking only for safe-
snaps. His present preference has reason in it.
First of all there is the chance of butting into good’
stone at 2500 ft., such as has put Windmill Hill in
such good heart. Next is the hope of picking up-
some good dirt adjacent to the Oollman fault, both
at depth and in the upper levels. Surveyor Cundy
is to help in unravelling these problems, and Bendigo,
likes Cundy.
When Burnt Creek, Maryborough (Vic.),
started to pay dividends three months ago, The:
Bulletin wasn’t very cheerful —-it doubted their-
continuance. Now the news goes round that the-
show may close down. Reasons given are high:
cost and low efficiency of the labor available. T'oi
these may be added a third and greater. Burn*
Creek never did more than flatter its followers-
Working first on the Bet Bet lead, it only won®
out its pumps. When it got on the Duke lead Sth
found floods of water, some puggv wash and am-
uneven bottom to the gutter. Conditions like tFwse
stick up the best mine unless the gold is plentiful!,
and Burnt Creek never could set up as a record-
breaker in that respect.
* * * *
Ajax Central, Daylesford (Vic.), on a
capital of £12,000, has already parted up £6ooo'
in divs. and is still going. The Murrav-Rain
crowd is believed to be in possession—which means:
that no idea is given, any more than in the Ajax
or the Nuggettv, of the extent of the mine reserves:
and their value. The mine has developed better
than the touts expected. Work to the west at
541 ft. is just now the programme, and if the
shoot lives there and opens up to the average of
the stone broken at No. 5. a few more will cer-
tainly Z added 6 to the list of divs., for at No. 5
there has been 110 ft. of stone Bft. wide north of
the shaft, all real stingo. Overhead the reef has
been proved to split, but about an equal quantity
of work down south of the shaft has helped up
reserves. Still, even if there had been no pinching
of the reef at this point, and even when other shoots
are throw into the scale, a very liberal valuation
has been given to the mine by those who back it in
the market at its present price.

Apropos the Minnie Moxham (N.Q. l,
complaint was made (B. 27/2/’l3) that, though
the mine was worth the promised new trial, the
promoters and vendors were opening their mouths
too wide. C. A, Jenkinson, the secretary, now
assures The Bulletin that the £3OOO cash which
the syndicate is taking “is being put back by the
vendors into the co.” That’s better.
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, notices
an improvement in No. 5 level. Recent crushings
have averaged below £5 per ton, which comparts
poorly with last year’s 3899 tons for £24,902. The
tonnage put through is, however, too limited to give
any sound opinion of the value of the mine. Three
important points to watch are the extension of No. 5
level, deeper development to No. 6, and lateral
driving off No. 4 east level in search of the hang-
ing-wall section of the reef below the old workings. The
deepest winze from the latter was showing a good
make of stone when work was suspended; in fact,
this prospect caused the directors to drive No. 4.
Whether the Day Dawn lives below 2600 ft. cannot
be told : but the Brilliant Block is sinking on a for-
mation below 2000 ft. vertical, and that formation
is supposed to be the Day Dawn.
One of the old water-jacket smelters has
been removed from Mt. Garnet to the Mammoth
copper mine at Klondvke (Q.). If Garnet were
not a freehold, the chances are that a diamond
drill would be put through the bed of zinc
that cut the copper out. It seems a pity that a
mine that gave such rich returns from the surface
should be allowed to go without another trial—by
boring, anyway.
The directors of Mount Molloy (Q.) have
notified shareholders of the failure of the re-con-
struction scheme. It was the Balkan war and the
Labor party that did it, it seems. Rotten
management in the past had nothing to do with
making a re-construction necessary, of course. It
never has.

The hand of Herman is in the new flutter
over the Mt. Balfour Copper Mines (Tas.). At first
it would seem as if the £3OOO spent in sending
Hector Macdonald and Langford to London had gone ;
hut this is not true, because Macdonald’s Tasmanian
Railway and General Trust kept things going to the
end of last year before it threw up the snonge. That
■crowd’s complaint all through was that Australia
didn’t back up Bull. If they knew what the average
Australian bull thought of the Balfour copper field,
they would not have wondered at it. Anyway, the
Trust still holds that the agricultural and forest
land along the line will bring in revenue to the co.’s
railway : so it has reserved the right to take uo half
the shares in the New Mt. Balfour should Herman’s
Australasian Guarantee Corporation, Ltd., decide to
father the float. To date Mt. Balfour has had spent
on it £38,309, including railway, £16,512;
■mining, £6482 ; surveys, £3218. Then £3300
has been lodged with the State authorities, and pre-
sumably the other £9OOO has gone in “missionary”
■expenses. The Australasian Guarantee Corporation’s
■obligations to the Mt. Balfour, should it form a co.,
Is to put up £200,000 for general purposes, to give
the Balfour folk £160,000 in shares (or half de-
bentures) in the new co., and to pay £2500 cash,
presumably as refund of a deposit to the Government.
A trifle of £9OOO is required to complete the first
section of the co.’s line (11 miles). It looks as
though it will cost £lOO.OOO for 40 miles. Then
there must be power works if electricity is to be
used, so that a big hole will be made in the
£200,000 if all Mertz’s ideas are carried out.
As The Bulletin expected, there is
mothing in the rumor that General-Manager Bell-
(enger, of Great Cobar, won’t return to Australia.
A cable comes saving that he will be back in Aus-
tralia next month, and will start at the mine again
in June, or thereabouts. Meanwhile, acting G.-M.
Danvers Ppyver is holding his end up very well.
But he is having his troubles, partly the results of
patched-up plant which is constantly going wrong
and holding up the output.
The demand for tin shows a regular increase, and
prices are likely to remain at a figure that will pay
any sound proposition first-rate returns. Tin mining
is a big industry at Stanthorpe (Q.), and a Brisbane-
Ipswich-Stanthorpe syndicate has secured the option
over a property that competent geologists report
very highly on. It is situated nine miles from Bal-
landean, on the Brisbane-Sydney line, and 14 miles
south of Stanthorpe. The mineral leases form one
block of 65 acres, and a prospectus has been issued
on behalf of the Bee Hive Tin Mining Co., Ltd.,
with a nominal share capital of £65,000, divided
into 65,000 shares of £1 each, of which 43,000
shares are available for application. Of these 7000
have been already arranged for, so that 36,000 are
now offered for subscription on the following terms.
2s. 6d. per share on application and 2s. 6d. per
share on allotment, with calls not to exceed 3d. per
share per month. Development work has been going
on steadily for some time. Mr. E. A. Derrick first
reported on the property. Then Mr. W. H. Rands,
A R S M., F.G.S., examined the find, and subsequently
Mr E. O Marks, 8.A., 8.E., Assistant Government
Geologist of Queensland, ran the rule over it. Their
reports are very favorable. For instance, Mr. Der-
rick says: “The recoverable black tin for the whole
300 yards of surface dirt round the contact should
be at least l i p.c.” Mr. Brands’s comment on this is;
“! am of opinion that Mr. Derrick’s figures are pro-
bably under the mark both as to quantity and as to
tin contents.” Mr. Marks’s report is even brighter
He took two lines of samples of the surface dirt at
chain intervals, above and below the contact. the
11 samples so obtained practically prove an area of
2) acres and average 0.74 p.c. metallic tin equal
to about 251 b. black tin per cubic yard. Wherever
cuts have been put in on this side the surface soil
has been over 3ft. in depth, so it is a reasonably
safe assumption that the 2) acres contain 12 000
yards of soil, carrying 110 tons metallic tan, worth
£24 000 gross. Mr. Marks says further: As the
soil elsewhere along the contact in the vicinity is
known to carry tin, there is no reason to presume
ithat the area sampled is by any means the total area
that will be found to carry payable tin A similar
area, adjoining the foregoing on the north side, has
since been sampled; it assays 2.57 p.c. metallic tin,
giving a result of £104,000 for the five acres. Sur-
face tin has been found on the south sl ' ]e sO ,
payable quantity, without consideration of the lode
matter at all. AH the geologists who have inspected
these lodes agree ®n the probable existence of true
lodes, apart from the contact lode : 31 tons of the
•contact ore were sent to Messrs. Lohmann and .
for treatment, and gave a return of £56 16s. per
ton gross, with tin at £2lB 10s. on day of samp
ting ” It is believed that the application and allot-
ment money will be sufficient for treating the
alluvial tin, and the returns from this should be
worth far more than the capital that will be re-
quired to be called up. Full prospectus and form of
application may be obtained from the broker Mr.
T PP A Bond, Brisbane, member of the Brisbane
Stock’ Exchange; or from the provisional secretary,
Mr W A Dvmock, Australian Bank of Commerce
Chambers, Tpswich (Q.). The Brisbane Courier of
March 8 contains an abridged prospectus as well.
It should be well worth the while of anvone wishing
to invest in a tin proposition to get full particulars
of this company.*
Hampden-Cloncurry (Q. ) is smelting
nearly equal quantities of ore from its two most
important mines. Last month Hampden supplied
1944 tons and the Duchess 2140 tons. The Salmon
(a limestone show) forwarded 216 tons, and 180
tons were purchased. The most important develop-
ment of late has been the improvement of the copper
values at 92ft. below the 550 ft. level in the
Duchess. A blank was met with in the sinking
of this winze, but it has been followed by a make
of 22 p.c. ore. The strike of pyritic ore at 412 ft.
in Hampden No. 1 shaft is of almost equal im-
portance. The evidence that dense sulphides live
below 400 ft. puts new heart into the enterprise.
The return for last month was 539 tons copper,
189 oz. gold and 50630 z. silver. Taking copper at
£6O, the value of the base metal was £32,340.
If half of that is profit the co. will be doing very
well.

A great mistake, when erecting new works'or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleiohert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Sydney, March 24, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The Stricklands have put off their garden
party this week. The brutal and entirely
useless slaughter of King George of Greece,
who seemed to take an unusually intelligent
interest in his job, is the cause. King George
was the Dowager Alexandra’s brother, so
his tragic death puts a black edge on vice-
regal junketing for a week or two. Nothing
more frivolous than the laying of an occa-
sional foundation-stone is likely to be toler-
ated.
The Easter Show developed no symp-
toms of the dry winter in this State.
Governor Strickland landed a neat
parcel of descriptive matter on the
little patch of Axminster at the offi-
cial opening. “An exhibition unrivalled in
this continent and famous throughout the
world,” said he. Mild applause from the
front row of the crowd, which was almost
asphyxiated by the heat, having steadily
broiled in the sun for an hour or two before
the vice-regal foot trod the pure-merino
sheep-pen in the stand. (While the Show
committee is spending money in excelsis on
various new buildings, etc., it has overlooked
the shabby shed, not half large enough for
present requirements, which is ironically
called a “grandstand.”)
Miss Strickland, a tall girl with corn-
colored hair and eyes of Wedgwood blue, re-
ceived the homage of the agricultural ladies
who were herded with the political petti-
coats.
Fate destines Speaker Willis to be chcek-
by-jowl with Mr. Francis Suttor, President
of the Council. They get neck and neck at
official gatherings, and no one could say who
was an eyelash in front of the other when
Governor Strickland was opening the big
cattle parade. Mrs. Trefffi, the show lady
in the present team of State Cabinet Minis-
ters’ wives, wiped the floor with the personal
appearance of any other political dame pre-
. sent on Wednesday. Old rose, in some soft
shimmering drapery top-dressed with black
hat, did the damage.
A few freaks fluttered about the Rand-
wick lawn on the first day of the Easter
races, _ but they came only as spies rather
than in battalions. Summer heat downed
the desire of the fat to get into grotesque
autumn garb, and the fashionable lean wore
their gossamer garments and mighty little
else, as has been their custom for some
months.
A new female hat may be mentioned with
some fear and trembling, though the boy in
the.street will receive it with shrieks of joy.
It is a small black silk tile, rather low and
flat in its roof. Thus far, it is distinctly of
the male species, and appears to have been
built for the head of an A.D.C. But a Bird-
of-Paradise perched on its narrow brim, a
little to one side, makes it a sexless brand
.of millinery. Worn at the races by a lady with
*a brawny figure and a sunset complexion,
■one of these man-woman hats attracted
frantic attention, till two more of the same
species loomed on the lawn. Each dame who
:got round under one of these autumnal shocks
was blessed with a jaw that naturally placed
Iher above the weak fear of criticism.
The short coat which is cut away in front
;and rounded off into a neat little sparrow-
ltail behind was present in legions. A vivid
Iblue ninon of this cult, worn over a cream
«kirt laid out in broad blue stripes, sounds
{aggressive, but was It, and the girl inside
i-a-a aw r ay from competition.
Stamped velvet of the kind we used to
sma%e into book-markers, in the days of short
muds and giggles, is one of the newest forms
2. f fethato vanity. The tall figure of Mrs.
3. S. Brunton was well groomed in a pow-
(der-blue jacket of this pile, worn over a skirt
in some silky . drapery. Mrs. Lebby Hor-
<dern, in floating white garments fine as
•gossamer silk, out-feathered some of the best-
tdressed plumage on the lawn. Pretty Etta
Norris, who wears periwinkle blue as a regu-
lar habit, had a small fur tail twined round
'her Easter race hat. A deadly varietv of
yellowish-green, defined by untutored Man
:as the color of the worst bilious attack he
-ever encountered, crept into the frocks of
some of the. expensively clad. Mrs. Mick
Dalton, a nice-looking young matron with
"2 n ivory complexion and the willow-wand
figure of a French doll, appeared in one of
these wicked jaundice-green gowns.
The Philip Morton girls have brought
back from Europe lots of the latest style
m quiet, dead-leaf tones, a mile or two
away from the shrieks they affected in early
and precocious flapperhood. Jean Morton
has grown, up into a very chic young
maiden, with a handsome pair of dark
brown velvet eyes. At one race-week orgy
of clothes, she wore the best-cut coat and
skirt exhibited by any young person in her
vicinity. It was built of one of those
new-tinted cloths that look like the sands
of Sahara when the sun sulks behind the
horizon.
The country sent us stacks of hearty, be-
•XTTuf-11 acs f°, r .fhe riot. McKinnons,
McPhillamvs and McMasters are magnifi-
£?« t ™- advertlse “ ents for the stability of
.N.S.W. pastoral pursuits, and the ‘solid
mash acquired by our suffering squattocracy
Women riders at the Show were a smart
and workmanlike lot. Mrs. Stace was once
more champion horsewoman, and confided
that she doesn’t like the ride-astride so
well as the old horned side-saddle. A small
girl, in a long white coat, sliced up behind,
and short divided skirts, rode her pony
astride round the Show ring like a
bird. Miss Dolly Fogg, who sits like wax
in her saddle, snatched the equestrienne
prize, with Miss E. Courtney, a dashing
second, hard at her horse’s heels.
Mrs. Walker, a Melbourne visitor, is the
large and cheerful-looking aunt of Mayoress
Cocks. Auntie Walker belongs to the genial,
sweet-natured type of elderly matron, ad-
dicted to flowered muslins of big patterns
and ample dimensions; and she looks a
credit to her daily bread. Slightly old-
fashioned beside the slim silhouettes of
1913, Mrs. Walker, all smiles, surrounded
the Easter bun like a halo or a benediction.
Lord Dick Nevill is over here for the
races. He slipped his gorgeous chamber-
lain’s collar, at present inky-black for the
untimely end of poor George in far off
Salonika, and crept unofficially into this
port. . On Saturday his extreme length,
clad in a meek sac suit, stalked across the
Randwick lawn, pursued by the buzz of a
swarm of friends.
There will have been private hops in the
I addmgton Town Hall and under the domes-
tic shingle at Darling Point before this
meets you. The Frank Davenports’ dance
this Tuesday brings out a flock of debutantes,
including the daughter of the house. Mrs.
I rank is one of our nicest-looking matrons,
who cheerfully cultivates the art of looking
as young as she feels—which is 30 or less.
The Mutton Matrons and other members
of the country nobility are giving the most
extensive hop of a tame Easter. A ton or
two of flowers have been snipped off for
decorative garlands, etc. The Paddington
lown Hall, which has about the most satis-
factory floor of any public building in this
town, is the place and Wednesday the date.
Uampbells, Whites (from up Muswellbrook
way), McMasters, Chisholms, Dangars,
Morts, Wills-Allens, pud half a score more
matrons whose husbands’ flocks and herds
munch a fat share of N.S.W. grass, are the
hostesses.
Most of the “Society” gatherings have
been engineered by women. Though a few
eligible bachelors have been morally squeezed
into answering back with a dance on Friday
night, a thrifty habit of not wasting cash on
other people’s champagne is noticed amongst
our gilded youth of 40 summers or so.
Beastly rot, don’t you know, stumping up
for parties for gangs of women !” said one
bright youth who was asked to lend his aid
to a race-week revel. “They lap up such
gallons of fizz !”
The Arthur Morgans, from Bananaland,
have been in the perspiring throng that re-
cently surged round Sydney’s Easter bun.
He is President of the Leg. Council in Bris-
bane, and grows side-whiskers as a regular
habit. For many years he was reign-
ing sovereign in Warwick (Q.) and its
adjacent pastures, and laid down the law a s
proprietor of the local newspaper.
A usually sprightly dame drops in a line
from the Federal camp at Canberra. The
line got waterlogged on the journey, hence
its belatedness. “The climate,” says she,
‘with the exception of the day of the chris-
tening, was everything that Canberra’s
fiercest opponents have declared it. We
arrived in burning heat, and went into camp
near the scene of the O’Malley’s Federal
crow. Since then, it has rained, hailed,
wailed, and, I believe, snowed on the hills.
\V e took our best frills, but goloshes and
an Arctic rug were the chief necessities of
life. It was fine for the show, but at other
times the wind has nearly torn the hair off
our heads. The unfortunate Light Horse
had many of their tents torn to tatters by
the gale that shrieked round the village of
canvas huts. The Denmans must have had
a blue and chilly time in their camp, for,
like others, they had no fire to warm their
toes. None of the inhabitants of milder
regions who lived under canvas at Canberra
“arlv in March. 1913, are likely to make
any frantic rush for suburban homes on
these wind-swept plains.”
Amongst the last strikes of summer are
the South Coast coal upheaval and the
sudden jibbing of the Sydney ferry-hands
An absent-minded North Sydneyite was
waiting on his usual wharf on the first
morning of the ferry hold-up. “Say, Mister
don teller know there’s a strike?” said the
casual boy who was dangling his body over
the pier. Thanks my lad,” says the man
of learning, 111 order a fuel stove this verv
day!
Artist Rupert Bunny writes from Paris :
The news of Rignold’s death brings back youthful
memories. I was so stage-struck when I had seen
him that I begged my father to apprentice me to
him, as I thought that was the prosaic path that led
to fame. If I hadn’t grown so ridiculously tall, or
rather lanky, I might have stuck to my first love,
the stage. Oddly enough, I was offered' a first-class
engagement in U.S.A. by a manager who chanced to
see me in some amateur affair we got up for the
American Club in prehistoric days. I was heavily
disguised with a wig and a false nose for this per-
formance, and even the shyest person can be more
than daring under such circumstances....
I am working hard, as I have two things going
for the Salon; if I have time I may try to finish
another picture for the Academy-, but fear I won’t
be able to manage it. . . .
It is a very funny thing, but to a certain extent
an artist must remain as simple as a stup’d child to
do really good work. An Abbe, a friend of ours
here, used to teach drawing to some children in his
parish. He observed that the stupidest drew best,
and I quite realise why. If you ask a child —an
ordinary, normal one—to draw you ; house that you
see about a mile away, she (or it) a- once starts by
drawing a square and putting in a, ors, windows,
etc., which are not seen at that distance probably,
but are remembered by an observant child ; whereas
the stupid youngster copies what it sees, and gives
you the real impression of distance. Most painters,
I fancy, find that one of the most difficult things in
art is to be able to forget what a. thing is really
like in detail, and try to see it as if their eyes had
just opened for the first time in their lives.
Concerning the Futurists and other
strange mushrooms that have sprung up in
the Latin Quarter, Bunny remarks: —
Art in Paris at present is in a state of revolution.
It requires strength of character and judgment to
decide what path to follow. Broad, flowered roads
which lead to destruction are opening all round one,
and I fear a great deal of the younger talent will
follow them down to oblivion. To-day, with this
post-impressionism and cubisme, etc., everybody is
a “genius,” and every youngster a “master” after
six months’ loafing in the Quartier Latin. The snob-
bism which has got' into art is appalling. People
pose pretentiously as they assume the comprehension
of absolute insanities. “Insanities” is a compliment,
but I can find no other word. Of course, in this modern
movement there is some good, but it’s very danger-
ous for the men who haven’t gone first to the foun-
dations.
Artist Julian Ashton, who has done so
much fine teaching in Sydney, tells a tale of
two travellers who were caught unawares
by the shock of the French Futurists’ wild
daubs. Traveller the first, an Australian
abroad, was lying on th« broad of his back
at the exhibition, kicking his heels in the
air, and emitting grunts, yells and other
symptoms of frenzied mirth. The floor was
covered with sawdust, presumably to prevent
the crunch of boot-leather breaking up the
harmoqies on the wall. Traveller the
second, recognising the person writhing in
the sawdust, rushed up and said, “What’s
the matter?” “Matter!” says he. “God
bless my soul, look at the show! We’ve
struck the finest comic cuts on earth !”
The ancient Standard has passed into
oblivion, to make way for its regarnished
survivor, The Little Theatre, which was
ouened at least three times last week.
Mayor Cocks was the first to officially click
the latch. This ceremony was performed
with a corkscrew. Two days later, half the
frills of Darling Point and most of the
Pottses and the Pipered Points sailed up
the main passage to get as near the orchestra
as possible. Having talked down the music
with great success,, the company broke into
fragments and chattered like mammies in a
large underground cell, well and truly fur-
nished. Here our sex can retire to sip
coffee while its legal lord (or someone else’s)
dashes into the night air to inquire about
the nearest bottled dog.
Mayoress Cocks was the godma of
the second opening of The Little Theatre,
having been secured for that purpose by
Hugh Buckler and beauteous Violet Paget.
The latter, by the way, had the best-brushed
head of golden hair present. General
Gordon seemed to suddenly smite the
rafters of the ladies’ cellaret'te, which
is designed to accommodate short, fat
women, rather than tall, lean men. The
musical Hollanders, the long elegance of
Mrs. Tom Rolin, a selection from the family
of Moses, the George Earps (little Mrs.
George just visible under the brim of a
fashionable millinery tomb), and Reynolds
Denniston, who has a finger in this theatri-
cal pie, rose from the surface from time to
time.
Zanelli, who came with the Melba Opera
Co., and has lingered on this shore ever
since, now packs up for Paris. Before throw-
ing a farewell kiss to the nicest-looking
persons hereabouts, he sings his swan song
in the Concordia Hall on April 8. Fiddler
Cyril Monk and the small, brown-eyed
Esther Kahn are amongst the assistant
artists.
Contralto Eugenie Boland is returning to
Sydney in May after about five years’ ab-
sence. The tall, fair girl from Toowoomba,
who is an ex-pupil of Madame Christian,
has been earning considerably more than a
crust in the English provinces and else-
where since she went forth to try her voice
in other climes. The inevitable welcome-
home concert is pending.
Ray Royce, the recently-arrived actor
from N’York, has played gentle, old-man
parts for so many years that he has assim-
ilated the character, and now wears it off
the boards. He’s so mild and suave in
manner that it is alleged he would apologise
to the stage doormat if he rubbed it up the
wrong way. Noting this extraordinary
meekness in a ’Murkan mummer, one mem-
ber of the J.C.W. staff remarked a day
before the “Fortune Hunter” opened: “You
don’t —er- — look, much like an actor?” “No,
sir,” says Royce, “but I hope to to-morrow
night.

The Macarthur-Onslows and Mr. “Ossv”
Watt are going to shape the destinies of the
“Twilight Home” for old ladies, as directors
or something of the kind. The next thing
is to get a building-site which all hands will
like. When the Home for Incurables wa«
moved from its humble tenement in a mean
city street to an airy mansion at Ryde sur-
rounded by a generous acreage of grassy
lawns, the people most concerned were
deeply offended. They were getting “too
much country life,” and for the first few
months they poured their sorrows into the
ear of every visitor. “To them that’s born
and bred in the town,” said one old dame
“there’s no place like the city. ‘You can
hear the birds singin’ in the trees where you
are now,’ ses one lady to me. I just told
her I’d rather hear the milk-cart clatterin’
down the street, or a man singin’ out
‘prawns’—and so I would!” If the Twi-
light Home is to have garden space and
breathing room, it can’t flap its blinds with-
in a stone’s throw of the G.P.0.; yet if the
committee gets too far from the madding
motor, high-spirited old veterans will want
to know if they’re going to be buried aliye,
or what?
The thunder of Willie Kelly’s oratory was
drowned in the uproar of the great Beef and
Bacon show. Willie addressed the “Liberal”
ladies of Rose Bay and adjacent lawns at an
evening party on March 19. Unfortunately
the oration fell up against dailies crowed
with tons of matter re pumpkins and prize
pigs. In consequence, what Mr. Kelly really
o d say to his lady friends about the wicked
ways of the Socialistic Tiger which gobbles
up young and tender maidens when they’re
not hanging on to the political coat-tails of
little Willie, is chiefly surmise.
Horticulture for women and the Right
Way to intimidate the Australian hen to lay-
eggs in Lent and other high-priced seasons,
is again struggling to the fore. The neces-
sary information will be locked up in lec-
tures, which begin on March 26 at No. 7
O’Connell-street (city). Mr. 11. R. Harris,
who knows all the subterfuges of the wily
fowl, is announced to give the first exposi-
tion on her many vices and occasional vir-
tues, for the benefit of women who want to
l ’unge into the chicken trade.
The Royal Art Society has invited its
friends and enemies to a private view of
cabinets on April 5. On the 7th the public
will have a chance of dropping its sixpence
at the portal.
The new autumn hats are either as small
and round as the Easter egg, with a chook’s
feather dangling behind, or fairly large and
strewn with a blizzard of ostrich plumes.
May 28 is fixed as the date for the whoop
of triumph to be raised on the top of Mount
York to celebrate the centenary of the cross-
ing of the Blue Mountains. Mt. York itself
will gain a water-tank and a pavilion. The
latter is not to be fenced off with barbed
wire, or guarded by an armed force. After
distracting discussion, the celebrations com-
mittee has decided to trust the tourist,
though he is so prone to attack mountain
scenery with a penknife. He will merely be
requested not to hack bits oat of the
memorial pavilion—and the rest will be left
to his own good sense, if he has any.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORLAN.
“Y.Z.” Yon must have noticed the difference
in the public frame of mind with regard
to the two Antarctic tragedies—that which
leaves Scott and his companions under a vast
monument of ice, and the other that buried
Mawson’s companions in eternal snow. The
one stirred humanity to its depths; it har-
rowed our souls within an inch of the limit,
and left us barren of compassion and cash.
The other has had scarcely any more effect
upon the public’s sensibilities than might
have been occasioned by the disappearance
of two Dutch inebriates from a fish-scow in
the Yarra. Yet, all things considered, the
end of Mawson’s men was quite as tremen-
dous and tragic as that of Scott and _ his
little band ; and, in common fairness, civil-
ised communities were called upon to be
almost as disturbed and pathetic over the
one as they were over the other. But it
could not be. Let this be a warning to
heroes, teaching them to beware of the anti-
climax. Every hero should know when to
die as well as how, and the one who elects
to perish magnificently right on the heels of
another who has used up all the available
human emotion is absolutely certain to be
cut dead.

You Know Dickens,
But DO You Know Schumann?

THE creator of “Little Nell” you surely know and love. His books have
furnished hour after Hour of amusement. David Copperfield and
Oliver Twist, Jenny Wren and Agnes Wakefield —all of his characters
are almost as living friends to you.
Suppose by an accident of environment you had never enjoyed
Dickens —that his works had no meaning for you—that you
had never read them. And, further, suppose that not only
Dickens, hut Shakspeare, Milton, Thackeray, Irving and all
literature were forbidden you. The world would be dull
indeed.
Yet do you know Robert Schumann—as great in music as Dickens in
literature? Dickens wrote “Nicholas Nickelby,” but Schumann composed
“Carnaval.” “A Christmas Carol” is no greater or more appealing than
“Traumerei.” Who can listen to “The Two Grenadiers” without a thrill
of exultation ? And Schumann is but one of a thousand famous composers.
Don’t imagine that to revel in music you must first study.
You can play to-day if you will only say I will. You can
play the

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 24, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
After Lady Fuller has settled her young
family at school, she intends to stay awhile
with grandpa and grandma in Wiltshire
(Lng.), and tell the old folks all about the
vice-regal achievements of son John. Her
Kx. feels the need of a quiet holiday, and
will not return till next October. Later on,
Auntie Phipps, who has just landed on In-
dian soil, is to join her again. Meanwhile,
the lonely Gov. and his reduced staff will
struggle along and wrestle with the food
problem. Help Wheatley, whose billet was
kept open for him, has definitely decided to
stay in England. This is disappointing, as
Y\ heatley is a useful sort of man who
can control a household. • The food depart-
ment has now been entrusted to Secretary
Wilberforce.
State Government House is getting very
confused with its visitors’ list, and the staff
lias been smudging ink on its ears trying to
plan a way out of the difficulties. As things
are at present, each shower of invitations
brings forth a crop of complaints from the
local nobility. Daughters get invitations
while Mamma is ignored as completely as
if she went out washing by the day. Then
Mamma bears down like an angry cow on
some anguished young man with beautifully
brushed hair, and demands to know who
will chaperone her girls if she is not to be
asked inside the place? One married man
received a vice-regal dinner invitation at his
club. . There was no mention of his more
talkative half, so he guessed it was purely
a man’s spree. When the lady in the next
mansion mentioned to his wife that she had
been invited, the fat was instantly at the
telephone, and the Fuller staff received an-
other jolt by wire. Governor Fuller should
sool his forces on to the compilation of a
secret. tome, to be called “The Feedirg
Families.” Perhaps it would be best to
have it in one of those books with removable
leaves. Then when poor old Smithjones fell
with a social crash, a Help, in faultless
clothes, could painlessly extract him from
the ledger without spilling ink over the sur-
vivors.
Prior to his departure for London as
Agent-General, Peter Mcßride was tendered
a final farewell in the shadow of the Gaud
Mayor’s rabbit-skin; while the Mayoress
entertained Mrs. Peter and her friends in
the “blue” room of the Town Hall. The
amateur music was so plentiful that guests
were forced to sub-edit their conversation,
ihe blinds were drawn, and the light was
dim enough for a spiritualistic seance. Mrs.
Peter, a handsome woman, was neatly
dressed in navy-blue tailorings, and sup-
ported a smart feathered hat. Young daugh-
ter Kathleen, also smartly tailored, wore a
cunning little presentation posy dangling
from a bracelet, whilst mamma bore like a
shield a brilliant shower bouauet handed to
her by the Mayoress. Mrs. Davey, with pink
roses in her hat, and Mrs. Pleasance, accom-
panied by a blue-and-white daughter, were
amongst those who sipped and chattered.
Mrs. Garnett inhabited a delightful gown of
mole color and purple, and Mrs. Geoffrey
Svme, her daughter, looked dainty in black
and white. During an interval in the music
a young and sweetly-frocked matron lisped
a fairy story. The organ did not groan
heavily over this function. Mr. and Mrs
Peter, with daughter Kathleen, left by next
morning’s mailboat, after a delightfully
tiring week of farewells.
The Druids’Bazaar blazed open on Saturday.
Before midday the echoing Exhibition was
tuff of carriers asking pietously “Ey!
Where s this ter go?” and aproned society
matrons rushing in three directions at once,
while, outside, motors jostled, and furniture
vans blocked the entrances, and scornful
shovers dredged unwieldy parcels from the
depths of fiery chariots. By two o’clock every-
thing was in order and ready for the solemn
opening ceremony by the Denman lady. The
attendant Help was Nutting, who wore im-
maculate white spats, black and white troll-
's of faultless cut, an irreproachable tie,
and a clean collar. Two serious policemen
made a pathway for the usual processional
visit to the stalls. Her Ex. wore black and
white stripes, pearls round her neck, and
black plumes on her hat. A velvet-collared
and very hot Druid of particular eminence
worried in advance of the little procession,
bide by side with him panted Mr* Hood,
crowned with a savage circlet of pink feath-
ers. Gaud Mayor Hennessy, severely frock-
coated, escorted his wife, who had all she
could do to keep up with him. Mrs. Fel-
stead’s white frock contrasted nicely with
Mrs. Brennan’s dignified black rig.' The
latter lady had no less than eight daughters
bushranging for charity. They are practised
brigands.. Pa is a Past Grand, or something
of the kind. Help Nutting had hard work
to get through, since convention compelled
him to linger behind and settle up for the
stall purchases of her Ex. Little Judith
Denman was also, included in the party, and
Nutting looked quite pleased when she tugged
imperiously at his sleeve, and insisted on
bemg taken behind the scenes to meet some
of the lady performers on the trapeze!
Her Ex. was due at the George Chirn-
sides’ Werribee place for evening dinner
and the week-end. As Werribee is a good
20 miles out of town, she began to sprint.
Possessing a fine sporting stride, she covered
much ground once she started to speed up.
The watching crowd was greatly cheered
when her dignified train had to start jog-
ging to keep within coo-ee. The social
handicap finished at the tea-tables in the
rooms of the Exhibition Trustees/
The far-out suburbs of Oakleigh and Mul-
grave joined forces in an original stall
laden with fruit and vegetables. Mayoress
Arnold Westley wore a low-necked white
muslin frock, and sold apples. Mrs. Wilmot
and Mrs. Tait, of Hawthorn, pushed off
potatoes, onions and home-made crabapple
jelly. Mayor Westley looked on with a
proud smile and helped trundle marrows
and things. Mrs. Quinnell, of the Austral
Salon, handed in a stout cheque to the
funds. The Salonettes did not fix up a
stall, but just plaved bridge a little, and so
got some money together.
Last week Prime Minister Fisher superin-
tended the marrying of his- wife’s sister to
Eli Edwards, one of our crack yachtsmen.
Christina Irvine was a bonny vision when
decked out in her silk and lace and crowned
with the conventional veil and blossom.
Tetty Fisher, the Prime Minister’s small
daughter, with an enormous pink sash
round her chest, carried the white satin
cushion for the bride to kneel on. The
grown-up maids were sister Miss Irvine and
Miss Edwards—one all pink and the other
all white, like cocoanut ice. The affair was
billed as a quiet function, but 50 relatives
saved it from being absolutely noiseless. Hugh
I aterson and his wife, sisters and daugh-
ters were the only guests not actually allied
to the family. The two clever Paterson
girls played the Wedding March, one
manning the piano and the other a violin.
Mrs. Andrew Fisher looked handsome in a
black gown that was built in London.
(Warning to Angry Interjector: “No! this
is not the place to talk about Protection!”)
Grandma was a soft vision in grey. And
the finale was all motor whir and confetti.
Mrs. Davey, ex-Mayoress, is now doing
a round of farewell tea-and-bun feasts
arranged by friends. She is listed as an
April departure for England. Davey’s
nephew has hung on to his job of Gaud
Mayor ot London for another term, and he
is anxious to board and lodge his aunt and
uncle while he reigns. Unhappily, business
will keep Uncle Davey here for another four
months; but as soon as he is free he will
steam after his buxom dame and escort her
back early next year.
Another pair of wanderers who intend to
turn Europe upside down are the doctoring
Godfrey Howitt couple. They sailed last
week in the tall ship Otranto.
Clara Butt approaches. She has left
America after a sustained conquest, and is
coming hither with the usual retinue. The
caravan, which, of course, includes all the
children, is timed to arrive in May, 10 days
before the opening bars are played. The
first concert will inaugurate the new Tait
concert hall, which is guaranteed to be fitted
with all the modern improvements.
A Canadian visitor, Mr. Cockshutt, is in
Melbourne making a noise like a million-
aare. His little dinners and suppers are per-
fect things in their way, and mothers of
nice girls are _ endeavoring to find out if the
rich and hospitable stranger .is a bachelor or
a mere grass-widower. On the last night of
the Asche-Brayton season, he dined a num-
ber of people at Menzies’. took them along
to the theatre, and afterwards suppered at
the Savoy. Others of the audience made
their way to the same place for the final
gorge of the day. The Asches themselves
had a table with a fountain in the centre-
piece threatening the electric globes with its
spray. Lily showed a considerable expanse
of dimpled shoulder and a few jewels above
the heaped flowers. Oscar’s face, shirtfront
and diamond stud radiated with good nature
and prosperity. None of the late feeders
disturbed their drowsy shovers until the cold,
thoughtful hours that walk on cold feet be-
fore the dawn. Rupert Clarke came on from
the theatre with another hungry party; and
Mrs. Garnett, with the Derhams and 'daugh-
ter Mrs. Syme, floated in to join another
contingent. '
Mrs. Cadoyin (once upon a time Bendigo's
Loyie Mueller) is now established with iher
Italian sculptor-husband in a flat; at White-
hall —the Melbourne Whitehall, not thqhis-
tonc London palace. Cadorin’s beautiful
statuary is all over the place, jostling‘tor
room with the sideboard, the piano an| the
coal-scuttle. One of the most pathetic sights
in the world is an art treasure fighting for
house-room with ordinarv furniture.
A cold, wet wind did its villainous best
to spoil the Fairbairn festivity, but, in spite
of all, a crowd of the Most Exclusive came
along to do their duty. The function served
as a general good-bye to daughter Alison
(who has since steamed off to London, after
collecting her captain husband and the small
Stewart Balmains), and as a welcome to son
Clive’s bride. Geordie Balmain and little
Valerie Purvis distributed fans imprinted
with a musical programme. Maggie Ster-
ling and daughter, Claude Schilling and
Weston Pett supplied the music ; but the in-
tended audience mostly eluded the melody,
and hurried away to play bridge. Mamma
Fairbairn was a gorgeous spectacle in ame-
thyst panne piped with parrot red; her hat
was cosily swathed with fur. Mrs. Balmain
wore an airy lace garment and a blue hat,
which fluttered with ostrich plumes. Pretty
Mrs. Clive was enclosed by yards of spiral
drapery as grey as the day itself. Millers,
Langdales, Clarkes and Emmertons abounded.
George Fairbairn wandered about wearing
a troubled look. The crowd was fed under
canvas, which, being soaked, showered rain-
drops on the gay plumage below. The at-
tendant brass band got exceedingly wet on
the lawn, and drank between bars the rain
that poured into their instruments.
The Good Friday holiday crowd, flocking
towards the Flinders-street station, stopped
and gaped, thrilled by the suggestion that
there was a funeral procession to make splen-
did the official gloom of the day. But it
turned out to be merely a vice-regal party
coming away from St. Paul’s Cathedral after
morning service. Governor Fuller led the
procession, endeavoring to conceal his nat-
ural chirpiness under a black top hat, and
talking in furtive undertones with Help Cur-
wen, who flapped sorrowful black coat-tails.
The Fuller lady, heavily draped in black,
followed with t" r o fair-haired children, both
of them woe-fully clad. A black nursery
Help towed another sad infant in front of
an attendant group of clergy. The lugubri-
ous march terminated at the kerb, where the
yellow motors were in waiting.
Aileen Bell, the Bananaland heiress who
has been staying with the Norman Army-
tages, was farewelled by Mrs. Norman at
the Alexandra Club. The hostess smiled
over the buns in pretty clouds of black, while
daughter Edna, robed in blue, hovered in
attendance. The departing Bell gurgled her
good-byes in a chalk-white frock and a hat
decorated with a rose of great size. Sep
Miller’s wife, fresh from her travels, looked
well under a wide satin lid. Violent color
contrasts were to be seen on every side.
Mrs. Hodges sported a violet sash 'over a
magpie blend. Mrs. Scobie Mackinnon shep-
herded a grass-green daughter. A brilliant
streak of purple located Mrs. Douglas Men-
zies, and a splash of aggressive blue pro-
claimed. Mrs. Clarke. Mrs. Seaton Williams,
the Wrixon dame, and George Paxton’s neat
wife were other features of the costly
kaleidoscope.
The Bowes Kelly matron brought over a
horrible attack of bronchitis from the Speck,
and was thereby defrauded of the gaieties
that accompany the Autumn horse-frenzy.
Her just-out girleen (who, by the way, has
inherited the maternal good looks) is now
tackling the housekeeping problem with
great courage. The elder son, Moncton, is
an elegant ’Varsity boy who fairly soars
out of his boots. He soars 6ft. sin.
Grey-haired Millie Carter is starting off
on a second tramp abroad with her bosom
friend, Mrs. C. M. Lloyd. The trip is in-
tended as a sort of consolation prize for win-
ning health after many weary months of
sickness. It was not appendicitis, but neu-
ritis, that kept her in her room, and her best
chiffons in the wardrobe. The departing
pair will give a tea in the Alexandra Club
on the last day of March.
Lawyer Warming proposes to chip into
the matrimonial boom. His fiancee is Lilian
Samers, the pretty daughter of Councillor
Samers, of Avoca.
Some cheery young men who had invaded
a Victorian country town incited the local
bachelors to arrange a dance. When the
programmes came out, it was found that
various trots and hugs had been mixed up
with the custom-honored gyrations. The
local dowagers started to cluck excitedly,
and sped to and Lo kicking up the dust,
until all the new contortions were called off
and the programme amended. The names
kill the modern dancing novelties. They
would have a much better chance of passing
the censors if they could be given dignified
processional labels like the St. Paul’s This
and the Westminster That.
Public opinion is an awful snag some-
times. W hen Mr. Woolacres and Mrs.
YV oolacres the second sailed into the dining-
room of a first-rank hotel, a sort of con-
certed shudder ran through the room. Fallen
cutlery rattled on plates, and the sibilant
piss of silk dresses sounded like 40 serpents,
as the women turned round to stare and
gasp, “How dare they!” The sympathy of
the house was plainly with the first Mrs.
oolacres. Later on, a deputation, swollen
with righteous indignation, approached the
manager, and put it plainly to him that they
could not continue to breathe the same
whisky and hash as the new Woolacre pair.
The thrilling episode terminated with the
more or less dignified retreat of the new
u oolacres towards a less partisan shelter.

In the midst of all this the Milne-Fotheringham
wedding occurred at Chalmers Church. The bride

Mr. Skoltowe, of the Eastern Extension Co who
married one of the good-looking Hacks, has been
transferred from “the East” to Sydney. His wife has
been in Adelaide for some time, staying with her
mother. & 1
Archdeacon and Mrs. Bussell and Dr. Humphrey
Marten are in the latest list of “returned from
England Mrs Marten is remaining behind, so
that a final polish may be put on her daughter's
education Dr. Harry Gilbert, Dr. Marten’s partner
is now planning a six months' trip to India - ’
Mr. W. Howchin had congratulations showered
upon him at the meeting of the Public Library
Board on Friday. The Australian Association for
the Advancement of Science has presented him with
a medal in recognition of his original research in
Australasia. Mr. Howchin is a keen geologist, and
can unfold many a tale of Australia’s goings-on in
the glacial epoch.
The whirl of winter is beginning. Invitations are
out for a datiee at Mount Lofty on trie Thursday
after Easter. A committee, about a dozen strong
is responsible for the festivity.
A cold, bleak wind reduced the attendance at the
Autumn regatta to its lowest terms. Oncp more the
Murray Bridge crew came to the front; but in the
Ladies’ Race the tables were turned and the country-
men were left languishing in the distance.
The Easter exodus from the city was great, and
accommodation in the hills is at a premium. Mr.
IV. Bakewell is having a house-party at Mt. Lofty
for the Easter week-end; it is hostessed by his
daughter, Mrs. O’Leary. Other lucky folk who havp
houses in these plutocratic heights are planning to
drive their friends over to Oakbank. Old stagers
complain that this Onkaparinga meeting is now too
luxurious, and sigh for other ways and days. Secre-
tary von Doussa takes no part in these repinings, and
watches the steadily-improving returns with a proud
eye.
Miss J. O’Halloran Giles leaves fpr England at the '
end of April, with Miss Mabel Marryat. Miss Meek
■and Mrs. Melville leave next week. Miss George, who
was farewelled by her one-time pupils at the High
School on Wednesday, has also booked for Europe.
The Port Adelaide races drew a redotd crowd to
Cheltenham on Saturday; but lovely woman mostly
stayed away. The totalisator showed an advance
of nearly £2OOO on last year. The sweetest thing
for backers was a -£l7 10s. dividend paid by Mar-
ooned in tlie Island Handicap.
The League of Empire is pushing on the move-
ment to erect in Adelaide a memorial to explorer
Sturt. So far neither he nor Flinders have any local
masonry to their honor. Light and Stuart are
Adelaide’s favored ones—with a Hercules and a
Queen Victoria in bronze near by.
The Misses Hawk os, of Koonunga station, have
gone .for a two-years’ trip to England. Their father
shepherded them as far as Fremantle, seeing them
safely through one of Clement Wragge’s typhoons,
and then left them to fend for themselves.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
Holman lias been telling the London
Daily Chronicle that Labor unrest in
N.S.W. has practically ceased. A visitor,
who has spent the last month in Sydney, has
been heard timidly asking what things are
like when the unrest really gets to business.
The Rev. S. Knight, at a Methodist Young
People’s Demonstration, Melbourne : “Put the
same excitement into church matters that
is expended on football matches, and you’ll
simplv set the land on fire.” That’s all very
well, but there are things one can’t get ex-
cited about. Cold suet duff, for instance.

POLITICAL
POINTS
THE DEFENDER , AND THE SAME
OLD SPIRIT.
Mr. Bruce Smith yesterday said the adoption of
the Referenda would be the greatest calamity that
ever happened to Australia. Political wreckers and
declared partisans were trying to force them upon
the Australian people.— Sydney paper.
The anti-Defence person makes strange
remarks. A Maoriland specimen of the
alleged Socialistic kind suggests mildly that
“when our Defence scheme is fully work-
ing, thousands of our youths will be re-
jected, not for financial but for political
reasons.” Which apparently means that, in
the view of this convinced preacher of the
doctrine of brotherhood, John Smith could
not be trusted with a rifle lest he should go
out and shoot his boss in token of blood-
relationship.
Two new amusements threaten the elector
in Victoria. One Renwick announces him-
self as a Freetrade candidate for the Senate,
which is equivalent to a nice young mis-
sionary-bishop announcing to the cannibals
of Malayta that he is succulent and tender,
and has never smoked tobacco, which is a
filthy weed. The other amusement is the
earnestness of the Age in its attempt to
become anew the electoral power it used to
be ere David the King waxed old. The new
crusade is conducted with stodgy conversa-
tions between the candidate and the elector.
The one-time trumpet of the Age is a
squeaking fife now; and it makes matters
worse by losing the music and playing bag-
pipe noises out of its own head.
“Side Lights” prods somebody:—
The men whose job it is to search out and look
after Australian ports will have to keep moving—
especially those whose job lies with ports that
haven’t got too much depth for the liners they
already know. The vogue of big and bigger steamers
has advanced with a jump during the past few years,
even the past few months. In England it was re-
cently demonstrated clearly by hard-headed men
who don’t care a cuss for anything outside the
question of what pays best, that it costs only 20 per
cent, more to carry 10,950 tons dead weight than
to carry 5910 tons. There were a lot of other im-
portant conclusions about higher speed and block co-
efficients, etc., but they are small fry besi'de that
20 per cent. fact. Owners and builders grabbed
the lesson with both hands, and hurriedly started in
to slam together bigger boats. The results are in
skeleton form in all the British yards to-day.
Four ships are building of over 20,000 tons. These
are Aquitania, Britannic, a new Holland-American
liner and a Red Star liner. The Royal Mail Steam
Packet and Pacific Steam are building liners of over
15,000 tons, and two Cape mailboats will go beyond
that. On the stocks there are in all 10 boats of
between 15,000 and 20,000 tons, nine of 12,000 to
15,000, and 16 of 10,000 to 12,000. Australian
lines can’t afford to ignore the cheapness of the big
boat. The first question, therefore, is how long will
it be before a fleet of 20- to 30-thousand tonners is
running to Australia? And the second is how many
Australian ports will be able to accommodate them?
Did anyone mention Jervis Bay and other neglected
deep-water harbors ?
THE BRITISH LION.
ENGLAND IN APATHY.
London, March 7.- The Guardian says that while
Australia has established a system of national mili-
tary service, and Canada has assumed the defence
of her coasts and frontiers. England has neither the
men nor the stomach to take up a like task, but
prefers to shout at football matches and eat sweets
in picture palaces.— Cable.
Commonwealth Statist Knibbs is advocat-
ing a five-year instead of the statutory 10-
year census. Serious discrepancies between
the Federal and State Statisticians are of
frequent occurrence, and 10 years is too
much to wait to clear them up. As long as
the Commonwealth pays the States 255. or
any other sum per head per annum, a fre-
quent census seems a necessity. The 10-year
event was adopted so that we might be just
like England; and we are not just
like England. England has unification,
while Australia, for the moment, thinks that
unification is some sort of untried dope
which shouldn’t be uncorked in respectable
company.
N.S.W. Works Minister Griffith had a
little heart-to-heart talk with the Hoskins
firm the other day, and said he thought he
was paying too much for the pipes that the
co. was making. If the firm couldn’t see
its way clear to meet him, he rather thought
the Department would start its own pipe
works and make the State independent of
Private Enterprise in the matter. There-
upon the S. M. Herald blew u" with a loud
leader, in which it declared that the Minis-
ter was not amenable to reason, that he was
either bullying or electioneering, that his
attitude represented an unscrupulousness
without parallel in our history, or it was so
much piracy. Less than a week before, the
Herald, commenting on “The Passing of
the Leaders,” wrote :
Men of ability and established position are re-
fraining from coming forward into public life.
The strain and worry of public life, the unwarranted
attacks on their character, sincerity and honor, all
discourage men who would be valuable legislators.
Does the Herald think that to declare a
Crown Minister either a bully or a pirate is
fair criticism calculated to cheer him up and
keep him going, and encourage others to
come in and share the compliments?
The Argus is heavily sarcastic because of
the Federal Government’s persistence with
its Referendum proposals; —
So it proposes to continue to pester the stupid
people until by force of repetition it gains from
their mere weariness what, owing to their want of
intelligence, they so emphatically refused to grant
before.
But The Bulletin can’t remember that
the Argus threw the same gibe at the direc-
tors of the A.M.P. Society for appealing again
and again to the members for authority to
push business in Britain. And it can’t
remember either that the Argus has told
the “Liberals” to leave off trying to oust
Labor Ministers because an attack has
been repulsed already. Also, when Scott
made one attempt to reach the Pole and
failed, what was the Argus doing that it
referred to his British bull-dog pluck m
having a second go at it?
Joe Hanan, who represents Invercargill’s
thirst in the Maoriland Parliament, has
been digging out some facts, with intent to
prove to Cohen that his investments in the
great Loan Land are all right. The export
figures per head of population he works out
as follows, excluding odd pence: Maori-
land, £2l; Australia, £l6; Britain, £11;
S’Africa, £9; Canada, £8; Germany, £5;
United States, £3; India, 10s. This may
be a very joyous story for Cohen, but, apart
from the fact that the figures mean little
unless you know what a country has to
export in order to pay interest to its foreign
creditor, it looks like pretty poor manage-
ment. A country that can send away £2l
worth per head in mutton, wool and other
raw materials could afford to keep a very
fine population at home to make better use
of most of the articles that go to help out
Bull’s breakfast and replenish his wardrobe.
Joe Cook has been opening his mouth
again, with the usual disastrous results.
The man of the many-colored coat was
snarling at the maternity al-
lowance, and remarked that no
one could even guarantee that
the money was going to the
person entitled to it, as there
was no machinery to follow it
to its destination. Apparently
Joe doesn’t know the payment
is made by post-office order,
payable only to the mother her-
self ; and if that isn’t “machin-
ery to follow it to its destina-
tion,” then the post-office order
business (which Joe himself
had a good deal to do with
when he was P.M.G.) is a
fraud. Incidentally, Joe re-
marked that some ladies got
the bonus and promptly paid
the money into their political
funds. Then he stopped. He
had given away one of the
“secrets” of the Great Liberal
Party.
A card from the “Constitutional Union,”
Melbourne, invites an army of local citizens
to become plural voters under the Electoral
Act of 1910, Prior to receiving a card
of instructions to the contrary, the average
tenant of a city office supposed One Man
One Vote to be the law in elections for the
Vic. Assembly. But the Constitutional
Union advises him as follows: —
Your City Vote for the Leg. Assembly, in addition
to your vote for the district in which you reside, is
now a Political Right of those who have Property
or Business Interests in the city.
The mere renting of a small office in a sky-
scraper building is a “business interest in
the city,” and. according to this reading, a
couple of hundred plural voters may be shel-
tered under one roof, thanks to the Electoral
Act of 1910.
King George of Greece was assassinated
last week. Another lamentable tonic for
the strengthening of Royalty and “Loyalty”:
Worse than useless, killing kings;
Monarchs leave successors;
Long as law allows such things,
Kings are not transgressors.
Bullets that have laid them low
Better far had missed ’em.
The assassin’s bomb or knife
Terminates a harmless life —
And serves a harmful system.
victoria. O. K.
A ghastly tragedy took place in George-
street, Sydney, the other night, when Jim
Smith, the Australian citizen, got into an
argument with his other self, Jim Smith, the
N. S. Wales man. Jim the Australian ex-
pressed a quiet hope that Jim the Cornstalk
would vote sensibly on May 31, and make
the national Government strong. Jim the
Cornstalk accused Jim the Australian
of trying to wreck the glorious Constitu-
tion and destroy the grand old Mother State.
The Australian portion of Jim replied that
there was something wrong, as both the
Constitution and State Rights couldn’t be
wrecked by the same amendment —one or
other of them must be aggrandised. He
also inquired which were the greater, the
rights of the States, or the rights of the
people living in the States; and, anyhow,
what were State Rights? He answered him-
self by remarking coldly that State Rights
were mostly Trust Privileges. Whereupon the
N.S.W. portion of Jim screamed that,
according to Joe Cook, there were no trusts.
The Australian part of Jim pointed out
that an investigation by Glvnn, Attorney-
General in the Dbakin Government, revealed
the presence of 33 trusts and combines in
Australia, only about four of which could
be dealt with as the Constitution stood ; and
it was declared that Parliament was power-
less to deal with the other 29 in the pre-
sent state of the Constitution; wherefore
Jim the Australian asked Jim the Cornstalk
whether he liked trusts and high prices so
much that he didn’t want any body to have
power to deal with them- Just about this
point the N.$.W. portion of Jim became
exasperated, and he made a violent assault
upon himself, howling that he would not
have his State Rights taken from him, or
his glorious _ freedom cut down. He then
took a running jump at himself, and was
last seen by the astonished poliee in hot
nursitit of himself in the direction of Callan
Park.
There are occasions when the State Pre-
miers can show a most Federal spirit. Re-
cently the question of an Australian contri-
bution to the Scott fund cropped up, and
Prime Minister Fisher communicated with
the various State Premiers, asking if they
intended to act independently. There was
a unanimous decision on the part of the
State Insects that the Australian Govern-
ment was the right body in the right place.
So Australia will put up £2OOO.

louseph Ward, at an Irish national
dinner in London, declared that every
Irishman in Maoriland had willingly borne
his share of the Dreadnought “gift.” How-
does Ward know? On the earliest possible
occasion after he had made the “gift” on
his own authority, he was kicked out of
office. If Ward has any evidence besides
that, in support of his theory that M.L.
favored his policy, he ought to produce it.

Joseph Cook! He spoke at a Defence
dinner the other night, and was the one
speaker to prove a bore. Watson is always
magnetic because he is manly; Pearce’s
manner becomes attractive because he is so
much in earnest; but when the man with
the “e-10-cution” of a galvanised-iron taber-
nacle began to bleat I felt that there may
be worse inflictions than a Jap invasion. As

" Now then! Produce your weapon and wade
