J. L. Fegan, who was Mines Minister in
the See Cabinet in the early ’9o’s, has been
unearthed to contest the Hunter Federal
seat against Matthew Charlton, the Labor
member. The mining vote is the deciding
factor in this electorate, and Fegan, an
ex-miner—very much ex —is looked to as a
forlorn hope.
DEVELOPMENT FROM WITHIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I say there is no need for a transcontinental
railway for some time to come. There is no need
for the expenditure of millions of money for a rail-
way north and south.... Don’t misunderstand me.
Developmental railways within the Territory are
necessary, but I protest against the construction of
the transcontinental line. — J. C. Watson.
We really do not need to build
A railway to the North at all;
The tropic fields are all untilled,
The population still is small;
In cautious haste we must begin
To fill the country from within.
A hooker prowling round the coast
May bring a settler now and then,
Or we may get by camel post
A straggling band of mining men;
While farm and station, gold and tin,
We still develop from within.
For we shall teach the cane to grow,
And strew the vales with tilth and corn,
While population starts to show
Its full intention to be born,
And stalwarts of our kith and kin
Develop grandly from within.
In one short fifteen hundred years.
Or eighteen hundred at the most,
We shall have mastered all our fears
And built some towns along the coast;
For in the end to fame we win
When things develop from within.
A strong horse-doctor at the wheel,
With plenty of officials near,
Fills us with pride and makes us feel
His is the proper way to steer ;
Our solid faith in him we pin—
His Excellency is within.
A railway costs the very deuce,
And might be money thrown away ;
Officials have their proper use,
And we are glad their screws to pay ;
With these in wisdom we begin
To fill the country from within.
There is no hurry ; all goes well;
We can afford to rest and wait —
At least until the Asian hell
Breaks loose upon our northern gate.
Then we shall yell and howl like sin
For rails to bring some soldiers in.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
This week-end was dull from the Sydney
sportsman’s standpoint. Cricket was off, the big
clubs mostly sending friendly teams into the coun-
try. Sailing was likewise off, and the sole pastimes
were horse-racing, bowls, boxing and running motor-
boat ferries. The last was almost as lucrative as
horse-racing. One lucky launch-owner is said to
have raked in £lO9 for Friday and Saturday, and
then he went forth on Sunday to pick up the fat
crumbs that fell from the hastily-organised Govern-
ment service—and got ’em.
* * * #
The first day of the A.J.C. Autumn meet-
ing saw over 40,000 people present, all intent on
taking down the bookmakers; and, needless to say,
most of them came home, more or less fleeced. For
the stars in their courses and the high gods in
Olympus fight for the bag- of Patrick Iscariotstein.
In the great Doncaster Handicap the public went
baldheaded for Hartfell, the son of Ayr Laddie by
Light Heart, and long before starting time the best
on offer was two to one against him. Meanwhile
his half-brother, Jolly Beggar, Ayr Laddie’s son by
Lady Trenville, with 21b. more to carry, was beg-
ging at. 12’s. Barring his stable, no one seemed to
want him particularly. His jockey wore a white
cap, and when the race started that cap was
like the white plume of Navarre —-always in the
front. It flashed away from ’he jump, and 21
horses thundered behind it. It made the pace a
cracker till they turned into the straight;
and then a grey horse wc.it alongside. But
he stayed there only a second or so; the
white cap streaked to the front again, and
thundered for the goal. The whips came down
like hail flashing in the sunlight; the horses
bounded under the urging of whip and spur; but
the one that bore the white cap swept on, and won
by a length and a half —another classic race for
“Lucky” Connelly. A curious thing was that the
1912 and 1913 Doncasters were run in exactly the
same time, lmin. 40sec. Hartfell, the unlucky half-
brother who carried the public hopes and coin, was
nowhere, swallowed up in the ruck. It was Jolly
Beggar’s race from start to finish.
Some curiosities of Saturday’s Doncaster mile.
This is the sixth time the race has been run in
lmin. 40sec. The 22 starters were equally divided
into those carrying more and those carrying less
than Bst. It was the 27th Doncaster, but for the
first time, in the history of the race nothing less
than Bst. was placed. Jolly Beggar carried Bst.
131 b., Sir Willie Bst. 3Tb. and Golden Hop Bst 41b.
Moreover, the next five past the post also carried
over Bst. (St. Medoc Bst. 51b., Malt Mary Bst. 71b.,
Cider Bst. 91b., Hartfell Bst. 111 b. and My Gavonni
9st. 41b.). The foremost of those below Bst., the
most dangerous division in handicaps, was Seaton-
dale, a rank outsider, who came ninth with 7st. lib.
After the mob of 22 that raced for the gilded
Doncaster, the little bunch of seven that came out
for the St. Leger mile and three-quarters looked
ridiculously small. Wolawa, the son of Wallace,
by the Infanta, with Bst. 101 b. up, was made fav-
orite very promptly at five to four against. Barios
was second favorite at nine to four against. The
only other to attract coin xvas Harpist—the rest
were not seriously considered. Barios flashed to
the front, accompanied bv Cagou, and stayed there
till they entered the. straight side by side. Behind,
the thunder of Wolawa’s hoofs gave warning that
he was coming. At the distance Cagou fell back, and
Wolawa, gathering speed as he flew, raced home a
l°ngth to the good, in 3min. 6jsec. Cagou was four
lengths behind Barios, who had sparkled consider-
ably. The time was 3-Jsee. better than Jacamar’s
last year, and has been beaten only four times
since 1890. The—record run for the race was
Cadonia’s 3min. 4fsec. in 1911.
Everyone expected to see Piastre do something
this meeting, but ouite a lot of people apparently
did not suspect that he would do it in the Autumn
Stakes, which had only £lOOO of added money.
Otherwise Patrick Iscariot would never have laid
seven to four against him. And yet it is in
w.f.a. events that Piastre has come so suddenly to
the front this season. Probably the presence of
such horseflesh as Hallowmas, Eudorus and that
capable but cranky steed Cadonia, who always has
big possibilities about him, threw Piastre some-
what in the shade. Still, he had some claim to be
called favorite, with Aurofodina and Eudorus close
up. Eudorus, Jacamar and Ladv Medallist accom-
panied Piastre round the field till the home bend
was reached, where HalloWmas came up with ffriri
and raged like the heathen in suite of his sanctified
name. But Piastre had tens of steam in reserve,
and hounded awav. while Hallowmas left the strug-.
gling Jacamar behind, and went after the fleeting
son of Positano. - A hopeless chase. Piastre won
as he liked, with the great imported gee-gee four
lengths behind, in 2rnin. 35Jsee.
• * * * *
The Sydney Cup demonstrated what most
followers of horseflesh have long suspected, that
Cadonia, when he feels inclined, can travel like
Satan in pursuit of a fat Wowser soul when he feels
inclined. The trouble apparently is to get the noble
animal to feel that way. This" was the 48th Cup,
and the 3min. 28isec. put up by Cadonia has been
beaten only twice—by Wakeful in 1902, when that
wonderful mare did it in 3min. 28sec. neat; and by
Tartan in 1905, when that noble little prad flew
over the distance in 3min. 27sec. Wakeful carried
9st, 71b., Tartan 7st. 131 b., and Cadonia 9st. 7 lib.
The £5OOO added money drew a field of 27, and
there were chances of some beautiful bumping.
Cadonia got a bad one, which shook his jockey up,
but he stuck tp the job and made a magnificent
finish, winning? by half a length from Cagou, with
Jacamar i. third. Cagou and Jacamar both ran a
splendid race, and it was when the issue seemed to
swing between Cagou and Jacamar that Cadonia, who
had been forcing his way through the field, came
with a terrific burst of speed and overwhelmed them
both. Hallowmas, who had been made favorite at 5
to 2 against, finished in the ruck, without eyer hav-
ing shown any symptoms of being a winner. Other
public fancies were Barios, at 6 to 1 against; and
Moorila. at 8 to 1 against. Cadonia, in the public
estimation, was a rank outsider, and went out at
anything from 25’s to 50’s. It w 7 as a sore smack to
the public.
< * * *
“Flat”: Wherefore all this talk of “sur-
prises” over the victories of Jolly Beggar and Cado-
nia in the Doncaster and Sydney Cups respectively?
The Newmarket Handicap and Australian Cup sup-
plied reliable form for the punter to lay hold upon
had he the wit to do it. Jolly Beggar and Cadonia
filled second places in the big events down south,
and, while the increased weight hindered the New-
market winner, Relievo, the Australian Cup winner
was withdrawn frofn the tussle for the Sydney uten-
sil. There has been no “form” which pointed so
glaring^ 7 to what might happen for many and many
a day. Still, the sapient punters could not see it;
neither could the tipsters, and Jolly Beggar was
allowed to go begging at 12’s, while Cadonia was
not even asked for. Verily the way of the punter is
hard and his sight dim.
* * * *
“0.K.”: If the .Doncaster had been run
at Flemington, the starting price of Jolly Beggar
would not have been eight to one after opening at
14 to one on the course. It would probably have
ranged from sevens down to fours. On his performance
in the Newmarket he was the pick of the handicap.
The judge placed him two lengths behind Relievo
in passing the post, but the general impression was
that he nearly caught the leader. Anyhow, the
difference between the six furlongs sprint and the
Doncaster mile seemed to be all in favor of Jolly
Beggar. The Westralian nag ranks as a stayer.
At Flemington, when he just missed the big
coup, he was running a lot short of his distance,
and from a Victorian and Westralian point of view
he was a good thing for the Doncaster if the stable
backed him. The fact that Hartfell started favorite
meant that a great army of local punters had seen
Merton’s horse win the Epsom in the spring, and
the books had got him heavily backed with Hallow-
mas for the double. As for the Sydney fancy for
Cider, on his A.J.O. Derby form, it seemed absurd to
Victorian punters. Cider was doing very well up
to the time he got an attack of colic, after which
he was foolishly raced for the V.R.C. Leger, and
was probably weakened for an indefinite period. In
any case he would have had no visible pretensions
to beat Jolly Beggar at a difference of 4lb. only.
A somewhat amazing feature of the A.J.C.
first day was the stampede out of the Sires’ Produce
Stakes. On Thursday morning somewhere about 20
were in the book, but by four o’clock that afternoon
all but four had faded out, paying a fiver apiece to
avoid having a share of the £2OOO subsidy. A
remarkable instance of a general public movement.
Last year saw a dozen compete. Beragoon was an
odds-on favorite at once, 11 to 8 being the quota-
tion. Radnor was second favorite at 3 to 1 against,
and he justified the faith in him by finishing with
the favorite’s nose at his girth, having covered the
seven furlongs in lmin. 281 sec.
* » * *
Something will have to be done to dis-
courage the papers from publishing pictures of
horse races taken at right angles to the course. The
animals are not horses in the picture; they are
dromedaries, bunyips, or prehistoric humorosities.
And any jockey has a first-class claim for damages
for being represented in the awful positions in which
the alleged pictures show him. Some day a man
suffering a recovery will gaze upon one of these
wondergraphs and go mad or fall down dead, and
his family will recover millions in damages.
* * * *
The conduct of the back-benchers at Syd-
ney Stadium on Saturday night, when a Tasmanian
and a Westralian fought for the resident light-
weight championship of Australia, suggests that a
new official is urgently required. For some reason
the back-benchers took a sudden fanev to Alfred
Morey, the Westralian, and just as sudden a dislike
to Jack Read, the Tasmanian holder of the title.
It expressed its prejudices by hooting Read vigor;
ouslv if he did well. It hooted him if he did
nothing. It hooted him for being on the face of the
earth. On the other hand, Morey had only to wipe
his nose to cause the w 7 elkin to be split by bellow-
ing cheers. He was cheered when he sat down, he
was cheered when he stood ud, he was cheered when
he landed wallops, he was frantically cheered when
he failed. Once he hitched his pants and the roof
rose. This sort of insanitv calls for the appointment
of a number of kickers, clad in mail and armed with
metal boots. Apart from this unpleasantness the
fight was an interesting bout, fought in ding-dong
style. Read was as dogged as ever, and played his
own style of the game to perfection. His trouble
was that his style was not the sort to cope with
such an opponent, who was elusive—almost evanes-
cent, in fact—-and capable of hitting with equal
force with either hand. In plain English, Read was
up against a better boxer, and the rest was equal.
And, naturally, the extra quality of the Westralian’s
boxing won the fight. He was not always elusive,
however, and it _ was quite common for Read, ex-
pecting to see him fade out of reach in tantalising
stvle, to come with a fearful rush, only to find that
Morey was standing his ground and poking out an
unerring left to meet that rush. That sort of thing
hurt. Read landed some heaw body blows, but
Morey’s condition was just perfect; and, anyhow,
he managed to do the swin'rin'r-door act in most of
such occurrences. Yes, undoubtedly Morey was the
better boxer.
The large crowd at the Summers-Picato
fight in Sydney Stadium on Easter Monday night
saw a very hard-fought, heavy-punched battle come
to a sudden and almost sensational end. The sad-
eyed, handsome Americo-Italian lad proved a
tougher nut to crack than the hard-hitting Johnny
Summers expected. Not that Summers ever under-
rates anybody; but Picato is a borer of the most
virulent order, and he takes punishment like a
statue—just bores and bores and bores till the op-
portunity comes, and then.... crash ! On Monday
night he bored, and landed blow after blow that
shook Summers severely, and forced the gritty
little Englishman to provide special defence for his
midriff. All the time Summers landed repeatedly
on the American’s faoe and head. Picato merely
shook his top-piece and came on again. Summers
wisely backed away, and hit hard as he backed.
But there were times when the ropes prevented him
backing any further, and then there was awful
slogging. This happened in the third round, when
Picato banged and smashed so that for a moment
it looked as though Summers would go. But he
blocked and hung on, and got through. In the
fifth he came again in deadly style, using his left
with destructive effect. Picato still bored in, and
got nasty punches for doing it; but he went on bor-
ing and driving Summers backwards. The end
came in the middle of the 19th. Picato glided in,
and hit and hit. Summers was at last hurled
against the ropes. But, like Grenville, of the Re-
venge, he was full of sting. As he rebounded from
the ropes his left drive caught Picato fair on the
chin, and the smite was as the smite of a hammer.
Picato fell. At eight he rose, dazed- Summers
came like a whirlwind and heat him to the floor
once more; then the voice of Authoritv said,
“Stop!” and Summers had won his first fight of
the tour.
* * * *
As might have been expected, Les O’Don-
nell, the bull-dog, is far from satisfied with the end-
ing of his terrific scrap with Dave Smith. He de-
clares he was just picking up nicely, and beginning
to feel a warm glow of anticipation, having realised
that Smith had shot his bolt and the sting had left
his wallops. But for the police interference, he be-
lieves he would have won. Perhaps! But Tammy
Burns said the same after his scran with Johnson had
been stopped just in time to save him having his
head knocked clean over the gasometer outside the
Stadium. When we’re in good nick, we all feel that
the sledgehammers of the day before yesterday were
mere thistledown.
The last heard of Jim Barry, the man
who made the sudden exodus from the ring at Bris-
bane after Langford had placed one on his ribs, was
a letter from Lewisham Hospital to the well-known
sporting writer, W. F. Corbett. It was a rambling
letter in which the evanescent pug declares that “no
one knows how 1 have suffered.” He denies that he
fooled the public, but in the next line admits that
he was quite unfit to enter the ring, and wanted to
call the bout off, but was persuaded to go on. Then
he remarks that he may pull through with the help
of God, and that he only went into the ring because
he had an aged mother to support, and could will-
ingly do it on one leg when she requires cash —-pro-
vided, presumably, he got his money in advance. As
a letter writer Barry is worse than as a boxer, and
Heaven knows that’s about the limit. He doesn’t say
what is wrong with him —whether he has a broken
rib, or is trying the rest cure, or suffering from cold
feet. * * *
The two colored brethren, Samivel Lang-
ford and Samivel McVea, fought a 20-round mill at
Brisbane on Saturday night, and had a merry time
of it. It was not 'the gory affair that their last
meeting in Sydney was, when McVea literally dripped
blood all over the place, and every blow Langford
struck sent red spray over the disgusted pressmen.
In fact, for a while McVea held his own and made
thing's more or less lively for the sawn-off one, so
that at the finish the referee said it was a draw.
The Sydney-to-Melbourne Motor-Car Reli-
ability Non-stop Test has drawn 24 entrants —15
from N.S.W. and nine from Victoria. To the last
event of the kind, held in November, 1905, Victoria
sent 16 cars, N.S.W. but four, and South Aus.
three. It was a terribly drawn-out tussle. Only
four dropped out on the run to Sydney, and of the
other 19 no less than seven secured full points. A
further deciding run was held from Sydney to Med-
low and back, another 132 miles, and only one car
(N.S.W.) lost points, leaving six Victorians still
dead level. Then they ran back to Melbourne in
four stages. One car got smashed up and five re-
mained still dead level. They agreed to remain
level. They were W. R. Grimwade, W. B. Wilkin-
son, H. Tarrant, Syd Day and G. Hobbs.
The mixture supplied by the London
Coupe and Motor-cab Co. to its drivers at per
gallon was not benzol, after all, but a mixture of
paraffin and petrol, which it has discovered will yield
a mileage equal to that of petrol. A lot of engineers
are experimenting with a carburation device to pro-
perly vaporise kerosene, and so end the incessant
struggle with the petrol bill.
* * * *
J. Bolger, of Wagga, who won the Dun-
lop gold watch by slipping across from Melbourne
to Sydney in 23hr. 41min., is to be, or has been by
this time, presented by his club with a gold badge.
The Sydney Bicycle and Motor Cycle Club is mighty
proud of Bolger’s performance, which certainly was
a great one, and it is a fine idea to institute a
sort of Order of Derring-do. If it is kept up, the
chances are the club will suddenly become famous.
It wilj *tso probably bury a few members; but that’s
all in the game—the motor-cycling game.
* * * *
H. W. Nesbit, a well-known Victorian
racing cyclist, has taken on an easy task—the
lowering of old “Plugger Bill” Martin’s Australian
one hour motor-paced bicycle record. Bill put up
31 miles 1425 yards, which is quite antiquated.
The world’s best was put up by Guiernard (France)
at Munich, where the handsome Frenchman with the
ugly name ran 63 miles 255 yards in 1909.
* * * *
Some German with a name quite impossi-
ble to spell, has been testing lubricating oil as
applied to motors and motor cycles. He was
anxious to eliminate the perfume, and discovered
that by adding a little aceton to the lubricant the
value of the oil was quite unimpaired, but when it
burned it emitted no odor. Probably the next mail
will bring particulars of the quantity to' be used,
and then we shall have no need to suffer the tor-
tures of the lost when our best friends waltz past
us in their car.
* * * *
The march of the motor. Last week some-
one made an official count of the vehicles that
passed along St. Kilda-road during 24 hours. Horse-
drawn contraptions totalled 1500; motor-propelled
vehicles 1100. Of course, St. Kilda-road is the
best and most popular short run for cars around
Melbourne; so the figures are of very narrow appli-
cation. The proportion wouldn’t hold good on any
other track in the State.
* * * #
Maoriland and N.S.W. Rugby Unions
having patched up their squabble, friendly visits
will be resumed in the coming winter. The Ma State
team will drop in on Maoriland in August, and
Maoriland will repay the call the following season.
Also, a Maori team has been handed permission to
assault the gate money to the uttermost at Sydney,
and will commence on June 2. It sounds like a frag-
ment of the sporting intelligence of the years agone.
* * * *
When Orange meets Green on the football
field things are apt to become like a foretaste of
Sheol. On November 16 of last year Linfield met
Belfast Celtic in the presence of 10,000 spectators.
The barrackers did their share by firing revolvers in
the air and singing “Gor’ Save,” the “Wearin’ o’ the
Green” and other ditties. Several hundreds of police
patrolled the ground. Nothing more dangerous really
happened than the defeat of Belfast Celtic by four
goals to nil.
* * * *
The few Victorians who stayed on in
Adelaide for the lawn tennis championships had a
profitable week. South Aps. is, like the Englishmen,
great in singles, but no good in doubles. R, V. Thomas
easily put out Wertheim (Vic.), and Justin O’Dea
licked Raws (Vie.), while none of them could ex-
tend Roy Taylor in the challenge round. Playing
together, however, Raws and Wertheim were well
ahead of Taylor and Thomas and all other pairs. The
superiority of Melbourne’s ladies made the Mixed
Doubles one-sided. Two Victorian pairs contested
the final, won by Wertheim and Miss Watson from
Raws and Mrs. McArthur. Miss Waxman won the
Ladies’ championship. A good volleyer, with a
variety of strokes—and incidentally very good look-
ing, and only 19-—-she should have a big" future.

There has been a slight alteration in Davis
Cup arrangements as far as Australia is concerned.
Wilding, now in England, has cabled that he won’t
he available so Horace Rice (N.S.W.) will make
the trip. Thus Ma supplies all the players. Rice
and Jones will leave by the Ventura on April 5 for
America, where the preliminary round is to be
fought. Doust, the third man, will be waiting for
them. While they are visiting Unc. Sam a series
of matches will be held against various Yankee
teams, with a vietv to fattening the association’s
purse. The big match is down for the middle of
June.

Champion swimmer Longworth’s monopolv
of State and club championships is growing so
stupendous that he will soon come under the opera
tion of the Anti-Trust law. Last week the 100
yards and the 440 yards of Eastern Suburbs (Syd-
ney) were thrown into his treasure chest; the ioo
yards in 58 l-ssec. and the 440 yards in sniin
58 3-ssec. Which are both worthy efforts.
* * * *
The bad old custom of filling-up the bookies
with fizz at the expense of the owner of a winning horse
was observed with special absurdity at the Victorian
Club the other Monday. Messrs. Baillieu, the owners
of Almissa, shouted the wine, but didn’t come along
to help drink it. A bookmaker, in proposing their
health, stated that “the members of the ring, as
a body, did not begrudge the Messrs. Baillieu 'their
victory,” which strikes one as a choice example of
the piffle talked on these occasions. Why, in the
name of almighty Odds, should the bo'oks begrudge
any particular owner a victory, except on the under-
standing that they would prefer every blessed horse
to get beaten? After this patronising announcement
of the ring's feelings, Mat Ellis responded on behalf
of the Baillieus, and proceeded to chant their praises.
Forgetting that he was merely their mouthpiece, and
not their advocate, he described them as “the best
sportsmen in Australia.” But why should any race-
horse owner —good, bad or indifferent—be expected
to pay a tax, in the form of costly liquor, on his
rare occasional winnings? The only people who
stand to win on every year’s transactions are the
books, who never, by anv chance, call the losing
owners together and invite them to drown their
sorrows in champagne.
The Australian Garrison (Sydney Divi-
sion) is nursing what looks like a world’s big-gun
record. A heaving target was towed out through
the Heads, and the quick-firers placed the whole
bundle of 21 shots into the object in lmin. 33sec.
Nothing like it has been done on this station be-
fore, and it proves that Australian gunners, given
the wherewithal, will prove ugly customers in a
big-gun scrap.
It cost Essendon £1678 to win the Vic.
League ’oofball premiership last season, though Col-
lingwood won it two years before on about half that
sum. The £1678 included players’ wages, £773
(over £2 per Saturday per man) ; trainers and
coach, £244; medical fees and sea baths, £164;
players’ refreshments, £95; insurance, £7O; testi-
monials and . wedding presents, £110; certificates,
medals, etc., £4B; uniforms and boots, £80;
allowances and holiday trips, £94. After that, it is
not surprising to learn that the committee finished
the season £52 in debt, expenditure being £2132
and receipts only £2OBO.

From the Liberal presentation of the case
against the Referenda, as put forward in
the pamphlet issued under authority:—
Momentous public issues are involved. This is
an appeal of citizens to fellow citizens, in their
common interests and tor the sake of Australia,
against the rash, reckless and unreasonable wreck-
ing of the Federal Constitution, which must result
unless the people’s answer is a resounding “No.”
One would think that the Labor party pro-
posed to chip something off the Fed-
eral power, instead of which it is pro-
posed to increase the powers of the Austra-
lian people—the verv people to whom this
fool-appeal is made. The Liberals are evi-
dently quite convinced that the pedple are
not to be trusted. Yet Joe Cook says he is
voing to call himself for the future a
Democrat.
“Scrutineer’s” grievance:—
Th e wages paid to election officials by the Home
Affairs Department are about the limit in clerical
sweating. The “day” (vide regulation 13) runs
trom 7 a.m. to 12 p.m.—an eight-hour day with
nine hours overtime. For this double day a door-
keeper gets 10s., or 7d. per hour. An ordinary poll-
hours) gets 15s. a scrutineering poll-
clerk (17 hours) gets a guinea, a presiding officer
two guineas, and an assistant returning-officer three
guineas.- A returning-officer gets 10s. per week for
routine work, and from £25 to £3O for the six to
eight weeks’ work involved, in an election. An
electoral registrar gets from £3, the minimum for
a 1000-voter poll, rising -by--steps of ss. for each ad-
ditional 100 voters, till the £l5 maximum is
reached. Keeping all the claims, removals, transfers
and alterations, checking, dating and acknowledging
same, and writing them into a 10,000 or 12.000
roll for £l5 per annum, is sweating of the most
gilt-spurred rooster character.

semi-floating type.

all shafts and gears in the transmission and

all parts requiring special stiffness.

wide seats.

rhyme may pass if the verse carpenter can make it

Fembank: “Caution” might be tremendously amus-
ing at the local store; but this isn’t the local store.
The Bulletin has little use for stuff that can’t be
read with about equal interest at Thargomindah and
Thursday Island. ... Gas: Distinct traces of humor in
“Methods of Prayer” ; but the work is uneven in
quality.... Square Vic.: Inspected fore and aft, and
no good thing found in it. .. .Banger: This paper
doesn’t fling promiscuous bricks at Private Enter-
prise. It doesn’t even throw promiscuous bricks at
combines. It believes, in fact, that a vast amount
of waste results from 30 bread-carts tearing along
one street to supply the residents thereof when the
job could be all done by one cart. But it realises
that, if one cart is given the job, the public must
be protected against exploitation by a monopolist.
That’s why it is keen on getting for the Common-
wealth power to deal with injurious trusts and com-
bines. The great thing is to get public necessities
and services supplied at the lowest possible price,
with fair pay to the men actually employed ; and,
so far as this paper is concerned, it is a secondary
matter whether the job is done by P.E. or the State.
....Fred F.: We’ve been looking for an epic on the
Founding of the City; and here it comes with the
blessed Bun: —
A weary wanderer seeking toil
Came strolling o’er the virgin soil
Of the newly christened city,
His throat was dry his feet were sore
He’d had no drink for hours before,
The sight would kindle pity,
But his hopes rose high though he was dry.
He came at last on the capital site
Where Gov. and Ministers had their skite,
And though consumed with a mighty thirst
His eyes grew bright and were like to burst
For bottles around him they lay thick
The sole remains of the christening trick ;
His smile grew calm and most serene
When he captured one, not a dead marine;
Says he to himself as he took a pull
A man need have no fear here
For though of water there’s not a drop
It seems a man Can-beer-ere.
. ...H.A.F.: This is a poor market for puns....

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
M.A.T.: As art it is unripe. As a jest it fell off
the tree- many seasons ago, and is no longer market-
able.
A Melbourne weekly makes the brilliant
suggestion that the influx of immigrants to
Australia should be increased a hundred-
fold, and maintained year by year. “Give
us immigrants enough and the rabbit plague
will soon disappear. These new-comers find
bunny very palatable.” And after the over-
whelming horde of workers have eaten the
rabbits they can. of course, return to their
old occupation of living on one another.
An assistant meteorologist at Melbourne
Observatory cut short his career the other
day under circumstances which reflect the
same old discredit on the teaching of sport-
ing melodrama, wherein the hero invariably
averts his impending ruin by backing the
winner for immense sums. The suicide,
who had two summonses weighing on his
mind, wrote to his wife: “Darling, I tried
to get the money, but could not, and as a
last resort went to the races, and, of course,
lost. I thought--you would be better off
without me. I am an absolute rotter.”
Apparently he was sape in all respects save
his estimate of himself. Absolute rotters
never commit suicide. They merely curse
their luck and live on.
“Southern Cross” lodges a complaint:—
It's about time that opposition was shown to
Jokook’s parrot-cry that Labor has introduced the
principle of spoils to the victors, that Labor Minis-
ters openly admitted that they ascertained the
political opinions of highly-placed officials before
they were appointed, that the first bold attempt at
the Americanisation of Australian politics had been
made by Labor, and so on. So far from this being
true, it is notorious that both Federal and State
Labor. Cabinets have had such bad attacks of the
timid feeling that 90 per cent, of all their big ap-
pointments have been given to Liberal officers, often
of the Toriest types. In fact, Labor has been almost
as resolute in placating and pushing Liberal place-
hunters as the most reckless of Liberal Governments.
What makes Jokook’s charge all the more
monstrous is that during all the riotous vears
of Liberal maladministration the system of spoils to
the victors flourished, to the almost total excluding
of even the ablest and most energetic civil servants
who happened to be even unaggressive Laborites. As
for the most capable officers who were militant
Laborites, they were turned down time after time.
“Kidette”: The Vic. Education Depart-
ment is losing a lot of its young male teach-
| ers, who are deserting to join either the
Commonwealth Service or some other branch
of the State Public Service. Over 50 p.c. of
the young fellows' who pass the junior teach-
ers’ exam, can, without extra cramming,
tmss the Public Service clerical exam. In
the Education Department a junior of from
17 to 20 gets for his first three years £4O,
£5O and £6O. provided he can pass his yearly
! exams. And they’re not trifling, so lie has
to study hard all the time. Then, when his
three years’ service is up. he can apply for
a sixth-class school at Lord-Knows-Where,
at a salary of £l2O a year. If, instead of
being so foolish, he gets into the general ser-
! vice, he starts £6O and rises by half-
yearly steps of £10; so at the end of three
years he is receiving £l2o—as much as he
would be eligible for if he were teaching.
And, meantime, he will hove received over £lOO
extra in salary. Moreover, the work is simple
'by comparison with trouser-dusting: promo-
tion is quicker; there ii no night study and
lesson preparations; .and he can mostly live
in civilised country. No wonder, then,
Frank Tate is short of males to fill his
worm-eaten little schools.

The Inky Way.
There are few gold mines on the Inky Way. W. F.
Monypeny, a director and leader-writer of the Times,
and sometime editor of the Johannesburg Star
( £2OOO a year), left an estate worth only £175.
During the last few vears, Monypeny had been writ-
ing the life of Benjamin Disraeli, Earl of Beacons-
field, and the second volume came out a few days
before his death. Another small will of interest to
inky-wayfarers is that of Emma Lavinia Hardy, wife
of Thomas Hardy, the novelist. She left only £2lB.
And that was above the average. Many writers only
leave the gas burning and the dog in the backyard.
It is a beautiful thing to help the widow and
comfort the fatherless; but there is nothing in it if
your trade competitor gets as much advt. out of it
as you do. The Sydney papers wept gallons of tears
over brave Captain Scott and poor Lady Scott, and
sent round the hat with enthusiasm to aid the relief
fund. And every day the S. M. Herald said what
its own fund came to, and the Gaud Mayor’s fund
and the Chamber of Commerce fund, and the fund
that is being raised in Britain; but never a word
did it mention of the rival Daily Tele-
graph’s fund, the Daily Telegraph being
a paper which, having no bowels of compassion,
could never, of course, be advertised as responsible
for a charitable fund. And morning by morning the
Daily Telegraph printed its list, and the Mayor’s
list and the various other lists —always, however,
excluding the S. M. Herald’s, lest that reptile con-
temporary should share a little of the limelight.
That, you see, is all to prove how the S. M. Herald
and Daily Telegraph really admire unselfishness,
and how utterly disinterested they were in pouring
out their gallons of hysterics and whooping up
public sympathy.

The Coolgardie Miner (W.A.) is undergoing
another editorial clean-up. This time “Bullant”
Mclntyre, of Southern Cross, is to go and guide the
destiny of the sheet. In the early days a man would
have "been laughed down in derision had he dared to
suggest that the day would come when it would
take the camp all its time to run a local rag. But
the old champagne town is down to beer, and rather
stale beer and not much of it at that. The new
proprietary is secretaried by Holman, M.L.A., who
was Minister for Railways in the previous Labor
Government.
“Quarter Single”: So many of the members of
the Victorian Ministry have been seeking new nests
lately, that the following announcement in the
Argus comes,,as no surprise:—-
“The State Cabinet yesterday decided to continue
the egg-laying competitions at Burnley.”
Alec Peacock will probably not compete. He may
be engaged merely to do the cackling.
* * * *
Howard Willoughby, the lovable, who has passed
from the Argus to the asphodel, had a habit of
stacking on the bookshelves, behind the editorial
chair, such volumes as were forwarded for the ten-
der mercies of the reviewers. One of the books,
having been dealt with, found its way back to the
shelf. It concerned the management of Sunday
schools, and the reviewer did not want to be in-
criminated by retaining it in his possession. Now,
there was a godly youth in the office, who was much
interested in Mothers’ Guilds, Leagues of the Cross,
Time and Talents Clubs, Reapers and Binders’
Unions, Gleaners’ Bands, and such. He laid eyes
upon the book, and the commandment about covet-
ing received a compound comminuted fracture.
When Willoughby arrived in the office, the youth,
much in the spirit of Imam Din, khitmutgar, wor-
shipfully soliciting the discarded polo ball from
the Heaven-born, in Kipling’s story, asked the editor
if he might have the book. “C-certainly,” said
Willoughby, with his gentle, stammer. “Y-you are.
I p-presume, g-greatly interested in these things?”
“Very much indeed, sir,” said the sad youth. “Ah,
well,” said Willoughby, as he handed over the book,
“d-don’t worry: y-youTl grow out of it —y’you’ll
g-grow out of it!”

“Rosna” (from London) : Australian writer and
traveller, Mary Gaunt, has packed up her typewriter
and best frock, and gone to call on China ; and here-
after a fat volume on the yellow-man’s acres be
confidently expected. On her way back she will pro-
bably take boat or aeroplane for the Northern Ter-
ritory, and write up her own country for a change.
* * * *
“Blinky”: I was assigned a stunt recently which
must be nearly the limit. An old Maori had died
at his pa ; he just died, and no one worried. It
was summer, which fact probably irritated the
corpse. The whites in the neighborhood heard the
corpse and began to feel suffocated. They drew the
attention of the authorities to the matter; hence
my trip, together with a health inspector. We
had absolutely no difficulty in locating Hori’s abode.
The inspector was provided with a large syringe
and a tin of formalin. - Fighting our way we
reached the door of the whare. Hereabouts _ the
heads of the tribe intruded and asked our business
in quite an unconcerned way. My friend consigned
them in toto to the place where Hori’s soul probably
was, and got his syringe to work on the whare. He
next telephoned to town for a zinc coffin to be ready
in five mihutes. Then the picnic began. He mus-
tered the tribe in the “big house,” produced another
tin of formalin from his trap and set to work. I
worked the pump, and he sprayed the inhabitants
from head to heel. Then the coffin arrived, but I
refused service when it came to transferring Hori
to it. I had had quite enough for one vear. The
inspector commissioned some of the lads to per-
form the ceremony, and they took to it with avidity.
It must have reminded them of dried shark.

“W.”: The Australian Journalists’ Association is
understood to be seriously considering whether it. is
anv longer wise to keen itself aloof from the common
herd of unions. Recent High Court decisions have
fractured the faith of the members in the Federal
Arbitration Court as a reliable shelter in stormv
times, and the ease with wh : ch some daily paper
proprietors manage to repudiate “honorable under-
standings” has made many of them wonder whether
the term “gentleman of the press” really means
much more than the euphemism “ladv-heln.” after
all. Amalgamation with the Typographical Society is
being clamored for by some bold pen-soldiers, while
the advocates of a newspaper employees’ federation
are more numerous than waterholes in the Cotter.

“Digamma”: As an old pressman, anything that
Mark Twain said on the subject of craftsmanship is '
worthy of attention. The most valuable remark he
ever made was this: “As to the adjective—-when in
doubt, strike out!” The sure sign of an unsure
workman is his effort to attain effect bv piling ad:ec-
tive on adjective, especially in headings—“Shoriripg
Accident: Man’s Dreadful Injuries; Ssh l Fatal-
ity.” Headings like these are all too plentiful, and
in the instance given two lines are wasted on adiec-
tives that merely make the reader impatient. “Fatal
Injuries” tells the story—it leaves two lines avail-
able to tell how the accident happened. Restraint
is ten times as effective as splurge, and tragedy is
not rendered more impressive by being bello’wed. The
surest road to grandeur is simplicity. The sub-edi-
torial staff who issued the Authorised Version of the
Bible knew this, and with them the unnecessary
adiective would have had as much chance as a cellu-
loid cat chased bv an asbestos dog through Hell.
* * * *
The Yankee interviewer gets a good word at last.
“I consider the American m'' + t' o d,” says Clara Putt,
“much superior to the English. The average Eng-
lish reporter sits there waiting for you to say some-
thing, until you begin to feel as uncomfortable as
be looks. The American, on the contrary, nuts you
completely at your ease bv doing most of the talk-
ing himself, and all you have to do is to fill in the
conversation.”

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
ntg

and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-

deep “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron ’stirrups,'and double buckle leather girth. The

BRISBANE.
IN
BANANA LAND
“A.E.D.” : Certain fruit merchants, backed
by the Sydney Chamber of Commerce, are
squeezing the Commonwealth Government in
an effort to get it to abolish the import duty
on: bananas, on the pretext that Queensland
cannot produce the fruit in quantity or
quality to supply the Australian trade.
Vefily the Little Australian dies hard.
There are thousands of acres in coastal
Queensland where the banana can be grown
to perfection ; and given a shipping service
equal to the Fijian, with reasonable protec-
tion against the fruit produced by Ram
Bagh and co., the output in the North, could
be trebled, and a decent living offered to a
considerable number of white settlers who
would soon embark in the industry and
sprint for the baby bonus in their spare
time. Under present conditions there is a
bare living for the battler. Abolish the
duty, and the plantations will soon be de-
serted, and a trade witb big possibilities in
it will pass to the foreign nigger.
Lawyer A. J. Thynne ,
M. L. C., Queensland.

MRS. BUSH PUB (to travelling artist): “ An ’ 'ow many o' these pietors can

The' father of the Brisbane City Council,
sturdy John McMaster, has been a member
for 42 years. Things haven't improved
much since he became an alderman, so he
still feels at home. The city has no real
town hall, _ and no sewerage system; its
ferries are in a shocking condition, and its
so-called public baths are shuddersome in-
stitutions.
Rev. Hulton Sams, Bananaland’s boxing
parson, gets on splendidly with the rough-
and-ready bush blokes amongst whom he
labors. But he is heavily handicapped in
one respect. He has, of course, to be his
own choirmaster, and his singing voice is
poor, likewise his ear. Latterly, he was
holding an informal service at a wool scour,
and started his trustful congregation on a
hymn from an impossibly high note. They
struggled along chokingly for a few bars,
then cracked and trailed into an embar-
rassed silence. “That was started too ’igh!”
ventured one' chorister at last. Then a long,
slab-sided Australian who had shrieked his
best and failed, burst out indignantly, “Yers
too blanky ’igh alltergether!” And Sams
said not a reproachful word about profan-
ity, but started them off again from a lower
note, and all was peace and sacred melody.
“S.S.S.” from Far Up:—
, * n r t , hc ', Gul , f . ° f Carpentaria country the medicine-
ien ot the lvoko-niundi peoples are pastmasters at
the sorcery yclept, pointing the bone. The imple-
rnent used is pare of a man’s thigh support, topped
by a bolus composed of human kidney fat and hair.
The spirit of the original owner of these
articles is expectedto get busy with the
blabk magic as the occasion requires. Should
the bone be pointed in the direction of the
victim over running water it merely makes him
very sick or unhappy; when it is pointed over a
“ re ' • kills. I saw a -very old medicine-man put
01 ? e ' °f these uncanny instruments on the ground,
while .he squatted soma yards away and mumbled
incantations. With my own eyes I saw that bone
rise up and bow to the audience thrice. On two other
occasions 1 saw the same thing happen. I’ve seen
other weird performances in Papua. -We had a hos-
pital orderly boy who went mad one day, and,
clad only in his sie-hie, did cannibal dances and
howled a prophecy to the effect that the mail
steamer would arrive next day (it was a very un-
certain vessel to chance a prophecy on) ; that a fat
white medicine man would be a passenger consigned
to the hospital where he would raise plenty of
trouble, and that at a goldfield (500 miles away)
too'many men would “dead.” It all happened just
that way. The mail boat rolled up in the morning,
the fat doctor fulfilled his part in the prophecy of
the mad native, and about 600 boys died on the
fields as the result of an epidemic.
Captain Mackay, Queensland’s harbor
master, told the Pearling Commission that
“diving was no,work for a white man —it
was too cruel.” When asked if it wasn’t
equally cruel for the Papuan natives, he
replied: “The sea is the playground of the
natives.” Times seem to have changed. We
used to be told that the sea was the play-
ground, battlefield and general skittle-alley
of John Bull. Now, however, John is only
expected to play skittles when human life is
not at stake. When life is on the table, the
honor belongs to the noble black person
whom we are “protecting.”

Andrew Fisher and Joe Cook, protagonist
and antagonist, milk and vinegar, had much
in common to begin with. Both started to
earn their living at a very early age.
Both took to Sunday-school attend-
ance, Sunday-school teaching and lay-preach-
ing. Both, crossing the seas to Australia
turned to mining, though Joe soon sought
the easy job of weigher. Both entered State
Parlmments as exponents of Labor ideals
Thereabouts divergences began. But in the
a pMC US - eS both b r ecame Ministers, Cook
tcLm, w a , n OE-Labor Government and
hisher as Works Minister in a Labor one
They entered the Federal House in 1901 as
directly hostile. And both will be Federal
Prime Ministers if Cook’s star looks up bu
it will be a very mad kind of star if it does.

The Wave.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
r fhe sun was lost in your gleaming hair
(Oh. how you stole from the careless sun!)
And the blue of the sea was in your eyes
Where deep the lure of the Lorelei lies.
‘‘One more wave,” you said. ‘‘Just one” —
I lost my will in your sea blue gaze)
“Ihen we go back to the wiser ways!”
One more wave. How it creamed and curled
hroin the sunny side of a sapphire world.
And its spray was pearls from some treasure
cave
As we linked our hands in. . . .that one more
wave!
Last night when the moon had tied Hie sea,
And the mangrove swamp was black.
By the ghostly loom of the unlit tent,
Through the mangrove shadows we silent
went, .
And the wet, uneven track. . . .
Where- a sea boomed on a sleeping
land....
Your petal fingers had found my hand....
“One more wave.” How the red blood beat
Till your life seemed part of my own, my
sweet!
Never a thought of to-day we gave
As we drowned our souls in that one more
wave.
Queensland.
M. FORREST.

The Indooroopilly tennis club tournament
on Monday drew a fair number to Auchen-
flower. Jacky Radcliffe (the Youngest
player to shake an inter-State racquet for
Queensland) and his sister carried off the hi?
prize.
The races on St. Paddy’s Day didn’t
bring out anything extra-noticeable in the
way of frocks. Mrs. Cecil Palmer wore an
imported garment of black ninon. and the
other really smart woman on the lawn was
Mrs. .Eneas McDonnell, of Toowoomba?
who was a walking demonstration of the
uses of spongecloth and spotted ninon.
The argument about the meaning of the
word ‘Canberra” reminds me of a man who
used; to vow that he spoke every aboriginal
dialect in Queensland. Once, at the blacks’
camp at Myora, I heard an aged Binghi give
Ins free and candid opinion of the ambitious
linguist: “Missa Jones come down here, we
gammon we understand ; yabber vabber.’ We
make yabber yabber, he gammon he under-
stand.” And the whole dusky crowd roared
with laughter. The point of which is that
it is useless listening to the blather of a
nock of newspaper correspondents who
know almost as much about Binghi’s language
as they do about the stone-age language of
the Eskimo. The one man whose remarks
can be relied on concerning the meaning of
Canberra is Archibald Meston, Banana-
land’s tourist ambassador at Sydney. He
says the word means “young jackass.” For
myself, I can't say anything as to the
young, but I’m willing to risk a pair of
gloves about the jackass. There is a town-
ship north of Brisbane named Nanago, and
for long the alleged aborigine experts argued
as to the correct interpretation of the word.
All sorts of romantic suggestions were made,
until one day a well-known local native con-
fided that the word was the nearest black
brother could get to the pronunciation of
William Goat’s sister.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIAN JANE,
The alien hotel-help question is still alive
in the Far North. Lately a Church of Eng-
land parson stayed at an Irvinebank pub
which employs alien help. Next day he
received a letter telling him that if he stayed
there again his church would be “listed.”
Which reminds this paper to ask, again, how
soon the unions are going to “list” their own
members in western towns where the Chinese
are piling up fortunes out of the custom of
unionists’ wives.
The directors of the Sydney Ferry cos.
reached the top-note of even their impudence
on Monday. They apparently hadn’t done
a thing to provide the public with the service
they had taken money-in-advance for; and
when the Government started to get moder-
ately busy they proposed to lease the State
their boats, provided that the Government
undertook not to employ so much as one of
the men who had gone on strike. That
ought to open the eyes of Sydney to the
sort of men they are in whose hands the
public trusts the ferry traffic; and if the
public doesn't insist oh a change, it de- ,
serves all the inconvenience and fleecing it
will get in the future. For the co. itself to
refuse to employ any of the men would have
been bad enough ; that it should attempt to
pass the curse on to another party to whom
it proposed to merely charter its vessels was
too contemptible for words.

Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Treasury,
Melbourne, 28th December, 1913.
COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£lOO Reward.
WHEREAS certain imitations of silver
coins have recently been passed into
circulation at Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales : Notice is hereby ~iven that a
reward of One hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth
for information leading to the apprehension
of the coiners and the discovery of the plant
used in the manufacture of the coins referred
to. The Commonwealth Government will
request the State Government to extend a
full pardon to any accomplice who will give
the desired information. This offer will
remain open for thre months from this
date.
ANDREW FISHER,
Treasurer.
KALGOORLIE TO PORT AUGUSTA
RAILWAY.
QUOTATIONS will be received by the
Engineer-in-Chief for Commonwealth
Railways. 84 William Street, Melbourne,
up to APRIL 3, 1913, for either of the fol-
lowing services in connection with the
strengthening and renewal of the Port
Augusta Wharf:—
Supply and Delivery of Wharf Timber.
Supply and Delivery of Wrought-iron
Bolts, Nuts and Plates.
Particulars are obtainable at the office bf
the Lngineer-in-Chief for Commonwealth
Railways, 84 William Street, Melbourne;
the Supervising Engineer, Port Augusta;
and the Commonwealth Works Registrar
Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth and
Hobart.
The successful tenderer will be required
to lodge a deposit of p.c.
KING O’MALLEY,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.

DUMB HOPPER BARGES.

conditions of tendering), until
Twelve Noon on MONDAY, APRIL 14
Barges f ° r SUpply of Six H °PPer
Drawings and specifications may be keen
at the office of the Director of Navy Coil-
tracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; or at the
Naval Staff Office, Sydney, Brisbane, Largs
Bay (S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
Copies may be obtained on payment of a
deposit of £5 each, which will be refunded
on receipt of a bona-fide tender, and the
return of the documents.
Tenders to be endorsed, “Tender for
Hopper Barges,” on the top left-hand corner
of the envelope, and addressed (if by post,
prepaid), to the Director of Navy Con-
tracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; or to the
Director of Navy Contracts, care of Dis-
tnct Naval Officer, Sydney, Brisbane, Largs
Bay (S.A.), Fremantle or Hobart.
The lowest or any tender will not neces-
sarily be accepted.
Tenders in all cases to be on the printed
form, which can be obtained as above.

The Seven Sisters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He. lives his life who dreams his dream
instead.
Full overhead
-The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went out Beside a still lagoon
I saw her stand enamored. Never moon
Had poured that glory in her eyes, I swear-
Nor could the Seven Pleiades compare
With all the starlight squandered on her
hair.
I stood in wonder and as one enthralled
’The while she beckoned to me, called and
called
With such a voiceful silence, never sound
Was heard like it to make so rich a wound
Within a heart whose throbbings only sought
To cease upon the rapture that she wrought.
She beckoned to me but I could not move
Seeing her only by that still lagoon,
Too troubled at the tender gleam of love
That promised me so sumptuous a boon.
Alas for me! Alas! The vision fled.
Full overhead
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went out Attired as a bride
Came one to meet me, riding down a plain
Of endless yellow sand. Her bridle rein
Hung idle by her, needing not to guide
The thing that bote her, with a spotted
hide
Like to a leopard’s and a lion’s mane.
Or what it was or who was- she or why
They came, I knew not, wishing but to die.
For verily was hunger in her mouth.
And verily, a fearful hate had she
And in its swollen tongue I read a drouth.
Licking those famished jaws so fearfully!
Nearer they came towards me, and their
breath
Scorched on my flesh as hurtled out of hell,
And I, who had not quailed to grapple
death,
Felt hope within me shudder at the spell.
Ah, well for me it was The vision fled.
Full overhead
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went out Beneath the umber
shade
Of dark-green wattles, dropping globes of
gold
In scented largesse o’er her, sat a maid
To pain the eyes, so dazzlingly arrayed
In silvern garments was she, shot with gold
To match her crimson braided hair, un-
rolled
In golden billows o’er her swelling vest.
With rippled, girly laughter, she caressed
The heap of golden tokens at her side.
And taking tiny fistfuls, held them up
In both her hands, togethered as a cun
Laughing the more to see the spoilage glide
Between her fingers with a clinking rush
That startled all the silence of the bush.
But when she spied me, witness of her play,
Her laughter stayed to wreathe into a smile
Of sweetest welcome, knowing no dismay,
Bidding me dally at her side a while.
Alas for me! Alas!....The vision fled.
Full overhead
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went out. . . .Was ever fairer grace
Than hers, who sprawled upon a poppied
lawn
In indolent delighting, all her face
Glowing with suppliance for a passion-
space?
Pink was her flesh as though a summer
dawn
Had joyed to make her nakedness divine
With rosy tinting; from her heavy lips
There came a sweetness that, whoever sips,
Is drunken with desire. Like a wine
That makes the blood in wanton tumult
flow
Througli all the veins, her eyes had magic
skill
To bring who looked upon them to her will,
And give them sweet carousal in the arms
That longed to bourgeon lovers with their
charms.
Alas for me ! Alas ! The vision fled.
Full overhead
The sun was hug#, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went 0ut.... A thunder tumult
crashed
Through all the heights of Heaven and the
glare
Of vivid lightnings yellowed all the air,
As, mounted on a coal black stallion, dashed
A maid whose eyes and golden tresses spilled
A glory on the darkness. Sword upheld,
She shouted and the thunders made reply,
As though exulting at her battle ery.
She shouted, and no other thing I sought
(For this the spell upon me that she
wrought)
But follow, follow, follow in her wake,
And, gluttonous for slaughter, lull the ache
That pained me bitter for her wild eyes
sake.
Alas for me! Alas ! The vision fled.
Full overhead
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went 0ut....1n meek and holy
wise,
Kneeling and with her frail hands clasped
before,
I saw one lift her reverential eyes
Towards a silver crucifix that bore
The crimson-sided, thorn-crowned form of
Him
Who died for Ilis ideal in the dim
Grey mists of early legend. Softly on
Her kneeling grace the candelabras
streamed
A flood of golden glory, till it seemed
As though her urgent soul were peering
through
The body’s gaoling. Deeper, deeper grew
The ardors of her vision, till she made,
Whiles as her body in its raptus swayed,
The airs in waves pulse from her, over-
weighed , ,
With fumes of heavy incense, and her eyes
Glowed with a flame, so rich her ecstasies.
Alas for me! Alas! The vision fled.
Full overhead , , ,
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red.
The sun went 0ut... -Great mountains,
blacl: as ink,
Were all around us, poignarding the sky,
n hose starry m.llions looked on me, to
blink,
In fell derision, each a bloodshot eye.
iNU) fear was mine, for who need be afraid
’’ aea , ev Fy cherished thing has taken
flight?
She only, on whose hollow lap I laid
My teeming head, had banished all delight
N\ ith it, the lethe draught that I had
sipped
At her commanding, grateful for the boon,
lo noise of falling waters did she croon
rn2 me Wllaby to ease the soul’s unrest
JhaT long had troubled all my lonely years.
V was I knew her calm was best
“ nd what a healing was there in the tears
Bhe dropped for ever, ever on my heart,
there in the silence, from the world apart.
If overhead
The sun was huge, a sullen clot of red,
I knew not and I cared not, being dead.
Dreaming a dream I died a death instead.
QUEENSLAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
Parvey the Philosopher.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PARVEY was a small, thin, sallow,
wispy-bearded man who regarded
himself as a devil of a fellow amongst
women. The women and girls who dealt at
his little read.v-cash grocery shop in a Mel-
bourne suburb humored his harmless delu-
sion. As he passed them pounds of sugar
or tea, or pints of treacle or kerosene,
across the counter, he winked with amorous
facetiousness, and they replied with coy
smiles or eloquent nods. To them it was
merely fun, and, now and then, overweight
or good measure. To Parvey it was Glory.
Every night when he went home to Mrs.
Parvey—they lived in a private cottage uot
far from the shop—he told her of the con-
quests he had achieved during the day, and,
figuratively speaking, laid them at her feet.
His idea in doing so was to make her realise
the magnitude of her good fortune in having
all to herself such an irresistible Lothario
as he (Parvey) could be if he liked to let
himself go. Rut Mrs. Parvey knew the
amiable little braggart too well to be much
impressed by these stories. Sometimes,
however, she would affect to be furiously
jealous, and refuse to be placated until the’
secretly-delighted Parvey had bought her
another ring, or an expensive bangle, .or,
maybe, a gold necklet. In this way she
managed to acquire a considerable quantity
of jewellery—over £BO worth, in fact. It
was in the boom' time, and Parvey was
doing w r ell. He had married his ideal woman
and adored her.
Mrs. Parvey was a florid-complexioned,
full-figured, full-blooded, reddish-haired,
light-eyed female with a head like a cat.
Parvey was not her ideal man, although she
had married him. But he gave her plenty
of money to spend, dressed her well, and
was her slave in all Things. Consequently
she despised him. He had, at times, a dim
suspicion of this, but his vanity always
brushed the doubt aside. “Julia knows when
she’s well off,” he used to say to himself on
such occasions. This was true enough in
the main. Julia Parvey was no fool. But
almost always, to a woman of her tempera-
ment, a day comes when passion strangles
judgment. And then curious things are
liable to happen.
When this day of days came to Julia
Parvey, she opened to it as a rose opens to
the sun.
• Bovis was the very antithesis of Parvey.
He (Bovis) was a thick-set, bull-necked
young butcher, with curly hair and a sen-
sual mouth. He was understood to be in a
good way of business, though he had not
been long in the suburb. It was impossible
for a woman liae Julia Parvey—who began
to deal at his shop—to look with cold in-
difference on this superb male animal, over-
flowing with vitality as vigorous as her
own. As for Bovis, he fell in love with his
handsome customer the first time he saw
her. It was a case of mutual swift attrac-
tion of red blood-corpuscles, In affairs of
the heart of this sort the march is rapid.
One day in taking change from Bovis Mrs.
Parvey absent-mindedly left her plump
bediamonded hand in his for a moment. He
boldly squeezed it. She glanced at him re-
proachfully. He laughed. They understood
each other.
Bovis became so infatuated as to be in-
cautious and compromising. He wrote
letters to her. Now any Christian reader
who has ever engaged in an amorous in-
trigue knows that it is fatal folly to set
down hot, passionate words, or even warm
terms of endearment, in black ink on cold
white paper. But Bovis was nothing to
boast of as a Christian, and, even if he had
been, was too much in love to count conse-
quences. Mrs. Parvey found it necessary
to remonstrate with him. He heard her
out, and answered (in a masterful way that
completely conquered her heart.) “Now
look here, Julia. I sold out my business this
week, and the money’s in the bank. Are
you willing to leave Parvey and come with
me to some other colony where we can be
happy together?” After some pretence at
hesitation she consented. It was arranged
that they should elope on the following
Saturday. , . ,
“I shall not come empty-handed to you,
Dick, dear,” she said. I shall have my
jewellery at any rate.”
“D —n your jewellery! I want you,
said Dick.
Then Parvey made a discovery. It was
not much of a discovery, but quite enough
to invest him with tragic interest in Ins
own eyes. He walked quickly into the
room, where his wife was, and stood glar-
ing a’t her, without speaking, for a moment
or two. He felt himself to be master of the
situation, and the sensation was so novel
as to be almost enjoyable. His wife knew
at once that he had found her out, and, in
a flash of inspiration, decided upon her plan
of action. . . T ,
“You have been deceiving me, Julia, he
said when his voice came to him ;
“You don’t mean to say so? she replied

1866, and of late years have been a sufferer from Indigestion.
To make a long story short I gave myself up as done with this
world when I happened to take up the “ Daily Record,” in
which I read of the cure for my complaint—Chamberlain’s
Tablets. lat once got a bottle and started on them, and
before I had half finished it I felt as if I could eat and enjoy
my food, which I could not do before. You may wonder how
I lived. I used to live on crushed biscuits until I started taking
that wonderful medicine—Chamberlain’s Tablets.”
MRS. M. A. HAWKE, yEast Street, Rockhampton, Q-
shamefully,” he repeated in a louder tone.
“Parvey, you’re mad, or you’ve been
drinking,” said the offended Lucrece.
“Am I? Have I?” he exclaimed angrily.
And drawing his right hand from behind
his back, in which position he had kept it
till then, he showed her a fragment of a
letter. She took it carelessly, glanced at it,
read “Darling Julia —my own dear love,”
and handed it back with a laugh. Her
levity made him furious. “What man dared
write that to you?” die almost yelled.
“One of my admirers,” she replied, still
laughing. “What about it?”
“ ‘What about it!’ she says,” he cried.
“Why you barefaced, brazen”
His wife put up her hand —she could have
knocked him down with the back of it if she
had liked —and the word he was about to
use died on his tongue. Her time had come
to speak.
“You!” she said slowly and scornfully.
“You to talk to me in this fashion, after
pestering me for years past with stories of
your carryings-on with strange women!”
“But you know there was nothing in
them —it was only harmless fun,” remarked
Parvey, suddenly reduced to acting on the
defensive. It was a horrible wrench to his
vanity to make this admission, but it had to
be done. He might have spared himself, for
all the good it did him. Mrs. Parvey cast
a withering glance upon him. “Only harm-
less fun? You would have me believe, then,
that you were telling me nothing all the
time hut paltry, miserable lies? And you
call yourself a man! Bah!”
The wretched Parvey found himself com-
pelled to choose between the horns of his
own self-constructed dilemma. But first he
tried to take a position between them.
“No, I don’t mean that,” he said. “At
least, not in the way you”
“I should think not,” burst in his wife
with splendidly-simulated rage. “You are
not the kind of man to stop at half-measures
where women are concerned. Parvey, you
are, by your own confession, a profligate—
a wicked rake —and yet you accuse me —-
me —after I have put up with your infi-
delities for years, and said nothing! But”
(taking a step forward and speaking with
slow, sneering emphasis) “I give you fair
warning—as you’ve given me the name, I’ll
have the game.”
Parvey went away a prey to confused
and conflicting feelings. He felt mean, yet,
at the same time curiously proud. The
wretched little animal was actually flattered
by the knowledge that his wife regarded
his innocent over-the-counter flirtations as
serious liaisons.
“She called me a wicked rake,” he
chuckled to himself several times during the
afternoon.
Mrs. Parvey, after he had gone, threw
herself into an easy chair and laughed till
the tears ran down her cheeks.
“It seems almost a shame to deceive such
a simpleton,” she murmured, when her
laughing fit was over.
* * * *
Parvey was, however, not quite as great
a simpleton as his wife took him to be. He
knew, partly by intuition and partly through
certain observations he had made, that she
was preening her wings for flight, and de-
cided to cut those wings as close as he could
when the time came. It was not long in
coming.
When he came home for lunch on the
following Saturday he entered the conjugal
bedroom to look for something, and noticed
signs of packing as for a speedy journey.
“It’s to be to-night, then,” he muttered.
“Very well!” He opened the drawer in
which Mrs. Parvey kept her jewellery, and
found it (the jewellery) done up in a neat
parcel. He slipped the parcel in his pocket,
went in to his lunch, ate it, and returned to
the shop without a word.
Bovis —who had been watching him from
the window of an hotel across the street —
rushed over to make Mrs. Parvey hurry up
with her arrangements. That conscien-
tious matron, however, informed him that
she couldn’t think of clearing out until she
had cooked the dinner for the following day.
“I may be going to act badly by Parvey,”
she said ; “but I’m not going to be so bad
as to leave the poor man without his Sun-
day’s dinner.” And she was thoroughly
serious. Bovis fumed to no purpose. “Men
don’t understand how a woman feels about
these things,” she observed.
However, the dinner was cooked at last,
rrand then, with a clear conscience, Mrs. Par-
vey dressed herself for her journey towards
the Unknown. When she was nearly ready
„to start she went to her drawer to get her
jewellery. The drawer was, of course, empty.
She realised in an instant what had hap-
pened.
“The mean scoundrel!” she exclaimed,
grinding her strong white teeth.
A few minutes afterwards she was in her
husband’s shop. A number of women were
standing at the counter waiting to be, or
being, served. Parvey was, as usual, ex-
changing winks and jests with them. Yet
he must know that she was about to leave
him! The callous brute! She tugged at
her wedding ring until it came off her finger.
Then, stepping forward swiftly, she cried,
“As you’ve taken so much, you can take
this.” And she flung the ring in his face.
It retjounded and fell on the floor. Parvey
picked it up quietly, placed it in his vest
pocket, and continued serving his customers.
That night when he went home he found
the house empty—as he expected it would
be.
“Julia’s gone, right enough,” he solilo-
quised dismally. Then, after a minute or
two spent in reflection, he said, almost cheer-
fully, “Well —there are others.”
This was Parvey’s first start as a philo-
sopher. And the irony of it was that it was
suggested by his wife affecting to regard him
as a Don Juan. Her flight had another
effect. It caused him to sell out of his busi-
ness. He felt that he was no longer in a
position to indulge in gay badinage with his
lady customers. He knew that they would
pretend to sympathise with him, and that
would be more than he could stand. So he
left the suburb, and, a week afterwards, was
once more making conquests of the sex
from behind the counter of a branch estab-
lishment of the City and Suburban Supply
Co.
• • • •
Bovis and his Julia went to Sydney.
When she told him the story of the im-
pounded jewellery he laughed boisterously,
and said that Parvey was a smarter man
than he had taken him to be.
They went everywhere and saw every-
thing, and, for a time, Julia enjoyed herself
to the full capacity of her sensuous nature.
Yet there was a fly in her ointment. Bovis
was willing enough to spend money upon
her, but he would give her none to spend on
her own account. She taxed him with this
on one occasion, and he replied with a coarse
laugh, “You don’t suppose I’m such a fool
as to give you the chance of clearin’ out with
some other fellow, do you? Not with my
money, you don’t.” Then he laughed again
and kissed her, but she was not pleased with
him.
Bovis, though a fine animal, had the de-
fects of his good points. When drunk (which
was his condition now and then) he was in-
clined to be brutal. Once he gave her a
black eye—a dangerous experiment to try
upon a woman with such a cranial conforma-
tion.
One day, after they had been in Sydney
about six months, the innate vulgarity of his
nature broke out in a hideously disillusionis-
ing fashion. He had been to the races, and
returned sour-mnd with bad liquor and bad
luck. It was a luxury to have Julia to spit
his spite upon.
“I’m d—d near dead broke,” he snarled,
throwing himself into a chair; “and you—
you (unprintable epithet) are the cause of
it.”
Recriminations followed fast and furious,
and Julia received her second black eye.
Next morning Bovis was repentant, and
apologetic. Julia answered with words of
forgiveness ; though her swollen face and the
thin straight line of her lips, which at other
times looked like a rosy double-bow, might
have made him doubt the genuineness of her
speech. But Bovis did not understand
women. _ If he could have seen what was
passing in Julia’s brain just then, he would
never more have gone to sleep under the
same roof with her, in the sure hope that he
would awake with a sound throat in the
morning.
He went out again that day, and returned
in an amiable humor.
“Got a letter from my brother,” he ob-
served.
“Yes,” said Julia, not raising her eyes
from some garment she was making.
“Yes, and he tells me there’s a good billet
open for me in Bendigo. So pack up and
we’ll go to Melbourne by next boat.”
They went by the next boat.
Bovis left Julia with her mother —-an old
lady who was singularly free from the pre-
judices of her sex and aae. “I shall only be
six months up in Bendigo.” he said, “and
then I’ll come back and make another start
in Melbourne. You take this” —handing
Julia £3O in bank-notes —“and furnish a cot-
tage. I dessav I shall be able to run down
two or three times and see how you’re gettin’
on.”
Julia, with a strange smile on her lips,
took thp money and placed it in the bosom
of her dress.
Some mornings afterwards, while dressing
in her bedroom, she heard a familiar voice.
“It’s Purvey.” she murmured. Should she go
out and meet him and have the trouble over?
She was a bold woman, but she bad tact
enough to know that her best policy, in the
circumstances, was not audacity.
She opened her door quickly, and then
started back, as if in amazement at seeing
Parvey in the room. But he had come pre-
pared for the meeting.
“It’s all right, Julia,” he said pleasantly,
“you needn’t run away. I'm willing to let
bygones be bygones if you are.”
He looked so clean, so sober, so nice and
considerate, and, in a word, so un-Bovis-like,
that Julia burst into tears —real, unsimu-
lated tears.
“Don’t cry!” he exclaimed, adding, by way
of philosophic consolation, “It’s no good cry-
ing over spilt milk.”
This made her laugh—hysterically. Then
she cried again.
“The past need make no difference,” said
Parvey. the Philosopher. “See. here is your
ring,” he continued, slipping it back to its
old place on her finger. “I keot it for you.
I knew you’d come back some time or other.”
It would be nrofanity to pry upon the
affpeting reconciliation that followed.
Parvey, before leaving, told his wife that
as soon as she was ready he would take her
to Albury, where there was a grocery busi-
ness in which he had a share, with the option
of buying out the whole concern at a cheap
price. He thought it would be a good in-
vestment.
“Here’s £3O towards the purchase, dear,”
said Julia, taking Bovis’s money from a
drawer arid handing it to her husband, who
took it without a blush.
“I reckon he owes me that much, anyhow,”
was all the comment he made.
* * * *
On the morning following, Julia received
a letter from Bovis, stating that he was
coming to Melbourne that day on business,
and asking her to meet him at the station in
the afternoon when the Bendigo train
arrived. She sent for her husband and
showed him the letter. “We’d better go to
Albury by the express this evening: I don’t
want to have any trouble,” said the pacific
Parvey.
They were at the station at 4.30 p.m. to
make sure of not missing the train.
“I’ll _go in advance and prospect—Bovis
may still be there waiting for you,” remarked
Parvey.
Bovis was . there—walking up and down
the platform in what appeared to be a state
of extreme irritation.
Parvey told his wife what he had observed.
He mustn’t see us. Surely he won’t stay
much longer?” she exclaimed, nervously
Parvey was also nervous. Their flight to-
gether began to appear like an illicit clandes-
tine elopement. Meanwhile, Parvey took two
first-class tickets for Albury.
Just as the express came in Bovis went
into the refreshment-room.
Parvey and his wife seized the opportunity
to dive into a carriage, into the furthest cor-
ner of which they cowered, quaking guiltily.
Bovis reappeared on the platform, arid
strolled along, glancing carelessly into the
carriages, as if to kill time. But, although
he peered into the very compartment in
which they were sitting, he did not recognise
them.
As the express steamed out of the station
the escaping pair looked into each other’s
eyes and breathed a long sigh of relief.
Bovis stood gazing listlessly after the
disappearing train, and then muttering, “It’s
no use waiting any longer—she must' have
had some other business on hand,” he turned
on his heel and left the platform.
CREVE ROE.
